** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SUSANA MIREYA MORALES-BRIBIESCA
File: AXXX XX0 293 - Cleveland, OH
OctOber 18, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jessica Rodriguez Bell, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Bruce D. Imbacuan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals an Immigration Judge’s September 3, 2008, decision granting the Department of Homeland Security’s (DHS) motion to pretermit the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal and denying the respondent’s request to withdraw her pleadings to the Notice to Appear. We review the Immigration Judge’s factual findings for clear error and legal conclusions de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). The respondent’s fee waiver request is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). The case will be remanded.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred by denying the respondent’s request to withdraw her pleadings. See Respondent’s Brief at 4-5. According to the respondent, the attorney who represented her before the Immigration Judge rendered ineffective assistance by filing an April 27, 2007, motion to change venue to the Cleveland Immigration Court in which the attorney admitted the factual allegations in the Notice to Appear and conceded that the respondent was removable for assisting an alien to enter the United States illegally. See section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Act. According to the respondent, her prior attorney conceded removability without first consulting with the respondent. See Respondent’s Brief at 6; Motion to Change Venue. Moreover, the concession of removability left the respondent with no apparent means to avoid losing her permanent resident status, in that she was ineligible for cancellation of removal because, at the time of the service of the Notice to Appear, the respondent had not accrued 7 years of continuous residence in the United States. When the attorney realized that the respondent was not eligible for cancellation of removal, she attempted to withdraw the pleadings before the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 13).¿FN1º
 
Motions to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel are governed by our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). The respondent has not submitted an affidavit setting forth her agreement with former counsel, or filed a disciplinary complaint against former counsel. Nevertheless, we find substantial compliance with Lozada based on an affidavit from respondent’s former attorney.
 
*2 In this affidavit provided on appeal, the respondent’s prior attorney admitted that she conceded the respondent’s removability without first speaking to the respondent or discussing the factual allegations with the respondent. See Exh. 5 at 1. Further, given the egregiousness of the representation, we do not deem the attorney’s admission binding on the respondent. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986).
 
In sum, given the circumstances of this case, we will grant the respondent’s motion to reopen for ineffective assistance of counsel and remand this case for a hearing on the issue of the respondent’s removability and eligibility for relief from removal. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

At the hearing before the Immigration Judge, the respondent, through counsel, argued that “since we found out that we’re not eligible for cancellation of removal we’d like to now amend the pleadings and require that the state or the Government prove that the respondent is removable” (Tr. at 13).

2010 WL 4213224 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OLGA MERCEDES SOLANO-VARGAS
File: AXXX XX7 390 - Seattle, WA
October 12, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Diana E. Moller, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Marci Ellsworth
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen her removal proceedings. In our decision dated November 18, 2009, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision which found her removable, granted her motion to suppress, but denied her motion to terminate. The present motion will be granted.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen (filed on February 19, 2010) is timely.¿FN1º The respondent alleges ineffective assistance of former counsel (Rosaura Del Carmen Rodriguez). In Matter of Compean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), the Attorney General directed the Board to continue to apply the previously established standards for reviewing motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel (pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
The respondent shows substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to allege ineffective assistance of former counsel. The respondent presents her declaration and Ms. Rodriguez’s declaration. The respondent has not filed a complaint with the appropriate bar authorities. The respondent explains that it would not be fair because of the pressure the Immigration Judge allegedly put on Ms. Rodriguez to plead to the allegations in the Notice to Appear (Form I-862) at the August 28, 2006, master calendar hearing (Motion at 12-13). Although we do not consider the respondent’s explanation to be satisfactory, we decline, under the circumstances present in this case, to find this to be fatal to the respondent’s claim.
 
The respondent shows prejudice. See Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893,898 (9th Cir. 2008) (prejudice is established when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings). The respondent hired Ms. Rodriguez to represent her (Motions Exhs. 1, 2). Ms. Rodriguez represented the respondent at her August 28, 2006, master calendar hearing and admitted the allegations in the Notice to Appear and conceded the charge of removability (Tr. at 3). The Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s motion to suppress but denied her motion to terminate. The only reason the Immigration Judge did not grant the respondent’s motion to terminate was because of Ms. Rodriguez’s admission of the allegations in the Notice to Appear and concession of removability (I.J. at 4-5; BIA at 2). We conclude that the respondent shows prejudice. Cf. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 383 (BIA 1986) (finding that the admissions and the concession of deportability were reasonable tactical actions undertaken by former counsel in order to enhance the alien’s chances of securing favorable action on the motion for change of venue and were properly held to be binding upon the alien).
 
*2 In summary, we grant the respondent’s motion to reopen because she shows substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, and shows prejudice. Because the respondent’s motion to reopen is timely filed, due diligence is not an issue.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and the proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Board has decided that a “grace period” applies to filings due from February 5, 2010, to February 18, 2010, and the filing was received on or before February 19, 2010 (Motion Exh. 9).

2010 WL 4213253 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARGARITO CONTRERAS A.K.A. MARGARITO CONTERA FLORES NORMA CONTRERAS A.K.A. NORMA PEREZ MERITO
File: AXXX XX4 668 - Newark, NJ
AXXX XX4 669
October 8, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Elizabeth C. Surin, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
The respondents appeal from the Immigration Judge’s September 9, 2008, decision. The Immigration Judge had previously, on April 21, 2008, granted the respondents pre-conclusion voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), the respondents having waived their right of appeal. The respondents thereafter, on August 18, 2008, filed a motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge denied the respondents’ untimely filed motion to reopen, concluding that the claim did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel during the course of the removal proceedings so as to support reopening. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondents are husband and wife. The respondents’ current counsel, in the motion filed with the Immigration Judge, requested reopening based upon ineffective assistance of counsel. It was noted in the motion that the previous counsel, after failing to timely file an appeal from a previous denial by the Department of Homeland Security (the DHS) of an employment-based visa petition (I-140), not only failed to advise the lead respondent that he could file a new Application for Employment Certification, but then further prejudiced the interests of the respondents by requesting voluntary departure. In response to the motion, the prior counsel stated that, early in December 2007, after the I-140 was denied, the employer pulled out, and no appeal was filed with DHS as, with no employer support, there was no ability to challenge the denial on appeal. The prior counsel noted that, as the respondents had no eligibility for relief, they were advised that voluntary departure was the only sensible option.
 
The Immigration Judge, after noting on several occasions that the first attorney representing the respondents did not seem entirely familiar with immigration law, concluded that the respondents did not receive ineffective assistance from their former attorney, finding that the interests of the respondents were not prejudiced during the course of the removal proceedings. The Immigration Judge found that the failure by the former attorney to request a continuance does not represent a failure to request a form of relief. The Immigration Judge further noted that she had indicated on the record that she was not willing to a grant a continuance unless there was evidence that a timely appeal of the I-140 denial had been filed, and, as there was no such evidence, this explains why no such motion was filed by the previous attorney. The Immigration Judge further found that nothing done in the Immigration Court prevents the respondents from filing for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i), even if they are not in the United States. The Immigration Judge, noting that she has no jurisdiction over an Application for Employment Certification, found that there is no evidence that, without an approved visa petition, the respondents were prima facie eligible for any relief other than voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge, after denying the motion to reopen, vacated the grant of pre-conclusion voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Act.
 
*2 In the motion to reopen the respondents claim that previous counsel rendered ineffective assistance. Pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to pursue such a claim, the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness; and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, to advance a successful claim for ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate a due process violation, that is, prejudice, i.e., he “must show that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case.” Uspango v. Ashcroft, 289 F.3d 226, 231 (3d Cir. 2002); see also Xu Yong Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 131 (3d Cir.2001). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998). The respondents have made no such showing. The respondents are clearly dissatisfied with former counsel’s representation because he failed in efforts to secure the approval of an employment based visa petition in proceedings before the DHS, but they have not shown that the hearing before the Immigration Judge was unfair or that, due to any alleged ineffective assistance of counsel during the course of the removal proceedings, they were prevented from reasonably presenting their case. See Balam-Chuc v. Mukasey, 547 F.3d 1044, 1050-51 (9th Cir. 2008).
 
The respondents’ principal allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel simply do not include any assertions of ineffective assistance of counsel during the course of these administrative proceedings before the Immigration Judge. Rather, the respondents’ arguments of ineffective assistance of counsel center on the lead respondent’s pursuit of his application for adjustment of status by way of an employment-based visa petition, difficulties over which we have no jurisdiction. The alleged ineffective assistance involved counsel who represented the respondents before the DHS, counsel who had been retained several years before these administrative proceeding were initiated by the issuance of the Notice to Appear in January 2008. It was only after the lead respondent’s application for adjustment of status by way of an employment-based visa petition was denied by the DHS, and no timely appeal was filed, that the respondents were left with limited options when they came before the Immigration Judge, albeit with the same counsel. If the respondents’ believe that, in pursuing a visa petition before the DHS, they are the victims of ineffective assistance of counsel, they must pursue this claim in a court of competent jurisdiction.
 
*3 The Immigration Judge weighed the evidence presented by the respondents and concluded that the factual assertions contained in the respondents’ motion were insufficient to reflect ineffective assistance on the part of former counsel during the course of the removal proceedings. The Immigration Judge’s factual determinations in this regard were not clearly erroneous within the meaning of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). Anderson v. City of Bessemer City, North Carolina, 470 U.S. 564, 573-4 (1985) (holding that where there are two permissible views of the evidence, the factfinder’s choice between them cannot be deemed “clearly erroneous”). Furthermore, in light of the Immigration Judge’s factual determinations we find no reversible error in her legal conclusion that the respondent did not receive ineffective assistance from his former attorney during the course of the removal proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
Thus, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The respondents’ appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4213253 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 4213304 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: UPSORN SRESTAPUNTE
File: AXXX XX9 431 - Los Angeles, CA
October 7, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Claire H. Kim, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia C. Contreras
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on December 21, 2004, when the Board affirmed, without opinion, an Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen her removal proceedings that were conducted in absentia on May 22, 2001. On August 4, 2010, the respondent, a native and citizen of Thailand, filed a motion requesting reopening. The motion will be denied.¿FN1º
 
With certain exceptions not pertinent here, the respondent is limited to one motion to reopen and that motion must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent’s motion to reopen is therefore barred by the time limitations applicable to motions.
 
However, even if considered timely, the respondent, who altogether failed to raise both her alleged lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel claims before both the Immigration Judge and the Board on appeal, has not sufficiently shown that such claims could not have been discovered or presented during the former proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Significantly, the respondent consistently and only asserted, before both the Immigration Judge and the Board, that she failed to attend her scheduled hearing because her “son became ill” (Resp. Brief on Appeal filed July 19, 2004; Resp. Brief dated March 30, 2004; Resp. Brief dated June 22, 2001). Further, the Immigration Judge noted that, even if the respondent’s motion was timely filed, no explanation for the delay was offered (I.J. at 1-2).
 
Under the circumstances, we do not find the respondent’s assertions, the alleged non receipt of the Notice to Appear and the ineffective assistance of her former counsel in connection with the untimely filings of both her motions before the Immigration Judge and the Board, alone sufficient to demonstrate patent error or prejudice. See, e.g., Joshi v. Ashcroft, 389 F.3d 732, 735 (7th Cir. 2004) (stating that “a bare, uncorroborated, self-serving, denial of receipt, even if sworn, is weak evidence”); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 58 (BIA 2006) (stating that a motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied). Nor has the respondent sufficiently complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
*2 Finally, we find no other reason for this Board’s further consideration of the respondent’s claim on our own motion pursuant to this Board’s sua sponte authority under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). On this record, the respondent has provided us with no reason to overlook her untimely motion. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has been removed from the United States to Thailand. In Coyt v. Holder, 593 F.3d 902 (9th Cir. 2010), the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals found that the Board may not find a motion withdrawn by operation of law, when the respondent is removed prior to the Board’s ruling on the motion. The motion is not, therefore, barred by reason of the respondent’s removal.

2010 WL 4213370 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ERICK ALEXANDER ESCALANTE-REYES
File: AXXX XX1 737 - Oakdale, LA
October 4, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rhonda McCauley, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 On August 26, 2010, the respondent filed a “motion to reopen/reconsider sua sponte” the Board’s May 7, 2010, decision dismissing his appeal. He also seeks a stay of removal. The motion and stay request are denied.
 
Whether treated as a motion to reopen or one seeking reconsideration, the respondent’s motion is untimely. Sections 240(c)(6), (7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6), (7); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(b), (c)(2). Although the respondent avers that two unidentified attorneys provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not complied with any of the requirements for reopening on this basis. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent’s reference to Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010) is unavailing as he has provided no evidence that he has filed any collateral attack against his criminal conviction, nor shown that the same has been successful. To the extent that he avers error in his underlying criminal conviction, the Board cannot go behind a record of conviction to assess its validity. Matter of Roberts, 20 I&N Dec. 294, 301 (BIA 1991). Moreover, while he now seeks to pursue an application for asylum and related relief, he has not shown that such a claim could not have been presented at the time of the proceedings below. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
We recognize and appreciate the many letters written by friends and family in support of the respondent’s motion. However, the facts presented do not represent exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of our discretionary authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings.¿FN1º Therefore, the respondent’s request for a stay of removal is moot. The motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Should the respondent satisfy the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance against former counsel and/or show that his conviction has been vacated pursuant to Padilla or another basis that supports reopening, he may seek sua sponte reopening before this Board. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).

2010 WL 4035390 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAYMOND ALLAN DENK
File: AXXX XX0 881 - Arlington, VA
September 30, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Aroon Roy Padharia, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The motion is denied.¿FN1º The respondent, a native and citizen of Canada, has timely filed a motion requesting reconsideration of the Board’s June 11, 2010, decision dismissing the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent removable as an aggravated felon, as charged, and ineligible for adjustment of status. To the extent the respondent’s motion rests on previously raised issues related to (a) his removability as an aggravated felon based on the validity of his convictions, (b) whether his conviction for the offense of malicious wounding in violation of Virginia Code § 18.2-51, constitutes a crime involving moral turpitude thereby rendering him inadmissible and thus ineligible for adjustment of status, and (c) his eligibility for a section 212(h) waiver of inadmissibility, the respondent has not identified any issue that was not considered in the Board’s previous detailed decision, nor has he identified any error of fact or law in that decision which would alter the outcome. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing the requirements for motions to reconsider). Similarly, the respondent has not identified any error in the Board’s decision to review the respondent’s appeal without a brief filed by the respondent’s attorney. As the Board previously concluded, the arguments contained in the Notice of Appeal were sufficient to enable meaningful appellate review (Board order dated June 11, 2010, at 2). To the extent the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel in connection with his counsel’s failure to file a brief on appeal, the respondent has not sufficiently shown patent error or complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.¿FN2º
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s appellate fee waiver request is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3).

2

A request for deferred action or humanitarian parole arising from the respondent’s alleged medical condition would have to be addressed to the Department of Homeland Security. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348 (BIA 1982).

2010 WL 4035398 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR MANUEL ROSARIO
File: AXXX XX8 483 - Orlando, FL
September 30, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Peter N. Macaluso, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(G)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(G)(ii)º - Marriage fraud - failed or refused to fulfill marital agreement
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Removability
 
On August 26, 2008, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged. The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, now appeals. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded.
 
On appeal, the respondent challenges the Immigration Judge’s finding that he conceded the marriage fraud charge set forth in the Notice to Appear (Respondent’s Br. at 4-6). See section 237(a)(1)(G)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(G)(ii). He also asserts that his due process rights were violated because the Immigration Judge did not require the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) to prove that charge¿FN1º (Respondent’s Br. at 4, 6). We review the factual findings in this case for clear error, and matters of law, judgment, and discretion under the de novo standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii) (2010).
 
The procedural history of this case raises significant concerns, particularly with respect to the Immigration Judge’s decision to sustain the marriage fraud charge. First, the Notice to Appear charges marriage fraud on the basis of entering into a marriage with a United States citizen for the purpose of procuring admission as an immigrant (Exh. 1). However, the notice only identifies a non-immigrant B2 visa admission, on October 6, 1998, as the respondent’s sole admission. The record indicates that the respondent married on August 26, 2006, but the notice does not indicate that the respondent adjusted his immigration status subsequent to his 1998 admission (Record of Sworn Statement of Leola Bernice Clark, dated August 7, 2007; Exh. 1).
 
Second, while the record contains a sworn statement of the United States citizen seeking to withdraw an I-130 visa petition because her marriage to the respondent was “fake,” the record does not contain an adjustment application or other document to indicate that the respondent sought an immigration benefit (Record of Sworn Statement of Leola Bernice Clark, dated August 7, 2007, at 1). Perhaps more importantly, section 237(a)(1)(G) of the Act pertains to aliens who “hav¿eº procured a visa or other documentation by fraud.” At most, the current record may indicate that the respondent sought to procure a visa or other documentation by fraud, but that does not suffice to sustain a charge under section 237(a)(1)(G).
 
*2 Third, after the respondent’s counsel conceded the charges set forth in the Notice to Appear, the respondent testified that he married his United States citizen wife because he loved her (Respondent’s Br. at 2, 4, 5; Tr. at 21, 27-28). The Immigration Judge told counsel that she should not have conceded the marriage fraud charge if the respondent planned to contest it (Tr. at 21, 29-30). Although the Immigration Judge gave the respondent and counsel an opportunity to discuss the matter, the Immigration Judge only asked for confirmation that the respondent had already conceded the charges, and not whether, in light of his testimony, he still wished to concede removability based on marriage fraud (Respondent’s Br. at 3, 5; Tr. at 32). The record does not indicate that the respondent recanted his testimony (Respondent’s Br. at 3).
 
Thus, we conclude that the record does not sufficiently support a finding that the respondent was removable on the grounds of marriage fraud. The Immigration Judge erred in sustaining the charge under section 237(a)(1)(G)(ii) on the basis of the record as it now exists. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii), 1240.10(c).
 
The respondent also asserts that he did not authorize his counsel to concede the marriage fraud charge, especially in light of his testimony that he married for love (Respondent’s Br. at 3). This assertion essentially raises a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Nevertheless, the potential ineffectiveness, combined with the above-described procedural deficiencies and the questionable nature of the marriage fraud count, lead us to conclude that a remand is warranted. Our remand does not preclude the DHS from endeavoring to prove the marriage fraud charge through the introduction of additional evidence into the record.
 
We also note that counsel withdrew the respondent’s request for voluntary departure when the Immigration Judge suggested that she would not grant it because the DHS opposed voluntary departure on the basis of the marriage fraud charge (Tr. at 30, 33). On remand, the respondent shall be given an opportunity to seek any relief for which he currently may be eligible, including voluntary departure.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and the record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Board Member Roger A. Pauley respectfully dissents and would not find this a patent case of ineffective assistance of counsel so as to excuse the failure to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Linda S. Wendtland
*3 FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent does not contest his removability under section 237(a)(1)(B) of the Act, as set forth in the other charge in the Notice to Appear.


2010 WL 4035398 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 4035397 (BIA)

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YVROSE BOUBERT
File: AXXX XX4 477 - Miami, FL
September 30, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank Murray, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jorge L. Pereira
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen her removal proceedings. In our decision dated April 8, 2010, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision which found her removable and determined that she was statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal for certain permanent residents. The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen (filed on May 25, 2010) is timely. The respondent moves to reopen proceedings to apply for relief under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (1995). The respondent alleges ineffective assistance of former counsel (Bryant Filomeno). In Matter of Compean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), the Attorney General directed the Board to continue to apply the previously established standards for reviewing motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel (pending the outcome of a rulemaking process). The respondent meets the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to allege ineffective assistance of former counsel. The respondent presents her affidavit, counsel’s letter to Mr. Filomeno, and a copy of the complaint filed against Mr. Filomeno.
 
The respondent shows prejudice. See generally Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 563 (BIA 2003) (finding that the alien had failed to show the requisite prejudice to warrant consideration of his late appeal). Mr. Filomeno filed an application for cancellation of removal for certain permanent residents on the respondent’s behalf, even though she was ineligible for such relief. See our April 8, 2010, decision. Mr. Filomeno did not file an application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act on the respondent’s behalf, even though she appeared to be prima facie eligible to apply for such relief.
 
The respondent became a lawful permanent resident on September 29, 1989 (Exh. 1), so she had 7 years of lawful domicile in the United States as of September 29, 1996. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1212.3(f)(1) and (2) (2004) (section 212(c) applications shall be denied if the alien has not been lawfully admitted for permanent residence or the alien has not maintained lawful domicile in the United States for 7 years). The respondent pled nolo contendere to two counts of aggravated child abuse on July 17, 1995 (Exh. 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(h)(1) (even if an alien is in removal proceedings, an eligible alien may apply for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act, without regard to the AEDPA, with respect to a conviction if the alien pleaded guilty or nolo contendere and the alien’s plea agreement was made before April 24, 1996). The respondent was thus eligible to apply for section 212(c) relief. We will therefore grant the respondent’s motion to reopen and remand for further proceedings. Our remand order is limited to allowing the respondent to apply for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and the proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.¿FN1º
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

A request for humanitarian parole or deferred action arising from the recent devastating earthquake in Haiti and its aftermath are matters beyond the jurisdiction of the Board and the Immigration Judges. See Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734 (BIA 1988); Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348 (BIA 1982). However, a statement released by the Department of Homeland Security on January 13, 2010, reflects that “all removals to Haiti ¿have been haltedº for the time being in response to the devastation caused by ¿theº earthquake.” Further, the Secretary of Homeland Security has announced that Haitians who have continuously resided in the United States since January 12, 2010, and have been continuously physically present since January 21, 2010, may be granted Temporary Protected Status (“TPS”). See 75 Fed. Reg. 39,957-58 (July 13, 2010). Jurisdiction over applications for TPS by aliens under final orders of removal is with the U. S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) and TPS applications must be filed with the USCIS. See 8 C.F.R. § 1244.7. Please contact the Department of Homeland Security for any additional questions you may have regarding how to apply for TPS (1-800-375-5283 or www.uscis.gov).

2010 WL 4035457 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANDRA MARIBEL SEGOVIA-SUPLIGUICHA
File: AXXX XX5 116 - New York, NY
September 23, 2010
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Albert Cohen, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robert Bergida
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider the Board’s January 8, 2010, decision dismissing her appeal of an Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered deported on August 23, 1995. The respondent also seeks a stay of deportation. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion alleges that the Board erred as it affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision and “did not engage in substantial review” of that decision (Motion at 3). She asserts that the Board erroneously did not address the likelihood of success regarding her application for adjustment of status, and should have granted her motion as it was predicated on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, as both the Immigration Judge and this Board correctly noted, the respondent’s motion did not comply with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent does not contest this finding, which was fatal to her motion to reopen. As such, we find no error in the Board’s prior decision, and thus, reconsideration is not warranted. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b).
 
To the extent that the respondent seeks to introduce new evidence reflecting recent efforts to comply with the Lozada requirements, this aspect of her motion is one seeking reopening and rescission, and is untimely and number barred. Section 242B(c)(3) of the Act. However, if the respondent had demonstrated substantial compliance with Lozada, and shown due diligence in pursuing the claim, the time limit could arguably be equitably tolled. While we recognize her efforts in this regard, the respondent has not substantially complied with these requirements. Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2d Cir. 2005) (stating that an “alien who has failed to comply substantially with the Lozada requirements in her motion to reopen before the ¿Boardº forfeits her ineffective assistance of counsel claim in this Court”).
 
In her current, third untimely motion to reopen, the respondent now claims that she hired “Mr. Bosch” at “Function Inc.” in New York to represent her in “immigration proceedings” (Tab 3). The respondent asserts that she hired Mr. Bosch on the same day that she married her first husband, and he later helped her husband filed a visa petition on her behalf in September of that year (Tabs 3, 5). She avers that Mr. Bosch advised her to not attend her immigration hearing as he would “take care of this matter himself” (Tab 3). The respondent alleges that she at some unspecified time “later” learned she had been ordered deported in absentia. She also asserts she had contacted Mr. Bosch several times “during that period of time” and he claimed that her adjustment application was “in process.” The respondent alleges that when she “later” contacted Function Inc. she was told that Mr. Bosch had “disappeared.” She further asserts that she is uncertain whether he was an attorney.
 
*2 The respondent has not provided any evidence indicating that she has informed Mr. Bosch of the allegations against him, or filed a bar complaint with the appropriate authorities. While she claims that Functions Inc. stated that he had disappeared, she submitted nothing from this agency supporting this claim. The complaint filed with the “consumer complaint agency” does not satisfy the bar complaint requirement in Lozada as there is no evidence that it has any authority over members of the bar or persons who otherwise purport to provide immigration advice (Tab 4).¿FN1º Given her statement that she is uncertain whether Mr. Bosch is an attorney, no explanation has been offered as to whether she consulted the state bar authorities to ascertain his status, and why a complaint was not filed with bar authorities. The claims in her affidavit regarding the representational agreement she had with Mr. Bosch and her efforts to keep in touch with him are notable for their vagueness and generality.
 
As the Immigration Judge noted in denying her prior motion, the respondent received personal service of the charging document, which informed her of the time, date and location of her hearing, and of the consequences should she fail to appear (Exh. 1). She has not explained why she nonetheless took Mr. Bosch’s alleged advice to not appear despite those warnings. Given these various deficiencies, we find that the respondent has not substantially complied with the Lozada requirements, and her motion is not exempt from the applicable time limitation. See, e.g., Omar v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 647, 651 (2d Cir. 2008) (finding that the Board’s decision to deny the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim against a non-attorney immigration consultant based on non-compliance with Lozada was “neither arbitrary nor capricious”).
 
In addition, the respondent has not shown that she exercised the requisite due diligence in pursuing her claim. Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006) (“no matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled”); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134 (2d Cir. 2000). This is further support for denial of reopening.
 
Finally, given this record, we do not find that the respondent has presented exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of our discretionary authority to sua sponte grant this untimely, number-barred motion to reopen. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
*3 As we find no basis for granting reconsideration of our January 8, 2010, decision, or reopening of proceedings, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

1

The nature of her complaint to the agency is also unclear as her report in the “description of complaint” section is cut off and unreadable.

2010 WL 4035459 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PATRICK LEVILAIN
File: AXXX XX0 652 - Portland, OR
September 23, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sarah C. Schreck
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Termination; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Canada, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s November 5, 2009, decision finding him removable as charged and ordering him removed to Canada. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed an opposition brief. The appeal will be dismissed. While this matter was on appeal, the respondent filed what the Board construes to be a motion to remand. This motion will be denied.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of H-L-H- & Z-Y-Z-, 25 I&N Dec. 209, 211 (BIA 2010).
 
The respondent, a citizen and native of Canada, was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident in 1967 (Exh. 1; I.J at 1). On February 14, 2007, in the Circuit Court of Marion County, State of Oregon, he pled guilty to the offense of burglary in the first degree, in violation of section 164.225 of the Oregon Revised Statutes, and he was sentenced to 19 months confinement (Exh. 2, Tab B at 1; I.J. at 1). In his decision, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent was convicted of a theft or burglary offense for which a term of imprisonment of at least 1 year was imposed, thus constituting an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G).
 
The respondent argues that his criminal conviction was invalid because he received ineffective assistance from a prior criminal counsel, who allegedly failed to inform him of the immigration consequences of his guilty plea (Respondent’s Notice of Appeal at 4). Although the respondent is apparently appealing a Circuit Court denial of post-conviction relief to the Supreme Court of the State of Oregon, to date the record does not reflect that the conviction has actually been overturned. Thus, his pending collateral attack on his conviction does not affect its finality for federal immigration purposes, or warrant a continuance. Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
In a letter associated with the record in April 2010, the respondent’s former immigration counsel appeared to reference Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. ___, 130 S.Ct. 1473, 2010 WL 1222274 (March 31, 2010), in which the United States Supreme Court ruled that a lawyer representing an alien in connection with a guilty plea to a criminal offense has a constitutional duty to advise the alien about the risk of deportation arising from the conviction. However, any suggestion that the respondent’s conviction might be invalid in light of Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, does not change its finality for immigration purposes of such conviction, unless and until it is in fact overturned or materially amended. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996); Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent alleged that he was ill served by the failure of his prior immigration counsel to seek cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, and he requested a remand so that the Immigration Judge could consider this application in the first instance (Respondent’s Br. at 1-2, 9). We construe the respondent’s submissions in this regard as a motion to remand and reopen his proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4).
 
However, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. That is, he has not submitted an affidavit detailing the agreement he had with his prior counsel, asserted that the prior counsel had been informed of any allegations leveled against him and been given an opportunity to respond, or confirmed that disciplinary complaints had been filed against the prior counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). His actions to date could not even be construed as substantial compliance. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 825-26 (9th Cir. 2003) (recognizing substantial compliance with Lozada procedural requirements where legitimacy of the claim is plain on the face of the administrative record).
 
Finally, a motion to reopen will not be granted unless it establishes a prima facie case of statutory and discretionary eligibility for the underlying substantive relief sought. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Since the respondent’s aggravated felony conviction thus far remains intact, he has not shown that he is statutorily eligible for cancellation of removal. See section 240A(a)(3) of the Act. Therefore, he has not established that he was prejudiced by the apparent failure of his prior immigration counsel to file an application for cancellation of removal. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003); Ahmed v. Mukasey, 548 F.3d 768, 772 (9th Cir. 2008).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4035459 (BIA)
End of Document

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NEREIDA RAMIREZ VALDEZ A.K.A. NEREIDA VALDEZ RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX5 514 - Houston, TX
September 23, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven T. Villarreal, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margaret R. Kugel
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on April 21, 2010, when we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s August 19, 2008, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. On May 28, 2010, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen with the Board based on the alleged ineffectiveness of her prior counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent’s timely motion to reopen now before us exceeds the numerical limitations for motions to reopen and will be denied. Under the law, with certain exceptions not pertinent here, a party is limited to one motion to reopen. Section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). However, as set out above, this is the respondent’s second motion to reopen as she previously filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (stating that a party may file only one motion to reopen “whether before the Board or the Immigration Judge ….”) (emphasis added). Consequently, the respondent’s motion to reopen is numerically barred.
 
We also reject the respondent’s contention that her motion to reopen should be granted based on ineffective assistance of counsel. Although the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has not squarely decided whether the number limit on motions to reopen can be equitably surmounted based on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, it has found that the Board does not abuse its discretion in finding that an ineffective assistance of counsel claim is insufficient where it fails to comply with requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000). As observed by DHS in this case, the affidavit submitted by the respondent does not set out the agreement and representations made by prior counsel and does not adequately explain why the respondent did not file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority (see Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Exhibit 3).
 
In addition, the respondent has not shown, as required, that she suffered “substantial prejudice” as a result of the ineffective representation. See Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th 1994). Proving prejudice requires a prima facie showing that had the cancellation application been filed, she would have been entitled to relief. See id. However, as pointed out by the Immigration Judge in denying the respondent’s first motion to reopen, the respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for cancellation of removal because she has not submitted evidence indicating that a qualifying relative would suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship upon her removal to Mexico. Consequently, even assuming arguendo the availability of equitable relief for a number-barred motion to reopen, we do not find that it is warranted. Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion for a stay is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4035453 (BIA)

2010 WL 4035455 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOSES MACHARIA GICHUHI
File: AXXX XX2 604 - Baltimore, MD
September 23, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stuart J. Snyder, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carrie E. Johnston
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Kenya, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on June 3, 2009, after failing to appear at a hearing on that date. He filed a motion to reopen on July 22, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied it on September 25, 2009, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be sustained.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). We decide this appeal de novo.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that “exceptional circumstances” prevented his appearance at the hearing. We consider the totality of the evidence in evaluating this issue. See Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998) and Matter of B-A-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 1998). See also generally Kaweesa v. Gonzales, 450 F.3d 62, 68-69 (1st Cir. 2006) (including the strength of the alien’s underlying claim); Romero-Morales v. INS, 25 F.3d 125, 129-31 (2d Cir. 1994); Celis-Castellano v. Ashcroft, 298 F.3d 888 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
Here, we consider that the record contains a statement from the respondent’s former attorney. Therein, counsel admits that she erroneously instructed the respondent to appear for his hearing on June 9, 2009, rather than the correct date of June 3, 2009. The respondent’s declaration corroborates his attorney’s account. In addition, we consider that the respondent filed his motion to reopen almost immediately after learning that he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia. His due diligence in this matter is strong evidence of the respondent’s lack of intention to miss his hearing. We also consider that the respondent appeared at numerous previous hearings since 2007. Further, a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel is present and the respondent has substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See generally Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). It appears that the respondent’s failure to appear at his hearing was caused by an errant instruction from his former counsel.
 
*2 We find based on the totality of the evidence that the respondent has established that reopening of these proceedings based on “exceptional circumstances” is appropriate. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and these proceedings are reopened and remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4035455 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 4035442 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GREGORY YOO BAIK NAGAO
File: AXXX XX0 390 - Honolulu, HI
September 21, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Emmanuel B. Guerrero, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Heith M. Kaneshige
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony pursuant to section 101(a)(43)(F), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(F)º
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony pursuant to section 101(a)(43)(G), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G)º
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(C), I&N ACt ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(C)º - Convicted of firearms or destructive device violation
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on July 24, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision finding that he did not derive citizenship through his mother or through his United States citizen stepfather, who adopted him in 1950. Our decision left in place a previous Immigration Judge decision finding the respondent removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony and ordering his removal from the United States. The respondent’s petition to the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, seeking review of our July 2006 decision, was dismissed.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of South Korea. He has timely appealed the Immigration Judge’s July 17, 2009, decision rejecting his motion to reopen because the Immigration Court lacked jurisdiction to consider this motion, as the Board last dismissed his appeal in July 2006. The respondent also has filed a motion to reopen with this Board, arguing that proceedings should be reopened to allow him to pursue adjustment of status though the approved visa petition filed on his behalf by his United States citizen wife. The appeal is dismissed and the motion to reopen is denied.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s factual determinations, including credibility determinations, for clear error. See United States v. National Assn. of Real Estate Boards, 339 U.S. 485, 495 (1950) (a factual finding is not “clearly erroneous” merely because there are two permissible views of the evidence). The Board reviews de novo questions of law, discretion, judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
We first note that we find no cause to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision that she had no jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s May 11, 2009, motion to reopen. Because the respondent had appealed the Immigration Judge’s last decision of record, jurisdiction over any subsequent motions would rest with the Board, and not the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2, 1003.23(b)(1).
 
*2 Turning to the motion to remand, the respondent claims that reopening is appropriate to assess his eligibility for “newly-acquired relief” in the form of an application for adjustment of status based on an approved visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife. See Respondent’s Brief and Motion at 6. Because the respondent’s motion was filed with the Board on September 8, 2009, it is untimely, as it was filed some 3 years after our last decision in this case, which was issued in July 2006. Generally, a motion to reopen must be filed no later than 90 days after the final administrative decision was rendered. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent argues that the time period for filing the motion should be tolled because his prior counsel provided ineffective assistance in (1) pursuing the argument that removal proceedings should be terminated because the respondent had derived United States citizenship through his parents, (2) failing to pursue below a challenge to the validity of the respondent’s convictions, and (3) failing to pursue below a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act. See Respondent’s Brief and Motion at 7-8.
 
While the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, under whose jurisdiction this case arises, recognizes equitable tolling of motions deadlines in ineffective assistance of counsel cases, an alien must demonstrate not only that his constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). Moreover, an alien must demonstrate substantial compliance with the general procedural requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (requiring the alien to lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative, to give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and to file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made).
 
The respondent has not shown due diligence or any attempt to comply with the Matter of Lozada requirements. In fact, the respondent entirely omits any explanation for the 3-year delay in filing a motion to reopen and claims to be exempt from meeting the Lozada requirements because the ineffective assistance of former counsel was “clear on the record.” See Respondent’s Brief and Motion at 8. As to the latter claim, we find former counsel’s decision to pursue a derivative citizenship claim—as opposed to a direct or an indirect attack on the criminal convictions and resulting grounds of removability—to be distinct from what would constitute “ineffective assistance of counsel.” Indeed, counsel’s strategic decision in this regard is not the type of conduct typically held to be “ineffective assistance.” See Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113, 127 n. 7 (BIA 1998) (citing Thorsteinsson v. INS, 724 F.2d 1365 (9th Cir. 1984)) (ineffective assistance counsel claim rejected where attorney made informed, strategic decision). In any event, such a decision would not constitute “clear” ineffective assistance obviating the need to make some attempt to comply with the Matter of Lozada, supra, requirements.
 
*3 As to due diligence, as noted above, the respondent’s motion is silent on this point. He offers no event that triggered his discovery of the alleged ineffective assistance of past counsel and does not describe his actions over the course of the 3 years that lapsed between the Board’s July 2006 decision and his first attempt to reopen proceedings before the Immigration Judge in May 2009. We note for the record that the respondent indicated that the visa petition filed by his wife was approved in August 2007. Yet since that time, the record does not reflect that the respondent did anything to pursue reopening of proceedings. In the absence of any evidence that he diligently tried to discover ineffective assistance of counsel or that he actually received any ineffective attorney advice, we decline to find that the deadline for timely filing a motion to reopen should be equitably tolled. Iturribarria v. INS, supra.
 
Finally, we note that the respondent’s motion lacks a showing of prejudice, even if we were to find that ineffective assistance of counsel occurred and that tolling was warranted because of the respondent’s diligence. That is, the respondent suffered three serious convictions, i.e., first-degree robbery, possession of a prohibited firearm, and theft. See Immigration Judge’s April 13, 2006, Order. The respondent has not shown that he is admissible to the United States, in light of these convictions, which raise the possibility that he has suffered one or more crimes involving moral turpitude. If so, his ability to secure a waiver of inadmissibility as to those convictions is doubtful, since (1) the robbery and weapons convictions were pursuant to a jury verdict, rendering him ineligible for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act, and (2) he has been found removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony, rendering him ineligible for relief under section 212(h) of the Act.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4035442 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 3780656 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CELESTINO PELAYO RENTERIA
File: AXXX XX4 329 - Orlando, FL
September 13, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michaelangelo Rosario, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 15, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on November 6, 2008. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
We find that proper notice was provided to the respondent. The record reflects that the Immigration Judge reopened proceedings on a prior occasion after the respondent failed to appear. Following the Immigration Judge’s decision reopening the respondent’s case, a hearing notice scheduling the respondent’s case was mailed to the respondent’s attorney of record, Pablo Cabrera, Esquire. We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent received proper notice inasmuch as written notice of his hearing was provided to his attorney of record. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (finding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (providing that whenever a person is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to the attorney or representative of record, or to the person himself if unrepresented). On the Notice of Appeal, the respondent does not contest the Immigration Judge’s determination that notice was provided to the respondent’s former counsel.
 
To the extent that the respondent argues exceptional circumstances because his attorney did not tell him the date of his hearing after it had been reopened by the Immigration Judge, his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is untimely as it was not filed within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Moreover, he has not satisfied any of the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A. G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”). See generally Vaz Dos Reis v. Holder, 606 F.3d 1, 5 (1st Cir. 2010) (stating that with respect to motions to reopen in absentia proceedings courts typically treat ineffective assistance of counsel claims as an exceptional circumstance). In sum, we find no reason to disturb the decision of the Immigration Judge.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3780656 (BIA)

2010 WL 3780637 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDNA HUNTE-DIAZ
File: AXXX XX5 010 - Los Angeles, CA
September 9, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anwer Khan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of Guatemala, was ordered removed from the United States on February 26, 2009. Her appeal of that decision will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). We decide this appeal de novo.
 
The respondent argues in her Notice of Appeal that she was denied consideration for any form of relief from removal on account of the ineffective assistance of her former counsel. She states that she is eligible to be considered for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b. The respondent infers that the Immigration Judge would have considered or even granted her cancellation of removal had it not been for her former attorney’s negligence by not adequately preparing and filing her application. She requests a remand for a hearing.
 
The Board will not grant a request for a remand based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel unless the respondent first establishes certain requirements. In Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. See also Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592 (9th Cir. 2004); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
In the instant case, the respondent has not submitted an affidavit or filed a complaint with the bar association against her former attorney. She has also not provided her former attorney with an opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel. Based upon this lack of proof, we are unable to find that the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements for proving that she was ineffectively represented by her former attorney in these proceedings. We will therefore not remand the record based on this particular allegation.
 
*2 The respondent further vaguely asserts in her Notice of Appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying her motion to continue her February 26, 2009, hearing to complete her application for cancellation of removal. The Immigration Judge noted that she had earlier continued the proceeding for that purpose from February 14, 2008, to February 26, 2009. We find for the reasons stated by the Immigration Judge that the decision denying the respondent’s motion to continue was correct. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3780637 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 3780677 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MICKEY H. FLAVIUS
File: AXXX XX1 442 - Guaynabo, PR
September 7, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul C. Agu, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jorge Ramos
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals the January 28, 2009, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact by an Immigration Judge under the clearly erroneous standard of review, but we may review de novo questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii) (2008); see also Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The record reflects that the respondent was granted voluntary departure on September 22, 2008, with an alternate order of removal. On January 16, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen seeking to adjust his status through his United States citizen spouse. The Immigration Judge correctly denied the motion as untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
In his motion, the respondent alluded to ineffective assistance of counsel by his former counsel. However, the respondent failed to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In that case, we held that ineffective assistance of counsel claims require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned is informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. The Court of Appeals for the First Circuit, where this case arises, has found the application of the noted requirements to be a necessary condition to obtain a reopening of removal proceeding on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Belte-Veloz v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 7, 10 (1st Cir. 2008). Here, the respondent has failed to fulfill any of the above-noted requirements. Nor has the respondent specified how he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s representation. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 562 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent maintains that the Immigration Judge should have reopened his proceedings sua sponte. We find that the respondent’s motion does not establish an exceptional situation sufficient to warrant reopening of his removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3780677 (BIA)
End of Document
