2010 WL 3780682 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABDUL HAI
File: AXXX XX5 144 - New York, NY
September 3, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ishrat Sami, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision issued March 11, 2010. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen because he had established good cause for a continuance of her hearing. The respondent contends that his attorney was required to attend another court hearing at the same time. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
We find that the Immigration Judge’s decision to proceed with the hearing on December 9, 2009, was appropriate under the circumstances. The regulations provide that an Immigration Judge has the discretion to grant a request for continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. However, parties are obligated to appear for a scheduled hearing unless and until the Immigration Judge expressly grants a request for a continuance. See Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988). Since the Immigration Judge did not rule on the respondent’s request for a continuance, the respondent was required to appear for his hearing. See Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996) (the filing of a motion for a continuance does not excuse an alien from failing to appear). The Immigration Judge was within her bounds to conduct proceedings in absentia.
 
Finally, we note that the respondent has not made a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F. 2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent has not established any other exceptional circumstance for his failure to appear. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3780682 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2010 WL 3536720 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MARTIN CARRANZA-VARGAS
File: AXXX XX9 796 - Honolulu, HI
August 26, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Manuel Q. Diones, Esquire
*1 After the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals remanded the case to the Board, we returned the case to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings on July 31, 2009. The Board on May 24, 2010, affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision, in which the judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings, which were conducted in absentia. The respondent, who is from El Salvador, filed a motion with the Board on August 16, 2010. The motion, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded, will be denied.
 
The Board first considers that the respondent was removed from the United States on August 16, 2010, the same day that the motion was filed. The Ninth Circuit has held that 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d) cannot be applied to cause the withdrawal of a motion filed by an alien who has been forcibly removed while the motion is pending before the Board. Coyt v. Holder, 593 F.3d 902, 907 (9th Cir. 2010). As the respondent was removed the same day that the motion was filed, the Board will assume that it retains jurisdiction over the motion, despite the respondent’s removal.
 
In the May 24, 2010, decision, we fully considered the record before determining that the respondent received adequate notice of his removal hearing, where he failed to notify the Immigration Court as to his most current mailing address. Section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(B). The Board also rejected the respondent’s claim that “exceptional circumstances” excused his failure to appear at the removal hearing. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act. Although the respondent contended that an attorney, Ronald Oldenburg, had in his possession the respondent’s address, “¿tºhe respondent … indicates his belief that he has not been a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and a claim alleging such has not been submitted to the Board.” Board’s May 24, 2010, dec., at 2. Moreover, the record did not contain an affidavit from either the respondent or Oldenburg.
 
The pending motion, in part, reargues the respondent’s contention that the Board should have sustained his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny reopening of the in absentia proceedings in this case. As such, the motion is an untimely motion to reconsider, as a motion to reconsider must be filed not later than 30 days after the date of the challenged order. Section 240(c)(6) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b).
 
To the extent that the pending motion may be considered a motion to reopen, it was timely filed. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). However, a motion to reopen imposes a heavy burden on the moving party to show that reopening is warranted. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Here, the motion merely presents an affidavit from the respondent’s girlfriend, stating that she has “instituted an investigation ¿with the Hawaii Office of Disciplinary Counselº with regards to Mr. Oldenburg’s ineffective assistance of counsel leading to ¿the respondent’sº absence from his November 2, 2009, heating.” Kalio Affidavit, at § 6; Respondent’s Mot., at 5, 7-8. The affidavit does not assert that the attorney was ineffective, nor does it explain how the attorney may be responsible for the respondent missing the removal hearing. Moreover, the affidavit does not come from the respondent himself. The girlfriend’s affidavit is insufficient to properly raise a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Oldenburg. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent’s pending motion will, therefore, be denied.
 
ORDER: The pending motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3536720 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 3536734 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AYFER YALINCAK A.K.A AYFEREFAT YALINCAK A.K.A. IRENE KELLY A.K.A. IRENE CATHERINE MARY KELLY A.K.A. IRENE C. KELLY A.K.A. IRENE KELLY YALINCAK A.K.A. JACKIE A.K.A. AYFEREFAT A.K.A. JACKIE YALINCAK A.K.A. AYFERFAT YALINCAK A.K.A. AYFER ELGEZDI YALINCAK
File: AXXX XX3 720 - Hartford, CT
August 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Turkey, moves pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen proceedings and to reissue our last decision, dated May 19, 2009. In that decision, we denied a motion to reopen as untimely. On May 22, 2008, we issued our final order in this matter, dismissing the appeal of the Immigration Judge’s January 16, 2008, decision denying a waiver under section 209(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1159(c), and also denying withholding of removal under section 241(b)(3) of the Act and the Convention Against Torture. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16-18.¿FN1º On July 31, 2008, we denied a motion to reconsider. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). In removal proceedings, motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The present motion, filed on April 14, 2010, is untimely as it was not filed within 90 days of our May 22, 2008, final order. 8 C.F.R. § 1002.3(c). However, the respondent argues that this is due to the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). The motion will be denied.
 
The time limit on motions to reopen may be equitably tolled where the alien establishes that the respondent exercised due diligence to discover or act upon the alleged incompetence of her prior attorney in a timely manner. 8 C.F.R. §1003.2(c); Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127 (2nd Cir. 2008); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3rd 167 (2d Cir. 2006); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000). In addition, the motion must establish that the ineffective assistance caused the respondent substantial prejudice amounting to a due process violation that would warrant a tolling of the time limit in.. See Id; Romero v. INS, 399 F.3d 109, 112 (2nd Cir. 2005), citing U.S. v. Perez, 330 F.3d 97, 101 (2nd Cir. 2001); Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 The respondent argues essentially that her prior counsel, Attorney Edwin T. Gania, failed to timely file a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit of our May 19, 2009, decision denying the first motion to reopen. Attorney Gania filed petitions for review of our May 22, 2008, merits decision, and of our July 31, 2008, decision denying the motion to reconsider, which were consolidated before the Second Circuit. These petitions were denied. Yalincak v. Holder, 352 Fed.Appx. 584, 2009 WL 3739067 (C.A. 2 November 10, 2009) (unpublished) (petition for rehearing and rehearing en banc denied and mandate issued on May 21, 2010). The respondent claims that she paid Attorney Gania to file a petition for review of our May 19, 2009, decision and he failed to do so, and therefore the decision should be reissued to allow the filing of a timely petition for review.
 
The respondent also argues that Attorney Gania was ineffective by failing to file a full and accurate copy of the “Joint Appendix” (a.k.a. “Joint Appendix”) with the Second Circuit (Motion to Reopen and Motion to Reissue Pursuant to Lozada (“Motion”) at 2, 6). The respondent also argues that there is new factual evidence warranting reopening in discretion pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
Regarding the first argument, that the Attorney Gania failed to file a petition for review of our May 19, 2009, decision, the motion proffers, inter alia, a February 11, 2010, letter from Attorney Gania to the respondent (Motion at Exh. 3). In the letter, the attorney admitted that he did not file a petition for review of the May 19, 2009, decision, and offered the respondent a settlement. Attorney Gania offered to waive further payment for the Petition for Rehearing and Rehearing En Banc that was pending at the Second Circuit, and to refund the money paid for the motion to reopen. Alternatively, if the respondent decided to pursue a grievance or bar complaint against the attorney’s office, the attorney would be forced to withdraw from the Second Circuit proceeding because of a conflict of interest.
 
The respondent apparently did not file a bar complaint as no evidence of a bar complaint has been proffered. Also, the Petition for Panel Rehearing and Suggestion for Rehearing En Banc (“Petition for Rehearing”) filed at the Second Circuit on February 23, 2010, indicates Attorney Gania still represents the respondent in front of the Second Circuit (Motion at Exh. 8). See also Yalincak v. Holder, supra. Therefore, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements to establish an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, supra. Furthermore, the respondent’s continued retention of the attorney is antithetical to the claim that the attorney provided ineffective assistance. Moreover, as discussed below, no prejudice from any alleged ineffective assistance of counsel has been shown.
 
*3 Regarding the claim that counsel failed to provide a complete copy of the “Joint Appendix” with the Second Circuit, the respondent’s Petition for Rehearing filed with the Second Circuit places the blame for this on the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) (see Motion at Exh. 8 Petition for Rehearing at 4-5). No ineffective assistance of counsel has been argued on this, or any other matter, before the Second Circuit, which belies the respondent’s claim before the Board that she believes Attorney Gania provided ineffective assistance before the Second Circuit, or the Board. The motion does not include arguments that could not have been raised in a timely motion to reopen or in a petition before the Second Circuit that may have warranted a different outcome in the matter. Therefore, the motion does not establish the substantial prejudice amounting to a due process violation that would warrant a tolling of the time limit in these proceedings. See Id; Romero v. INS, supra; Matter of Lozada, supra. The motion thus does not establish an exception to the time limitation on motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
Finally, the instant motion sets forth discretionary factors for consideration. These include the fact that the respondent’s husband and daughter recently became lawful permanent residents, and that the respondent and her husband are facing health issues (Motion at 16-21). However, there is no objective documentation of the medical or other discretionary factors. Notwithstanding, in consideration of all the factors involved in this matter, this case does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen these proceedings or reissue the final decision under our sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997)(stating that “¿tºhe power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen proceedings and to reissue our May 19, 2009, decision is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

On April 4, 1998, she had been granted asylum (I.J. at 3, 6; Exhs. 1, 2). 2010 WL 3355080 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN CARLOS VALERO-MONTOYA A.K.A. JOSE A. ARCE
File: AXXX XX9 990 - New York, NY
August 11, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Louis Leeder, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 18, 2009, denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on January 19, 2000. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Upon de novo review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision for the reasons stated therein. In reaching this conclusion we note that the respondent’s assertion that he received inaccurate advice from an “agency” and an unidentified attorney which scared him to the point he decided not to go to his hearing on January 19, 2000, is not an exceptional circumstance to excuse his failure to appear. See section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1); 8 U.S.C. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Although the respondent has essentially alleged that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from the unidentified attorney, he has failed to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, he has claimed that he married a United States citizen and that she has filed an I-130 visa petition on his behalf. However, he failed to present evidence that the I-130 had been filed and approved or otherwise establish that he is eligible for adjustment of status. Consequently, we find no basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s determination.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2010 WL 3355073 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IVAN ERIC LINTON
File: AXXX XX8 528 - Miami, FL
August 10, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Richard Jurgens
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative order in this case on August 29, 2008. This case was last before the Board on May 25, 2010, however, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and reconsider for having been filed out of time. In that decision, we found that the respondent had not properly set forth a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, or otherwise offered arguments or evidence of any exceptional situation to persuade us to reconsider our prior order sua sponte or to reopen the proceedings on our own motion under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). On June 23, 2010, the respondent filed the instant motion. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
Although the motion is titled “Motion for Reconsideration”, the respondent has not contested the bases of our decision denying his previous motion. See generally Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (holding that a motion to reconsider a decision of the Board must include, inter alia, an allegation of material factual or legal errors in the prior decision that is supported by pertinent authority). Instead, the respondent has submitted new evidence, including evidence that since our prior order he has attempted to comply with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We find, therefore, that the instant motion is in the nature of a motion to reopen. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). As a motion to reopen, the motion is untimely and number-barred. See sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). We do not find that any of the general exceptions to the filing restrictions apply to the respondent’s motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). Moreover, for the reasons below, we do not find that the exercise of our sua sponte authority is warranted in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
In our decision dismissing the appeal, we found no legal error in the Immigration Judge’s decision pretermitting the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1158(c), because the 1989 conviction underlying the charges of removability was by jury trial. See I.N.S. v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (U.S. 2001); Alexandre v. United States Attorney General, 452 F.3d 1204 (11th Cir. 2006). The respondent now appears to blame his former counsel for his inability to apply for the section 212(c) waiver. Specifically, he asserts that his former counsel failed to inform him of the immigration consequences of his criminal convictions when she conceded removability on his behalf. See Respondent’s Motion at 9.
 
*2 It was due to the respondent’s criminal convictions, rather than to any action on the part of his former counsel in these proceedings, that the respondent was placed in these removal proceedings. The respondent has not contested the fact that he was convicted by a jury trial rather than by a plea of guilty. Cf Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (U.S. 2010) (holding that counsel engaged in deficient performance by failing to advise the defendant that his plea of guilty made him subject to automatic deportation). Nor is there any evidence offered in support of this motion to indicate that an attack on the respondent’s convictions has resulted in a vacatur. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (BIA 1996). We find, therefore, that the respondent has failed to establish that his former counsel committed any error resulting in prejudice to the respondent when she conceded removability on his behalf. See Dakane v. United States Attorney General, 371 F.3d 771 (11th Cir. 2004). In sum, we reject the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and we conclude that reopening sua sponte is not warranted in this case. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely, number-barred motion to reopen will be denied.¿FN1º
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is denied.


2010 WL 3355073 (BIA)

2010 WL 3355059 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SUNIL SOODOO A.K.A. SUNIL RAVIE A.K.A. RAUIE SOODOO
File: AXXX XX3 853 - New York, NY
August 5, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On April 22, 2010, the respondent, a native and citizen of Guyana, filed a timely motion to reopen these removal proceedings to afford him the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status and a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c).
 
In our prior order we agreed with the Immigration Judge that it was futile for the respondent to apply for a 212(c) waiver because it could not waive the respondent’s 2005 conviction for the unlawful possession of more than 30 grams of marijuana.
 
The respondent now argues that his accredited representative erred in admitting that he was convicted of the offense of unlawful possession of marijuana in 2005 and conceding that he was removable under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation (Exhs. 4, 9 and 13; Tr. at 72-73). He claims that his accredited representative should not have introduced the lab report which reflected that the respondent was in possession of 3.8 ounces of marijuana. See Respondent’s Motion at 3. The respondent asserts that he was prejudiced when the lab report, which does not constitute a record of conviction, was submitted to the Immigration Court. In short, the respondent asserts that the Department of Homeland Security was only able to establish that he was convicted of possessing more than 30 grams of marijuana and that he is removable pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act - because of the actions of his representative. The respondent has complied with the requirements for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009).
 
Essentially, the respondent seeks to withdraw the admission and concession entered by his accredited representative. See Respondent’s Motion, Attachment C. We have long held that absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made before, during, or even after a proceeding by an attorney acting in his professional capacity binds his client as a judicial admission; and that there is a strong presumption that such a decision is a reasonable tactical decision. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). Notably, the respondent does not claim that the pleading is factually untrue or incorrect. Given the facts and circumstances of this case, we find that the respondent is bound by the admissions and concessions he made by and through his representative.
 
*2 Additionally, the respondent’s marijuana conviction would have rendered him inadmissible pursuant to section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), and, thus, ineligible for adjustment of status. See section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). Even if the lab report had not been submitted, he would be ineligible for a corresponding waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), because he is a lawful permanent resident who has been convicted of an aggravated felony.
 
Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472 (BIA 1992) (motion to reopen may be denied based upon the failure to establish a prima facie case for the relief sought).
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3355059 (BIA)
End of Document

CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
Considering the intervening events in this case since it was last before the Immigration Judge, the respondent’s motion to reopen her removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge will be granted and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge to provide her the opportunity to pursue an application for adjustment of status. In this regard, we note that the respondent has complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for advancing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel; the immediate relative visa petition filed on her behalf by her United States citizen husband has been approved; and, the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) now states that it does not oppose the respondent’s motion to remand her proceedings to the Immigration Court noting that she appears statutorily eligible to adjust her status. Accordingly, the appeal from the denial of her motion to reopen is sustained and these proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings not in consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
David B. Holmes

2010 WL 3157451 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS CASAS-AGUILAR
File: AXXX XX2 374 - Phoenix, AZ
July 22, 2010
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher J. Stender, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Victoria Levin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on August 5, 1992, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s March 16, 1992, decision finding the respondent statutorily ineligible for voluntary departure and ordering him deported as charged to Mexico.
 
On April 29, 2010, the respondent filed a motion to reopen these proceedings premised, in part, on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion is untimely filed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not relevant here, a motion to reopen “must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which a final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is earlier”).
 
However, because this case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit and the respondent presents an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we will consider whether this case warrants equitable tolling of the time limits on motions to reopen. The Ninth Circuit has held that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
The respondent has failed to provide an explanation of his efforts to pursue his case since he was issued a final deportation order in 1992. Therefore, we find equitable tolling of the 90-day deadline is unwarranted. In addition, the respondent has not complied with the requirements for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009).
 
Furthermore, the respondent has failed to show prejudice resulting from his attorney’s actions. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra; Ahmed v. Mukasey, 548 F.3d 768, 772 (9th Cir. 2008) (“prejudice results when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. The petitioner does not need to show that she would win on her claims absent her attorneys’ deficient performance. She need only show plausible grounds for relief” (citations omitted)). The respondent is correct that section 249 of the Act does not require that the registry applicant be a person of good moral character for a specified period of time. See generally Matter of Sanchez-Linn, 20 I&N Dec. 362 (BIA 1991). However, we need not determine whether the respondent could have established good moral character for registry purposes, because he was otherwise ineligible for registry at the time of his 1992 deportation hearing. To be eligible for registry, the applicant must be admissible pursuant to section 212(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a). See section 249 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §1259. The respondent was inadmissible under section 212(a)(2) of the Act due to his 1989 drug-related conviction.¿FN1º Accordingly, he has failed to establish that he was prejudiced by his attorney’s conduct. Based on the foregoing, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
*2 The respondent’s request for a stay is denied. The Board entertains stays only when an appeal, a motion to reopen, or a motion to reconsider is pending before the Board. In the instant case, the respondent does not have an appeal or a motion pending before us. See 8 C.F.R. §1003.6.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In its motion opposing reopening, the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) proffers documentation showing that the respondent has two 1989 drug convictions. See DHS’s Motion, Attachments A and B. The DHS has also provided documentation showing that the respondent was criminally convicted and incarcerated in 2008 and 2009. See DHS’s Motion, Attachments C and D.

APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent’s motion is denied. This case was last before the Board on November 17, 2009, when the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent removable as charged and denying the respondent’s request for a continuance. On January 19, 2010, the respondent filed a motion requesting reconsideration and stay of removal. The respondent’s motion is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Furthermore, the respondent, who has presented no evidence demonstrating either that his first visa petition was improperly denied¿FN1º or that his second visa petition, which was filed on his behalf by the same spouse/petitioner who filed the first denied petition, has not identified any error of fact or law in the Board’s previous decision that would alter the outcome. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing the requirements for motions to reconsider). See also Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785, 792 (BIA 2009) (recognizing no abuse of discretion in the denial of a continuance for adjudication of a second visa petition when the first I-130 filed by the same petitioner was denied) (citations omitted). Moreover, we do not find the respondent’s mere disagreement with or disapproval of the outcome of the Board’s previous detailed decision sufficient to demonstrate either that the Board improperly evaluated or disregarded the facts and evidence presented or legal error.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).

2010 WL 3027616 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BESIK AVALIANI
File: AXXX XX5 950 - New York, NY
July 9, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brian I. Kaplan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated June 4, 2008, denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
We review findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, under a clearly erroneous standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent seeks reopening on the grounds that his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance. To warrant reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent must comply with the requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and demonstrate that he was prejudiced by counsel’s deficient performance.
 
The respondent raised his ineffective assistance of counsel claim with the Immigration Judge but the Immigration Judge did not address it. Inasmuch as we do not make findings of fact in the course of deciding appeals, we will remand the record for such findings of fact and legal conclusions. See generally Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). Accordingly, we will grant the respondent’s appeal and the record will be remanded.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for the entry of a new decision.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD

2010 WL 3027580 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JESSICA PATRICIA LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX6 711 - Bloomington, MN
July 9, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kenneth Byrd, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on December 17, 2009, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal that was entered against her on November 16, 1999. The respondent now files another motion to reopen, claiming that she received ineffective assistance of counsel from the prior attorney who filed her first motion to reopen and urging that her proceedings should be reopened. The Department &Homeland Security has not responded to the current motion.
 
Even assuming the time limits do not apply to the present motion to reopen, because she still seeks reopening based on a claim of lack of proper notice, the motion is number-barred. Section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A) (providing that an alien may filed only one motion to reopen removal proceedings); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (only one motion to reopen may be filed regarding an order of removal entered in absentia); see also Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000).
 
Although the respondent does not make her argument clear, it appears she wishes the motions number limit to be equitably tolled because she received ineffective assistance of counsel with regard to the filing of her first motion to reopen. Habchy v. Gonzales, 471 F.3d 858, 864-65 (8th Cir. 2006) (discussing other circuits’ recognition of the “sparingly invoked” doctrine of equitable tolling for ineffective assistance of counsel claims). Specifically, she contends that his failure to include an affidavit or other evidence caused her motion to be denied.
 
We first find that the respondent has failed to meet any of the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), such that she has properly raised an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. More importantly, she has not shown that equitable tolling is warranted in this case because she has not shown that she was prejudiced. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had signed the certified mail receipt showing she received the Notice to Appear (NTA), and she has never claimed that she did not sign that receipt. Rather, her argument on appeal was that the certified mail receipt might have pertained to some other document, not that she did not sign it. In our decision, we specifically found that the certified mail receipt related to the NTA. Thus, it does not appear that an affidavit would have any impact on the finding that the respondent was properly served with the NTA in this case and had notice of the removal proceedings against her. Nor has she identified any other evidence that counsel should have submitted that would have refuted this finding. She thus has not shown any failure on prior counsel’s part that prejudiced her, such that equitable tolling of the motions number limit would be warranted. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3027580 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 3027587 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BENEDICTO EMILIO CAMPOS-GUZMAN
File: AXXX XX1 957 - Houston, TX
July 8, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Daniel Perez-Garcia, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Wm. Tracy Hamby
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 1, 2009, which ordered him removed from the United States. The parties have provided arguments on appeal. On appeal, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion denied.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but we review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). In this case, the record shows that the Immigration Judge set a deadline of March 31, 2009, for the filing of any and all applications for relief and advised the respondent of the consequences for the failure to file applications by the deadline (Tr. at 14, 16). However, no application was filed. Based on the undisputed facts, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deem the applications for relief abandoned. Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c).
 
The respondent seeks to reopen proceedings based on ineffective assistance of counsel and to remand the record so that he may pursue applications for cancellation of removal and withholding of removal. However, the motion does not establish compliance with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Specifically, although the motion indicates that the respondent filed a grievance against prior counsel, the motion is not accompanied by evidence to that effect. In addition, inasmuch as the motion does not include an application for either form of relief or any supporting documentation, the respondent cannot establish that prior counsel’s failure to timely file a claim for such relief resulted in substantial prejudice. Bolvito v. Mukasey, 527 F.3d 428, 438 (5th Cir. 2008). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed and the motion is denied.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3027587 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2010 WL 3027582 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABDEL KARIM ABDRABBOH AL-TARAWNEH
File: AXXX XX5 243 - Chicago, IL
July 8, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hena Mansori, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joseph M. Yeung
Senior Attorney
*1 The respondent, who is from Jordan, filed a timely motion to reopen on January 27, 2010, after the Board dismissed his appeal on October 29, 2009. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) opposes the pending motion, which will be granted.
 
An Immigration Judge on November 4, 2008, issued a removal order concerning the respondent. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent was ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act, as failing to show the requisite hardship to a qualifying relative. The Immigration Judge also declined to further continue proceedings. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent failed to show that he was prima facie eligible for adjustment of status, not having submitted an application for relief or supporting documents (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 36). Rather, the adjustment application had been filed by the respondent with the DHS (Tr. at 32).
 
The respondent seeks to have proceedings reopened in order to pursue an application for adjustment of status. See section 245 of the Act. The respondent’s wife was adjusted to lawful permanent resident status on February 27, 2008 (Respondent’s Mot., at Exh. G). This resulted after a visa petition filed by the respondent’s wife’s brother, which was approved on April 4, 1997, became current (Respondent’s Mot., at Exh. F). The respondent argues that he is eligible for adjustment of status as a derivative beneficiary to the visa approved on behalf of his wife.
 
The respondent claims that his prior counsel, Raul Villasuso, Jr., was ineffective, because counsel failed to pursue an application for adjustment of status with the Immigration Judge, or inform him that the application had to be filed with the Immigration Court, rather than with the DHS (Respondent’s Mot., at 8-11).
 
Contrary to the argument of the DHS, the Board finds that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Former counsel disputes the respondent’s charges, although he does not explain why he argued, on appeal to the Board, that the Immigration Judge should have allowed the respondent to pursue his adjustment application before the DHS. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(1)(i)(“In the case of any alien who has been placed … in removal proceedings ¿other than as an arriving alienº, the Immigration Judge hearing the proceeding has exclusive jurisdiction to adjudicate any application for adjustment of status the alien may file”).
 
*2 The respondent also contends that former counsel did not inform him of the Board’s October 29, 2009, decision until January 7, 2010, and therefore his failure to depart under the Board’s voluntary departure order should be excused (Respondent’s Mot., at 6, 7-8; Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶ 36). See Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87 (BIA 2007)(alien has not voluntarily failed to depart the United States under section 240B(d)(1) of the Act when the alien, through no fault of his own, was unaware of the voluntary departure order). Under the circumstances, the respondent’s failure to depart under the Board’s voluntary departure order will be excused.
 
In consideration of the respondent’s motion and arguments, the case will be remanded to the Immigration Court for the respondent to pursue an application for adjustment of status.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded in order to afford the respondent an opportunity to present his application for adjustment of status to the Immigration Judge.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3027582 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 3027550 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JULIO LEDESMA-VASQUEZ
File: AXXX XX9 953 - Los Angeles, CA
July 1, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Merlyn N. Hernandez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on January 22, 2010, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
On February 22, 2010, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider our prior order. In our prior decision we observed that although the respondent indicated that he would supplement his motion to reopen, he had failed to do so. The respondent now asserts that he did submit additional documentation to this Board on or about September 16, 2009. Such documentation shows that he complied with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for presenting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The documentation also establishes his prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1255(i).
 
Giving the respondent the benefit of the doubt that the additional documentation was submitted prior to the issuance of our decision, we will consider the respondent’s prior motion together with the newly submitted documentation. The respondent’s July 24, 2009, motion to reopen was filed five years after we issued a final administrative order in this case on July 26, 2004. Because this case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit and the respondent presents an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we will consider whether the 90-day motions deadline should be equitably tolled. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that the motions deadline may be equitably tolled where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error).
 
The respondent has demonstrated that he exercised due diligence in pursuing his case. The respondent states that his former counsel represented him for 13 years from March 1996 until around February 2009. The respondent retained former counsel to file a motion to reopen in 2006 so that he could apply for adjustment of status as an immediate relative. In early 2009, he sought a second legal opinion and eventually learned that the 2006 motion had never been filed. On July 21, 2009, as a result of current counsel’s inquiries, counsel acknowledged that he failed to file a motion to reopen in July 2006, as he had agreed to do. He refunded the $3,610 legal fees to the respondent. The respondent filed a motion to reopen shortly thereafter.
 
To successfully raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent must show that (1) counsel failed to perform with sufficient competence; and, (2) he was prejudiced by counsel’s performance, that is, that counsel’s performance was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004).
 
*2 While the respondent has demonstrated that former counsel failed to perform with sufficient competence, he has failed to demonstrate that his case was prejudiced by counsel’s conduct. In July 2006, the respondent sought reopening for adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(i) of the Act, based on his wife’s April 2006 naturalization. The evidence of record establishes that the respondent was prima facie eligible for 245(i) adjustment of status at that time. However, even if counsel had promptly filed the motion to reopen in 2006 as agreed, the motion would have been untimely filed. Absent exceptional circumstances, which have not been shown, the motion would have been denied as untimely filed. Accordingly, even though the respondent exercised due diligence, he failed to establish that counsel prejudiced his case. Accordingly, we find no error in our prior decision.
 
Additionally, we find no error in our resolution of the respondent’s due process argument. The respondent asserts that his case should not have been “repapered” and, in any event, it was done without giving him notice pursuant to section 309(c)(3) of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996 (IIRIRA). It is undisputed that he was served with an Order to Show Cause (OSC) on or about December 28, 1994. We recognize that the record is unclear about whether the OSC was actually filed with the Immigration Court and, if so, whether those proceedings had been terminated (Tr. at 21-29). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.14(a) (proceedings commence when the charging document is filed with the Immigration Court by the Service).
 
Regardless, repapering is inapplicable in this case. Even if the respondent had been subject to deportation proceedings, he would not have been subject to the “stop-time rule” in conjunction with an application for suspension of deportation under former section 244(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1234(a). See section 309(c)(5)(A) of the IIRIRA, as amended by section 203(a)(1) of the Nicaraguan Adjustment and Central American Relief Act of 1997 (NACARA). See also Matter of Nolasco, 22 I&N Dec. 632 (BIA 1999). The respondent acknowledged that more than 7 years had transpired between the date of his initial entry into the United States and the date of service of the OSC. See Respondent’s July 24, 2009 Motion at 19. See also Memorandum of Bo Cooper, General Counsel for the INS, dated Dec. 7, 1999, reproduced in 77 Interpreter Releases 39, App. 1 (Jan. 10, 2000). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3027550 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAXIMILIANO ARGUELLO GARCIA A.K.A. FRANCISCO AYALA RODRIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX2 500 - Cleveland, OH
June 30, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brian C. DiFranco, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The motion is denied. This case was last before the Board on December 18, 2009, when the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). On May 17, 2010, the respondent filed a motion requesting reconsideration and sua sponte reopening. The respondent’s motion is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b), (c). Furthermore, the respondent, who was found to have acquired his lawful permanent resident status through fraud or misrepresentation, has not identified any error of fact or law in the Board’s previous decision that would alter the outcome. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing the requirements for motions to reconsider). Cf. Matter of Koloamatangi, 23 I&N Dec. 548 (BIA 2003) (finding that an alien who acquired permanent resident status through fraud or misrepresentation has never been “lawfully admitted for permanent residence” and is therefore ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act). Significantly, we do not find the respondent’s mere disagreement with or disapproval of the outcome of our previous detailed decision sufficient to demonstrate either that we improperly evaluated or disregarded the facts and evidence presented or legal error.
 
Moreover, the respondent has not identified any changed circumstances in Mexico that would have excepted his untimely motion from the applicable time limitations. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). The respondent’s motion also fails to comply with both the statutory and regulatory requirements for reopening. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 100.3.2(c). The respondent’s motion does not contain any new or previously unavailable or unobtainable evidence demonstrating the respondent’s eligibility for any other forms of relief.¿FN1º
 
Finally, we find no other reason for this Board’s further consideration of the respondent’s claim on our own motion pursuant to this Board’s sua sponte authority under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
*2 On this record, we find no reason to overlook the untimeliness of the respondent’s motion. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess

2010 WL 3027567 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NEVON MILTON
File: AXXX XX0 786 - Oakdale, LA
June 30, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Nora E. Norman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Continuance, termination
 
The respondent has filed a timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s April 5, 2010, decision. In that decision the Immigration Judge found the respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, removable, as charged, based on his admissions, and concession of removability (Tr. at 9), including his 2009 Florida controlled substance conviction,¿FN1º a “drug-trafficking” aggravated felony offense as defined under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101 (a)(43)(B). See also Group Exh. 1. In addition, the Immigration Judge had previously denied the respondent’s request to continue the proceedings while awaiting the result of his post-conviction motion to vacate this conviction. Finally, noting that the respondent had expressed no fear of persecution or torture if returned to Jamaica, and having found the respondent convicted of an aggravated felony offense, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent had failed to establish his statutory eligibility for any relief from removal, and ordered his removal to Jamaica. The appeal will be dismissed. The respondent’s request to proceed on appeal n forma pauperis is granted under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). See Matter of Chicas, 19 I&N Dec. 114 (BIA 1984).
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2010); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, judgment, and all other issues in an appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (2010); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008) rev’d on other grounds, Kaplun v. Attorney General of the United States, 602 F.3d 260 (3d Cir. 2010); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
*2 At the outset, to the extent the respondent has challenged the Immigration Judge’s findings as to his removability and eligibility for relief from removal, we consider that the distinct and formal concession of removability, made by the respondent’s attorney acting in his professional capacity and in the respondent’s presence during the removal hearing, is binding on the respondent as a judicial admission (Tr. at 9). Applicable regulations, which have the force and effect of law as to the Immigration Judges and this Board, provide in pertinent part that “the immigration judge may determine that removability as charged has been established by the admissions of the respondent.” 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c) (2010). The respondent in the instant case has not established that circumstances exist that would permit the withdrawal of his prior judicial admissions. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made as a tactical decision by an attorney in a deportation proceeding is binding on his alien client); cf 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(B) (authorizing summary dismissal of appeals when “¿tºhe only reason for the appeal specified by the party concerned involves a finding of fact or a conclusion of law that was conceded by that party at a prior proceeding”); see generally Pool Co. v. Cooper, 274 F.3d 173, 185 (5th Cir. 2001) (finding that the court was entitled to treat a concession as a binding judicial admission of fact.); Martinez v. Bally’s Louisiana, Inc., 244 F.3d 474 (5th Cir 2001) (a judicial admission is conclusive, unless the court allows it to be withdrawn).
 
The respondent has also raised a claim of denial of due process premised on his allegations of ineffective assistance by his former attorney’s presentation of his case. Where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is made, the Board has found that certain criteria must be met. Specifically, the claim must be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make, counsel must be informed of the allegations against him and provided an opportunity to respond, and the respondent must indicate whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162 (5th Cir. 2006) (citing Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-498 (5 th Cir. 2000) (upholding the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada)). We note the respondent here has failed to meet any of the criteria set forth in Lozada, in order to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*3 Moreover, even if we were to consider arguendo the respondent’s contentions on appeal challenging his removability, we find his arguments to be unavailing. The record includes undisputed evidence showing that the respondent was convicted of Possession of Ecstacy with Intent to Sell or Deliver, in violation of FLA. STAT. ANN. § 893.13(1)(a)(1). Although the Palm Beach County Circuit Court withheld adjudication of guilt and placed the respondent on 18 months’ probation, this offense qualifies as a conviction for federal immigration purposes. See INA § 101(a)(48)(A), 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A) (defining a “conviction” under the Act, in the case of an alien whose adjudication of guilt has been withheld, as a case where the alien has entered a plea of guilty to an offense and the judge has ordered some form of punishment, penalty, or restraint on the alien’s liberty to be imposed); see also Madriz-Alvarado v. INS, 383 F.3d 321, 331 (5th Cir. 2004).
 
Furthermore, the respondent’s application for post-conviction relief in the Florida courts does not alter the fact that the 2009 Florida “drug trafficking” aggravated felony conviction may nevertheless be used as the factual predicate for an order of removal or a determination of statutory ineligibility for relief from removal. Specifically, the respondent contends that his conviction may be invalid because he entered a defective guilty plea due to ineffective assistance from criminal counsel. However, the fact that the respondent may be pursuing post-conviction relief in the form of a collateral attack on a conviction in criminal court does not affect its finality for federal immigration purposes, or warrant the grant of a motion to continue. See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506 (BIA 1992). As a general rule, an Immigration Judge need not hold removal proceedings in abeyance based upon the mere possibility that a collateral criminal proceeding could have a future impact on the alien’s immigration status. The respondent had (and has) a valid “conviction” for immigration purposes, and his collateral attack upon that conviction does not operate to negate its validity until it is in fact overturned or materially amended. Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996).
 
The respondent has presented no evidence with this appeal that any collateral attack on his 2009 Florida conviction underlying these removal proceedings has been vacated, and therefore the respondent has not established that the Immigration Judge’s denial of his continuance request has compromised the fairness of his removal proceedings. The respondent references the recent decision of Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S. Ct. 1473 (2010), in which the United States Supreme Court ruled that a lawyer representing an alien in connection with a guilty plea to a criminal offense has a constitutional duty to advise the alien about the risk of deportation arising from the conviction. However, the respondent’s speculation that his conviction might be invalid, including in light of Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, does not change the finality for immigration purposes of such conviction, unless and until it were to be overturned by a criminal court. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996).
 
*4 Accordingly, for the reasons discussed above, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record reflects that the respondent was convicted on August 5, 2009, in the Fifteenth Judicial Circuit Court, in and for Palm Beach County, Florida, for the offense of Possession of Ecstacy with Intent to Sell or Deliver, in violation of FLA. STAT. ANN § 893.13(1)(a)(1), and was sentenced to a term of probation of 18 months (Exh. 3).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JONATHAN R BECERRA-TORRES
File: AXXX XX2 909 - Imperial, CA
June 29, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank T. Morell, Esquire
 
REISSUED DECISION
 
*1 The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal on February 26, 2009, and denied a previous motion seeking reissuance on July 22, 2009. On November 27, 2009, the respondent, who is from Mexico, filed an untimely and number-barred motion to reopen. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The Board will reopen proceedings under its authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and reissue the February 26, 2009, decision.
 
First, however, to the extent that the respondent seeks reopening as to the merits of the Board’s February 26, 2009, decision, based on ineffective assistance of counsel of prior counsel Ray Antonio Estolano, or pursuant to 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(a), the motion will be denied. In affirming the Immigration Judge, the Board found the respondent ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, as being unable to establish the 7 years of continuous residence necessary to qualify for such relief, after being admitted as a lawful permanent resident of the United States on October 10, 1994. Section 240A(d)(1) of the Act.
 
A respondent claiming that he received ineffective assistance of counsel must show that counsel failed to perform with “sufficient competence,” Alcala v. Holder, 563 F.3d 1009, 1019 (9th Cir. 2009), such that proceedings are “so fundamentally unfair that the alien is prevented from reasonably presenting ¿ hisº case.” Torres-Chavez v. Holder, 567 F.3d 1096, 1100 (9th Cir. 2009). The respondent “must also show substantial prejudice by demonstrating that the alleged violation affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Id. (citation omitted); Zetino v. Holder, 596 F.3d 517, 525 (9th Cir. 2010)(a showing of prejudice concerning an alleged due process violation “… means that the outcome of the proceeding may have been affected by the alleged violation”).
 
The motion argues that former counsel did not present facts showing that the respondent resided with his lawful permanent resident parents for 7 years prior to his first adult use of marijuana, in July, 1999. Respondent’s Mot., at 7; Mercado-Zazueta v. Holder, 580 F.3d 1102 (9th Cir. 2009)(alien’s father’s lawful permanent resident status was imputed to the alien when he was an unemancipated minor child living with his father, in order to satisfy the residence requirement of section 240A(a)(1) of the Act).
 
*2 The respondent fails to show that he was prejudiced by counsel’s actions. The respondent testified that he came to live in the United States in 1994 (Tr. at 11-12). His current declaration admits that he has no personal recollection of when he came to live in the United States, and his only evidence in this regard is an immunization record indicating that he may have received an immunization in California in 1992 (Respondent’s Declaration, at ¶ 7; Respondent’s Mot., Exh. A).
 
The motion also argues that his use of marijuana in 1999 was an act of juvenile delinquency which did not render him inadmissible under section 212(a)(2) of the Act and prevent him from establishing 7 years of continuous residence after his admission (Respondent’s Mot., at 8-9). However, as the Immigration Judge found, the respondent turned 18 on July 31, 1999, and smoked marijuana on that date, as well as in 2000 (I.J. at 5; Tr. at 17-20); see also Board’s Feb. 26, 2009, dec. To the extent that the respondent otherwise argues that he should have been found eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, Respondent’s Mot., at 9-12, the Immigration Judge and Board fully considered his testimony and found otherwise. The Board finds no reason to reconsider these decisions. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006)(a motion to reconsider must allege a material factual or legal error, assert that the Board erred in affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision without opinion, or argue a change in law).
 
As to the pending motion’s request for reissuance of the Board’s February 26, 2009, decision, the motion argues that the decision should be reissued, based on Estolano’s actions. That is, in the prior motion seeking reissuance, Estolano admitted that he failed to provide the Board with a change of address form, as required by regulation. The respondent has met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The Board’s decision will be reissued and treated as if entered on today’s date. Singh v. Gonzales, 494 F.3d 1170 (9th Cir. 2007) (reissuance of prior Board decision may be warranted).
 
ORDER: The respondent’s pending motion to reissue is granted. In all other respects, the motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated February 26, 2009, attached hereto, is hereby reissued and shall be treated as entered as of today’s date.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s July 22, 2009, denying reissuance is vacated.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2010 WL 2846329 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANGEL HERNANDEZ-CRUZ
File: AXXX XX3 319 - San Antonio, TX
June 28, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pascual Madrigal, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carmen A. Leal
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s March 11, 2010, decision denying his application for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1255. The respondent also requests a waiver of the appellate filing fee. The fee waiver request is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). The record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility determinations, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was admitted temporarily to the United States as a non-immigrant worker on March 27, 2008, but remained longer than authorized (I.J. at 2; Exh. 2). He is married to a United States citizen and is the beneficiary of an approved Alien Relative Petition (Form I-130) (I.J. at 2; Exh. 5). Based upon the foregoing, through his former counsel, he articulated relief from removal in the form of adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, and he was scheduled for a hearing on the merits of this application to be held on February 1, 2010 (I.J. at 2; Exh. 7).
 
At the February 1, 2010, hearing, the Immigration Judge observed that no adjustment application had been filed with the Immigration Court (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 11 - 13). The Immigration Judge continued the matter until March 11, 2010, and set a deadline of March 4, 2010, for filing the adjustment application and any supporting documents (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 35). The respondent, through former counsel, submitted his application for relief without supporting documentation or a motion to accept late filing on March 8, 2010 (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 39-40, 42-43; Exh. 7). At the March 11, 2010, hearing the respondent’s attorney first sought a further continuance and then attempted to withdraw the respondent’s application for adjustment of status without first consulting with his client (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 54-62). Then, after being given an opportunity to speak to the respondent privately, his attorney indicated that the sole form of relief sought was adjustment of status, and he submitted the case for a decision without further supplementing the record (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 62-64). Based on the foregoing, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s adjustment application (I.J. at 7-8). The Immigration Judge also held that the attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel throughout the course of proceedings as he failed to (1) provide competent representation, (2) act with reasonable promptness and diligence, and (3) adequately communicate with the respondent in violation of 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.102(o), (q), and (r) (I.J. at 3-5).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent, through new counsel, cites his prior attorney’s deficient performance and seeks a remand to further pursue his application for adjustment of status (Resp. Brief at 4). Generally, a motion to remand is subject to the same substantive requirements as a motion to reopen. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). As such, usually a respondent must make a prima facie showing of both statutory and discretionary eligibility for the relief requested. See e.g., INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, supra. Additionally, because the respondent’s remand request is based on ineffective assistance, the general requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), are relevant. According to our holding in Matter of Lozada, a motion based on ineffective assistance generally should be supported by: (1) an affidavit from the aggrieved respondent testifying to the relevant facts, including information about scope of the representation agreement and any alleged breaches by the attorney, (2) evidence that the former attorney has been advised of allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond along with any response offered, and (3) evidence that appropriate disciplinary authorities have been notified or, if not, the reason such notification was not pursued. See id. at 639.
 
This case is unique because the Immigration Judge developed the record and made specific findings of fact with respect to the ineffective assistance provided by the respondent’s former counsel and these findings are relevant to the factors outlined in Matter of Lozada. Specifically, he concluded that the respondent’s former counsel breached his duty to communicate with and keep the respondent apprised of developments in his case, explain the nature of the proceedings against the respondent and the relief available to him, and timely prepare and file the application for relief (I.J. at 3, 5; Tr. at 11-15, 36, 39-55, 59, 63). For example, the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent’s counsel did not inform the respondent about the untimely filing of the adjustment application and the respondent first learned about this particular breach on the record at the March 11, 2010 hearing, evidencing one instance of the attorney’s failure to appropriately communicate with the respondent (I.J. at 5; Tr. 44, 50). Additionally, the Immigration Judge also specifically highlighted the attorney’s attempt to withdraw the adjustment application without first consulting with the respondent as another serious breach demonstrating ineffective assistance (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 54-62). In light of these specific findings illustrating the particularly egregious nature of the attorney’s conduct, particularly when coupled with the respondent’s apparent statutory eligibility for adjustment of status, we find that remand is warranted in the interests of justice.¿FN1º See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998) (requiring ineffective assistance be so egregious that it renders the hearing unfair); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640; see also Ogbemudia v. INS, 988 F.2d 595, 598 (5th Cir. 1993) (requiring the respondent to demonstrate “substantial prejudice” in order to establish that a hearing was fundamentally unfair). On remand, the parties may supplement the record as necessary to assist the Immigration Judge in adjudicating the application for relief.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On appeal, the Department of Homeland Security maintains that the removal order should be upheld as the respondent failed to submit an affidavit of support below, and thus, he cannot establish that he is admissible and that he is not a public charge (DHS Brief at 2). While this is an accurate characterization of the record, we cannot conclude that the failure to submit a legally sufficient affidavit of support can be separated from the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim, as the claim is based in part on the attorney’s failure to communicate with him. As such, this failure to communicate appears to have infected the entirety of the proceedings.

2010 WL 2846335 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUBEN ISIAS ELIAS CASTRO
File: AXXX XX8 628 - Arlington, VA
June 28, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kamah Thoronka, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Deborah Todd
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 29, 2008, granting the respondent the privilege of voluntarily departing the United States pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). On appeal, the respondent claims, through present counsel, that he was ineffectively represented at his hearing before the Immigration Judge. The respondent has not presented any evidence in support of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and does not identify any evidence before the Immigration Judge that supports his assertion that his prior attorney never advised him of other potential relief. Indeed, the record indicates that prior counsel explored all possibilities with the respondent (Tr. at 3). The statements of respondent’s present counsel on appeal are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980). The respondent’s uncorroborated assertion on appeal, through counsel, is insufficient to establish that his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance in this matter. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Inasmuch as the respondent has failed to establish that his former counsel rendered ineffective assistance in this matter, the respondent has failed to establish that the waiver of appeal required in order to receive voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Act was unknowing and involuntary. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(D). Therefore, the respondent, through former counsel, waived appeal and the appeal he has filed with this Board is improper. This Board has no jurisdiction over the respondent’s appeal because he necessarily waived appeal in order to receive the extended period of voluntary departure he pursued through former counsel. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. The Immigration Judge’s decision in this matter was final on October 29, 2008, to the same extent as if no appeal was taken. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction.
 
Jim Hilz
