Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAUL GARCIA RODRIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX1 143 - Lancaster, CA
June 25, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaak Olesk, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thelma M. Gonzalez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Termination; remand
 
In a decision dated March 3, 2010, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable based on his concessions and, since no application for relief had been filed, ordered him removed to Mexico. The respondent’s appeal of that decision will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) failed to submit evidence to establish that the respondent was present without being admitted or paroled. Although he asserts that he has withdrawn all prior concessions to the charges of removability (see Tr. at 2), the Immigration Judge did not permit the respondent to do so because the only reason given for the request was that the respondent had a new attorney; no allegations of ineffective assistance were made and the respondent did not present evidence to meet the procedural requirements for such a claim or to show that he was prejudiced.¿FN1º The Immigration Judge also noted that the respondent and his current counsel had sufficient time, after numerous continuances, in which to gather and submit evidence to support the respondent’s claim that he had been lawfully admitted or paroled into the United States and, therefore, was not removable (I.J. at 4-5).
 
On appeal, the respondent again does not assert ineffective assistance of counsel and also provides no evidence to support his continuing claim of lawful entry into the United States. We can find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent removable as charged. Since no applications for relief have been submitted and the respondent does not allege eligibility for any form of relief, we decline to remand proceedings.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (stating that in order to prevail on a motion to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must establish and include the following: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not); see also Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000) (requiring error and prejudice for an alien to prevail on a due process claim).

2010 WL 2846365 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOUGLEY SOLEMEON SCOTT
File: AXXX XX7 266 - Buffalo, NY
June 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert D. Kolken, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Denise C. Hochul
Senior Attorney
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact (withdrawn)
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
  
APPLICATION: Termination; adjustment of status
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s July 10, 2008, decision pretermitting his application for adjustment of status and finding him removable as charged.¿FN1º The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a brief in opposition to the respondent’s appeal. The appeal will be sustained in part, and the record will be remanded.
 
The respondent argues that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance by conceding the charge of removability and that the Immigration Judge should have found that he is not removable pursuant to section 237(a)(1)(A), with reference to section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(1)(A), 1182(a)(6)(C)(i). We find, however, that the respondent’s prior attorney conceded removability, as so charged, and that concession of removability is binding on the respondent. See Tr. at 15, 19; Hoodho v. Holder, 558 F.3d 184, 191 (2d Cir. 2009) (holding that “facts admitted by a party are ‘judicial admissions that bind th¿atº ¿partyº throughout th¿eº litigation.”’). While the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not complied with the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The respondent argues in the alternative that the Immigration Judge should not have pretermitted his application for adjustment of status. The Immigration Judge pretermitted the application, finding that the respondent did not meet the threshold requirement for statutory eligibility to demonstrate that he is not likely to become a public charge and thus would not be inadmissible to the United States. In particular, the respondent submitted an Affidavit of Support (Form I-864) with his spouse as his sponsor. The Immigration Judge concluded that the income his spouse listed on the form does not meet the minimum household income requirement based on the DHS attorney’s statement that the 2008 poverty guidelines for a family of four require an income of $26,500 (Tr. at 27). The respondent argues that the Affidavit of Support met the threshold requirement because it included his income in addition to his wife’s income.
 
*2 An affidavit of support is sufficient if it demonstrates that a sponsor’s:
reasonably expected household income for the year in which the intending immigrant filed the application for an immigrant visa or adjustment of status … would equal at least 125 percent of the Federal poverty line for the sponsor’s household size as defined in 8 CFR 213a.1, under the Poverty Guidelines in effect when the intending immigrant filed the application for an immigrant visa or for adjustment of status ….
 
8 C.F.R. § 213a.2(c)(2)(ii)(C). Under the regulations, “household income” is defined as “the income used to determine whether the sponsor meets the minimum income requirements under sections 213A(f)(1)(E), 213A(f)(3), or 213A(f)(5) of the Act” and includes the income of the sponsor, and of the sponsor’s spouse and any other person included in determining the sponsor’s household size, if the spouse or other person is at least 18 years old and has signed a Form I-864A. 8 C.F.R. § 213a.1. The “household income may not include the income of the intending immigrant, “unless the intending immigrant is either the sponsor’s spouse or has the same principal residence as the sponsor and the preponderance of the evidence shows that the intending immigrant’s income results from the intending immigrant’s lawful employment in the United States or from some other lawful source that will continue to be available to the intending immigrant after he or she acquires permanent resident status.” 8 C.F.R. § 213a.1 (emphasis added). Further, the instructions to the Form I-864 state that the income requirements can be met using income from the intending immigrant if the income will continue from the same source after he or she becomes a lawful permanent resident.
 
The respondent’s application for adjustment of status (Form I-485) was filed with the Immigration Judge in 2007, and therefore, the sponsor’s “reasonably expected household income” for 2007 is given the greatest evidentiary weight in this case. The Affidavit of Support, filed in 2007, indicates that the current total annual household income is $32,172.10, including the respondent’s income (Respondent’s Application for Adjustment of Status at Attachment 10). The regulations and the instructions to the Form I-864 specifically acknowledge situations where a sponsored alien’s own income may be counted towards meeting the income requirements of the Affidavit of Support. The Immigration Judge did not consider whether the respondent’s income could be included in the sponsor’s household income under the regulations. We find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further fact finding with regard to the Affidavit of Support and to determine whether the respondent is otherwise statutorily eligible for, as well as deserving of, adjustment of status. We note that if more than 1 year has elapsed since the submission of the Form I-864 the regulations provide the Immigration Judge with the discretion to request more current information if necessary to the proper adjudication of the case. 8 C.F.R. § 213a.2(a)(1)(v)(B).
 
*3 Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained in part.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Although the Immigration Judge also denied voluntary departure to the respondent in the exercise of discretion, the respondent’s appeal brief states that he is not appealing that particular denial.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAUL MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX5 286 - Laredo, TX
June 21, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pascual Madrigal, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Justin Adams
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision of March 10, 2010, denying his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a) (the “Act”). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493, 497 (BIA 2008). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); see also Matter of Almanza-Arenas, 24 I&N Dec. 771, 775 (BIA 2009).
 
The Immigration Judge first determined that the respondent had not met his burden of proving statutory eligibility for cancellation of removal, given his inconclusive proof as to the date he was admitted as a temporary permanent resident (I.J. at 3-5). The Immigration Judge next made the discretionary determination that, after weighing the respondent’s long list of arrests and convictions against his positive equities, the respondent failed to prove his continued desirability as a resident of the United States (I.J. at 5-8).
 
The respondent first argues that he demonstrated statutory eligibility for the form of relief requested, given that no offense occurred within 7 years of his admission on May 4, 1988. The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge erred in considering the respondent’s arrest for robbery as a negative discretionary factor because no records were available to show the disposition of the case. Finally, the respondent argues that his attorney’s failure to advise him of the need to have an I-130 petition filed by his common law wife after divorcing his present wife constituted ineffective assistance of counsel. We will address each argument in turn.
 
Even assuming that the respondent satisfied the three statutory prerequisites for relief under section 240A(a) of the Act, he also must also establish that he warrants such relief as a matter of discretion. Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998). The respondent has not done so. Rather, the Immigration Judge properly balanced the negative factors and positive equities in determining that the respondent did not merit a grant of relief in the exercise of discretion. The Immigration Judge considered, inter alia, the respondent’s long residence in the United States and work history, as well as his girlfriend’s serious medical condition, but found that his criminal record, including convictions of aggravated assault causing serious bodily injury and possession of cocaine, outweighed these factors (I.J. at 6-7; Tr. at 45-49). Although the respondent assigns error to the consideration of his arrest for robbery, this is only one among a list of offenses that weigh against granting cancellation of removal. The respondent presented no evidence here that effectively offset unfavorable factors. Therefore, we agree that the respondent has failed to demonstrate his continued desirability as a resident of the United States.
 
*2 Lastly, the respondent’s allegation regarding deficient advice has not established a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel, as he has failed to comply with the standards set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2010 WL 2601539 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CLENIO ACEVEDO ABREU A.K.A. CLENIO ACEVEDO
File: AXXX XX6 498 - Los Fresnos, TX
June 14, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jason Goodchild
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent, a native of the Dominican Republic and a lawful permanent resident of the United States since October 5, 1995, has filed a timely appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 16, 2010. In that decision, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable, as charged, on account of his 2007 New York conviction for the offense of assault in the second degree,¿FN1º which he found to be a conviction for a “crime of violence” aggravated felony, as defined under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(F) (Exh. 2). The appeal will be dismissed. The respondent’s request to proceed on appeal in forma pauperis is granted under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). See Matter of Chicas, 19 I&N Dec. 114 (BIA 1984).
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2010); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, judgment, and all other issues in an appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (2010); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008) rev’d on other grounds, Kaplun v. Attorney General of the United States, 602 F.3d 260 (3d Cir. 2010); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The principal issue raised by the respondent on appeal concerns the Immigration Judge’s finding of removability. Upon our de novo review, however, we are not persuaded to disturb the Immigration Judge conclusion that the respondent’s 2007 New York conviction for the offense of assault in the second degree qualifies as a “crime of violence” under 18 U.S.C. § 16,¿FN2º and an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, and that there was no basis to grant the respondent’s request to terminate proceedings. We agree with the Immigration Judge and find that the respondent’s conviction is a crime of violence under 18 U.S.C. § 16(b).
 
*2 To determine whether an offense is a crime of violence under 18 U.S.C. § 16(b), the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case arises, employs a “categorical approach,” which looks to the generic elements of the statutory offense and asks whether the criminal conduct required to violate the applicable statute is “by its nature” a crime of violence. See, e.g., Larin-Ulloa v. Gonzales, 462 F.3d 456, 463-65 (5th Cir. 2006). In other words, the pertinent question is whether the crime inherently involves a substantial risk that intentional physical force will be used in the commission of the crime.¿FN3º See Leocal v. Ashcroft, supra, at 383 (holding that 18 U.S.C § 16(b) “covers offenses that naturally involve a person acting in disregard of the risk that physical force might be used against another in committing an offense”); see also Larin-Ulloa v. Gonzales, supra, at 465. Before we may consider whether the respondent’s conviction so qualifies, we must first examine the statutory elements of New York’s assault in the second degree statute.
 
However, before we do so, we note that NY PENAL LAW § 120.05 is a divisible statute - that is, it encompasses offenses that both are and are not crimes of violence. See NY PENAL LAW § 120.05. See generally Larin-Ulloa v. Gonzales, supra, at 463-65; see also Shepard v. United States, 544 U.S. 13, 20-21 (2005). Divisible statutes require what is referred to as a “modified categorical approach,” in which we must first look to the record of conviction to determine whether the actual offense of which the alien was convicted qualifies as a crime of violence. See Larin-Ulloa v. Gonzales, supra. In making this determination, we may consider any of the following documents: the charging document, written plea agreement, transcript of plea colloquy, and any explicit factual finding by the trial judge to which the defendant assented. See id; see also Shepard v. United States, supra.
 
The respondent’s record of conviction reflects that he was convicted of violating subsection 2 of NY PENAL LAW § 120.05. This particular subsection provides that a person is guilty of assault in the second degree when “¿wºith intent to cause physical injury to another person, he causes such injury to such person or to a third person by means of a deadly weapon or a dangerous instrument” (emphasis added). NY PENAL LAW § 120.05(2). Under New York law, this offense is classified as a class D felony. See NY PENAL LAW § 60.05(5). As such, at the time of the respondent’s conviction, assault in the second degree was punishable by a maximum term of 7 years imprisonment. See NY PENAL LAW § 70.00(2)(d). Because the respondent’s crime was punishable by a maximum term of imprisonment of more than 1 year, it also qualifies as a federal felony. See 18 U.S.C. § 3559(a)(4). Thus, his offense is unquestionably a “felony” within the meaning of 18 U.S.C. § 16(b). United States v. Villegas-Hernandez, 468 F.3d 874, 884 (5th Cir. 2006).
 
*3 Moreover, we find that the crime for which the respondent was convicted involves a substantial risk that physical force against the person or property or another may be used because the elements of intent and actual physical injury are both present. Cf. United States v. Calderon-Pena, 383 F.3d 254, 260-61 (5th Cir. 2004) (contrasting crimes that actually involve the use or attempted use of physical force against the person of another with crimes, like child endangerment, that only create a risk of injury). See generally Larin-Ulloa v. Gonzales, supra.
 
The Fifth Circuit has ruled that, in considering whether an offense is a crime of violence, it is appropriate to determine the “ordinary, usual way the crime is committed,” not whether it is “possible” to commit the offense without the use of physical force. Perez-Munoz v. Keisler, 507 F.3d 357, 364 (5th Cir. 2007) (“Being able to imagine unusual ways the crime could be committed without the use of physical force does not prevent it from qualifying as a crime of violence under § 16(b).”) Although the offense may be committed without physical force actually being used against the person of another, it is evident from the terms of the statute that, in the ordinary case, an individual who commits such an offense necessarily disregards the substantial risk that, in the course of committing it, he might be required to intentionally use physical force against his victims. See James v. United States, 127 S. Ct. 1586 (2007). Therefore the offense, which is a felony under New York law, qualifies as a crime of violence under 18 U.S.C. § 16(b), and an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, rendering the removable on that basis. See Leocal v. Ashcroft, supra.
 
Finally, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s appellate contentions that he was denied constitutional due process, first by the Immigration Judge, and secondly, by his former counsel, based on allegations of ineffective assistance in the presentation of his case.
 
Initially, we find no support for the respondent’s claim that he was denied constitutional due process by the Immigration Judge. We have held an alien must demonstrate that he has been prejudiced by a violation of a procedural rule or regulation before his deportation proceeding will be invalidated. Matter of Garcia-Flores, 17 I&N Dec. 325, 329 (BIA 1980). This is consistent with the rule that an alien must have been prejudiced by a procedural defect in his deportation proceeding before he will be found to have suffered a denial of due process. See Ka Fung Chan v. INS, 634 F.2d 248, 258 (5th Cir. 1981). In the matter before us, no such prejudice has been shown. The respondent’s appellate contentions as to “entrapment” and the giving of wrong information by the Immigration Judge are belied by the record. Moreover, the record reflects that the respondent, represented by counsel, had ample opportunity to prepare for and address the removal charges. Finally, to the extent the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge “never allowed ¿himº to excise (sic) ¿hisº rights to the mitigate the facts of ¿hisº case,” ignores the fact that having been convicted of an aggravated felony offense, the respondent is statutorily precluded from establishing his eligibility for almost all forms of relief from removal, including cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(3), such that there are no mitigating circumstances for the Immigration Judge to consider.
 
*4 The respondent’s arguments in the nature of collateral attacks on his conviction are not properly before us for consideration, as neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board can entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction unless that judgment is void on its face, and cannot go behind the judicial record to determine the guilt or innocence of the respondent. Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996); Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999). The respondent has presented no new evidence to indicate that his 2007 New York conviction for the offense of assault in the second degree, has been overturned, vacated, or expunged; rather, the evidence merely indicates that the respondent’s post judgment motion has been scheduled before a judge.¿FN4º Furthermore, there is no objective evidence in the record to indicate that the respondent was misadvised regarding the potential immigration consequences of his guilty plea. See Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).
 
The respondent has also raised a claim of denial of due process premised on his allegations of ineffective assistance by his former attorney’s presentation of his case. Where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is made, the Board has found that certain criteria must be met. Specifically, the claim must be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make, counsel must be informed of the allegations against him and provided an opportunity to respond, and the respondent must indicate whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162 (5th Cir. 2006) (citing Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-498 (5 th Cir. 2000) (upholding the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada)). We note the respondent here has failed to meet any of the criteria set forth in Lozada, in order to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Nonetheless, we find that there is nothing in the record, nor has the respondent provided any evidence, to suggest that his former counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair.
 
Based on the record before us, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is subject to removal from the United States based on the respondent’s admissions and record of conviction (Exh. 2). See section 240(c)(3)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3)(A). Moreover, having expressed no fear of persecution or torture if returned to the Dominican Republic, the respondent has failed to establish his eligibility for any relief from removal, including cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a)(3) of the Act. See section 240(c)(4)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d).
 
*5 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record reflects that on March 8, 2007, the respondent was convicted in the Supreme Court of the State of New York, County of New York, of the offense of assault in the second degree, in violation of NY PENAL LAW § 120.05(2), for which he was sentenced to a term of imprisonment of 2 years (Exh.2).

2

Under 18 U.S.C. § 16, the term “crime of violence” means either “(a) an offense that has as an element the use, attempted use, or threatened use of physical force against the person or property of another, or (b) any other offense that is a felony and that, by its nature, involves a substantial risk that physical force against the person or property of another may be used in the course of committing the offense.”

3

To illustrate the intended reach of 18 U.S.C. § 16(b), in Leocal the Court explained how that section would apply to the crime of burglary. “A burglary would be covered under § 16(b) not because the offense can be committed in a generally reckless way or because someone may be injured, but because burglary, by its nature, involves a substantial risk that the burglar will use force against a victim in completing the crime.” Leocal v. Ashcroft, supra.

4

Notwithstanding, we note that in the Fifth Circuit the pendency of a direct appeal has no effect on the validity of a criminal conviction for immigration purposes. Garcia-Maldonado v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 284, 290-91 (5 th Cir. 2007); Moosa v. INS, 171 F.3d 994, 1009 (5th Cir. 1999); see also Puello v. Bureau of Citizenship and Immigration Servs., 511 F.3d 324, 332 (2d Cir. 2007); United States v. Saenz-Gomez, 472 F.3d 791, 794 (10th Cir. 2007); Montenegro v. Ashcroft, 355 F.3d 1035, 1037 (7th Cir. 2004) (per curiam). Thus, even assuming arguendo that the respondent’s 2007 New York conviction for the offense of assault in the second degree was pending on direct appeal, and not on a motion for post-conviction relief, in the jurisdiction of the Fifth Circuit, this would not prevent it from being used as the factual predicate for an order of removal or a determination of statutory ineligibility for relief from removal.


2010 WL 2601539 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2010 WL 2601508 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TASHTHED HAIDER KABIR
File: AXXX XX4 231 - Atlanta, GA
June 8, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrew P. Johnson, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On November 23, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings in which he claims to have received ineffective assistance of counsel. He contends that his former counsel failed to notify him about his immigration court hearing until the night before, when it was too late for the respondent to make the trip from New York to Atlanta. The respondent’s failure to attend his hearing led to his being ordered removed in absentia. The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a reply to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must substantially comply with the requirements set out in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1221-23 (11th Cir. 2003). In Lozada, we required: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.
 
The respondent’s motion states that he has filed a bar complaint against his former counsel and that a copy has been included with the motion as Exhibit F (Mot. at ¶ 19). The motion also states that the former attorney has been sent a copy of the bar complaint (Mot. at ¶ 22). However, the motion before us has no copy of a bar complaint and no Exhibit F. Nor is there evidence to show that the respondent’s former counsel has been advised of the allegations against him. Because the motion to reopen does not substantially comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, the motion will be denied. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 1221-23 (11th Cir. 2003).
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2601508 (BIA)

2010 WL 2601499 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DARRYL SAMBURY
File: AXXX XX6 391 - New York, NY
June 7, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bindhu Vijayan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leslie S. Evans
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on August 26, 2009, when we denied the respondent’s untimely filed motion to reopen proceedings. On November 24, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen in which he alleged ineffective assistance by his prior attorney because that attorney failed to file a timely motion to reopen after the respondent advised the attorney that he planned to marry a United States citizen. The Department of Homeland Security has filed an opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
With certain exceptions, an alien is entitled to file only one motion to reopen and the motion must be filed not later than 90 days after the final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s motion is a second motion to reopen and was filed more than 3 years after this Board’s final administrative order, which was entered on August 4, 2006. The respondent’s motion is untimely and number-barred unless it falls within an exception to the filing requirements.
 
Under the controlling law of the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, aliens raising ineffective assistance of counsel claims in a motion may have the time limits on motions equitably tolled. See Iavorski v. U.S. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 129-33 (2d Cir. 2000). “In a situation where fraud or concealment of the existence of a claim prevents an individual from timely filing, equitable tolling of a statute of limitations is permitted until the fraud or concealment is, or should have been, discovered by a reasonable person in the situation.” Id. at 134.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must substantially comply with the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that the following requirements apply: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The Second Circuit has held that an alien who has failed to comply substantially with the Lozada requirements in a motion to reopen before the Board forfeits his or her ineffective assistance of counsel claim in the court of appeals. See Jian Yun Zheng v. United States Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2d Cir. 2005).
 
*2 The respondent’s motion does not reflect that the respondent has informed his former counsel of the allegations leveled against him and that the former counsel has been given an opportunity to respond. Thus, the respondent has not met an important requirement for ineffective assistance of counsel claims. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639 (given the potential for abuse, the alien’s former counsel must be given an opportunity to present his version of events if he so chooses).
 
The respondent’s motion also does not include a detailed statement concerning the agreement the respondent entered into with his former counsel. The respondent’s affidavit states that he advised his former counsel that he planned to marry a United States citizen. However, the respondent does not set out any details about what occurred after he actually married or what his agreement was with his former counsel once the marriage took place. The respondent’s affidavit does not even specifically indicate that he was in contact with his former attorney between the time of the marriage and the end of the 90-day period during which a timely motion to reopen could have been filed.
 
Additionally, the respondent has not established prejudice. The record reflects that the visa petition filed on behalf of the respondent by his United States citizen wife was ultimately denied on May 13, 2009, because the couple did not attend their interview. Accordingly, the respondent has not shown that the actions of his former counsel actually affected the outcome of his case. The respondent’s motion, which was filed by new counsel, states that the respondent was specifically told by his former attorney not to attend his “Stokes interview” (Mot. at 3). However, the respondent’s attached affidavit does not include this allegation (Exh. B). Nor is there other evidence included with the motion to support a claim that the former attorney had a role in the denial of the visa petition.¿FN1º Without an approved visa petition, the respondent cannot adjust his status. Thus, the former attorney’s failure to file a timely motion to reopen for the purpose of seeking adjustment of status has not been shown to have resulted in prejudice.
 
Because the respondent has not substantially complied with the requirements of Lozada and has not established prejudice, his motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The statements of the respondent’s counsel are not evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89, n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980).


2010 WL 2601499 (BIA)

2010 WL 2390824 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DUANE LYN
File: AXXX XX9 636 - York, PA
May 28, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sara Elizabeth Dill, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William E. Lore
Senior Attorney
*1 The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision mailed on April 24, 2009, that denied his motion to reopen based on his removal from the United States. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) supports the decision of the Immigration Judge. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
On December 28, 2005, an Immigration Judge issued a final order in the respondent’s case. The then-detained respondent was granted a period of voluntary departure “under safeguards” until January 26, 2006, with an alternate order of removal to Jamaica. The respondent waived the right to appeal to the Board. On December 31, 2005, respondent’s prior counsel mailed the DHS the documents necessary to effectuate the respondent’s departure. As it developed, however, the respondent was unable to depart “under safeguards” because on January 10, 2006, the DHS was obliged to turn him over to Pennsylvania state authorities for criminal prosecution. On June 6, 2006, the respondent was convicted of conspiracy to possess marijuana with intent to deliver and was sentenced to imprisonment for a period of not less than two years. On September 24, 2007, upon completion of his sentence and long after his period of voluntary departure expired, the respondent was removed from the United States to Jamaica.
 
Years later, on April 21, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge. The motion sought, inter alia, to have the Immigration Judge vacate the prior order of removal so that the respondent could voluntarily depart, or seek adjustment of status. The motion revealed that the respondent either entered or sought to reenter the United States sometime before May 28, 2008.¿FN1º The Immigration Judge ultimately found that he lacked jurisdiction over the motion due to the respondent’s removal from the United States. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge properly found himself without jurisdiction over the motion. In Matter of Armendarez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008), the Board held that it lacks jurisdiction to reopen proceedings if the alien departed after those administrative proceedings were completed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d). The Board in Matter of Armendarez, supra, recognized that 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) imposed a similar departure bar rule to motions filed before Immigration Judges. The respondent argues that the Board’s interpretation of its regulations has been invalidated in certain circuit court decisions (Respondent’s Br. at 15). As the Immigration Judge correctly observed, however, I.J. at 2, jurisdiction over this case rests with the Third Circuit Court of Appeals, and “… the Third Circuit has not held that 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), or any similar regulations, are invalid.” Accordingly, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s jurisdictional ruling.
 
*2 We separately note, however, that the respondent’s motion was long since untimely filed. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent urges that the proceedings nonetheless should be reopened sua sponte. However, we find no exceptional circumstances that would warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997)(stating that “¿tºhe power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”).
 
We note that the Immigration Judge properly determined that Dada v. Mukasey, 554 U.S. 1, 128 S.Ct. 2307, 2320 (2008), did not require a different result in this case, as the Supreme Court specifically declined to address 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d), the regulation deeming a motion to reopen filed with the Board withdrawn when the alien departs the United States. Moreover, the Immigration Judge properly considered, Dada v. Mukasey, supra, concerned a situation where the alien sought to withdraw a voluntary departure request prior to its expiration. This is not the situation with the respondent.
 
The respondent’s brief argues that the departure bar rule should not apply to him because he was “physically unable to comply with his voluntary departure order” (Respondent’s Br. at 11-13, 15-17, 19). However, at the time that voluntary departure period expired on January 26, 2006, the respondent was not detained by the DHS, but by state authorities concerning a criminal charge (Respondent’s Br. at 2, Exh. H). Thus, the respondent was not prevented from voluntarily departing by the DHS. The respondent urges that he did not fail to depart “voluntarily”, citing Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87 (BIA 2007). However, this is not a case where the failure to depart was “through no fault of his … own.” Id at 94. Rather, the respondent’s inability to voluntarily depart resulted from his own criminal misconduct that lead to his conviction and two-year sentence of imprisonment.
 
When the voluntary departure period expired, the respondent (after serving time in prison for a criminal conviction) was properly removed to Jamaica based on the alternate order of removal entered by the Immigration Judge on December 28, 2005. See Respondent’s Br. at 2; Chedid v. Holder, 573 F.3d 33, 34 (1st Cir. 2009)(Immigration Judge granted voluntary departure, with an alternate order of removal, “in case Chedid failed to comply with the grant of voluntary departure by the deadline …”); Mambwe v. Holder, 572 F.3d 540, 546 (8th Cir. 2009)(voluntary departure period granted, after which the alternate order of removal would take effect).
 
*3 Although the respondent argues on appeal that he was “never given the chance to defend his case or present evidence,” Respondent’s Br. at 13, in fact he appeared before an Immigration Judge on December 28, 2005, who issued the removal order.¿FN2º
 
The respondent also wrongly claims that he was not given notice of the removal order, and his right to due process was thereby violated (Respondent’s Br. at 17-19). In fact, the Immigration Judge rendered an oral decision on December 28, 2005, and a summary of her order was provided to the respondent or his counsel.
 
The respondent’s motion and brief alternatively argue that the untimeliness of his motion should be excused because he received ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the motion did not comply with the procedural requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Further, the respondent’s filing, which is unsupported by his own affidavit and leaves various relevant dates unspecified, does not adequately show that he acted with diligence such that the motions deadline should be tolled.¿FN3º See Mahmood v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 248, 252 (3d Cir. 2005) (party in immigration proceedings who brings an untimely motion to reopen claiming that he has received ineffective assistance of counsel must show that he acted with diligence before equitable tolling will extend the filing deadline).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed because we agree with the decision of the Immigration Judge. However, even if the provisions of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) were not found applicable, his motion would be properly denied as untimely filed.
 
ORDER: The decision of the Immigration Judge is affirmed.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s motion and subsequent filings do not reflect the actual date of the respondent’s return. The respondent indicates, however, that he has been convicted for “reentry after deportation.”

2

The respondent’s brief also states or implies, incorrectly, that his removal hearing was issued in absentia (Respondent’s Br. at 13, 17, 26).

3

We know from the documents presented that the respondent returned to the United States at some point before May 28, 2008, and did not file his motion with the Immigration Judge until April 21, 2009.

2010 WL 2390803 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SOK KUAN LEI
File: AXXX XX1 487 - Los Angeles, CA
May 27, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kathleen S. Koh, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 29, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider the denial of her motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia. On appeal, the respondent argues that “¿tºhe Immigration Judge erred in finding that the respondent did not present extraordinary circumstances (medical illness and ineffective assistance of counsel) that prevented her from appearing for her master hearing.” The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
A motion to reconsider is a request that the Board or the Immigration Judge reexamine the previous decision in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked, while a motion to reopen is usually based upon new evidence or a change in factual circumstances. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 n.1 (BIA 1991). As the Immigration Judge recognized, despite being characterized as a motion to reconsider, the respondent’s motion did not specify errors of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s prior decision, based on the evidence that was previously presented to the Immigration Judge, but rather sought to present evidence, such as a doctor’s letter, evidence of an approved visa petition (Form I-130) and documentation regarding ineffective assistance of counsel, not previously submitted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2). As the respondent’s motion did not specify errors of law or fact in the Immigration Judge’s prior decision based on evidence previously presented, the motion to reconsider was properly denied by the Immigration Judge.
 
Inasmuch as the respondent sought to present evidence that was not previously submitted, the motion is properly characterized as a motion to reopen. However, the regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(4) provides that a party may file only one motion to reopen proceedings where the party is the subject of an in absentia order of removal. In this matter, the respondent previously filed a motion to reopen, which was denied by the Immigration Judge on February 19, 2009. Hence, we decline to reopen for consideration of the doctor’s letter or evidence of the approved I-130.
 
*2 Lastly, to the extent that the respondent argued in her motion to reconsider that the Immigration Judge should have permitted the respondent to comply with the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Compean, Bangaly, and, J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), given the date of its issuance, we note that on June 3, 2009, the Attorney General vacated the decision in Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, supra. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly, and J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). Even if the respondent was unaware of the issuance of Matter of Compean, Bangaly, and J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), at the time she filed her motion to reopen, the respondent should have complied with the less rigorous evidentiary requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), which required submission of (i) an affidavit setting forth the agreement between the alien and counsel and the basis for the alleged ineffective assistance claim; (ii) evidence that former counsel was informed of the allegation and was given an opportunity to respond; and (iii) evidence that a complaint was prepared for submission to the appropriate disciplinary authorities.
 
In light of the foregoing, we find that the respondent has failed to demonstrate that the Immigration Judge erred in denying her motion to reconsider. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2390803 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 2390771 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIGUEL GUTIERREZ-OCHOA
File: AXXX XX9 049 - Buffalo, NY
May 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
L. Manuel Macias, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robert P. Levy
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before the Board on June 9, 2009, when we determined that the respondent’s interlocutory appeal became moot upon the Immigration Judge’s decision of May 1, 2009, ordering the respondent removed in absentia. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 24, 2009, denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in characterizing his claim as one of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent claims that his attorney, Mr. L. Manuel Macias, previously filed an appeal with the Board regarding the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to change venue, and Mr. Macias erroneously changed his internal records to indicate that the respondent had no upcoming hearings before the Immigration Court. Mr. Macias claims that the administrative error was his fault and not the fault of the respondent, such that reopening is warranted based on exceptional circumstances, or that the case should be reopened sua sponte.
 
An order issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 240(b)(5)(C), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1).
 
As the respondent’s attorney received notice of the hearing, proper notice was provided to the respondent. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). The respondent did not establish that he failed to appear for the hearing due to “exceptional circumstances,” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act. Furthermore, the Board has held that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve the respondent of his responsibility to appear, and unless the Immigration Judge grants the motion, the respondent remains obligated to appear at the appointed date and time. Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988); Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 1985), aff’d, Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986).
 
*2 We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). In a decision dated June 3, 2009, the Attorney General determined that the Board and Immigration Judges should apply the pre-Compean standards to all pending and future motions to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, regardless of when such motions were filed. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009). Consequently, in his decision of June 24, 2009, the Immigration Judge correctly applied Lozada when he adjudicated the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
An attorney’s administrative error or his failure to advise a client that a change of venue motion had not been granted and that he must appear for the hearing is not a basis for reopening absent compliance with Lozada. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established ineffective assistance of counsel for his failure to appear. The respondent has not submitted an affidavit asserting that he failed to appear due to ineffective assistance of counsel, nor did he indicate that a disciplinary complaint had been filed against his attorney. The Immigration Judge correctly determined that reopening is not warranted in this matter, as the respondent has made no attempt to comply with the requirements set forth in Lozada.
 
Finally, the respondent requests that the Board exercise its sua sponte authority to reopen his removal proceedings. We find that the facts presented in this case do not warrant an intervention of our sua sponte authority to reopen the proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2390771 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2010 WL 2390769 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RISTO PETKOV
File: AXXX XX6 727 - Elizabeth, NJ
May 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on October 2, 2009, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and reinstated a period of 60 days’ voluntary departure. On November 3, 2009, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen. The filing of his motion to reopen within the reinstated voluntary departure period automatically terminates the prior grant of voluntary departure. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(e)(1) & (2). The previously-entered voluntary departure order will therefore be vacated. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion.
 
Applicable regulations provide that a motion to reopen “shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted and shall be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent urges in his motion that certain evidence pertaining to his father was not submitted because he was advised against it by his attorney, and that there were some deficiencies in the translation of his testimony at the hearing. The respondent, however, despite stating that he would submit evidence later to supplement his motion, has submitted no affidavits or other evidentiary material in support of his motion to reopen. Nor has he identified any particular translation errors or shown how they affected his case. Moreover, we point out that his vague claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not meet any of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), such that he has properly raised such a claim. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s October 2, 2009, order reinstating voluntary departure is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s alternate order of removal to Macedonia is now in effect.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2390769 (BIA)

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
  Order Vacated on Reconsideration by IN RE: RAMIRO A. POLANCO URENA A.K.A. POLANCO RAMIRO A.K.A. EDY RODIGUEZ, BIA, July 16, 2010
2010 WL 2390766 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMIRO A. POLANCO URENA A.K.A. POLANCO RAMIRO A.K.A. EDY RODIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX3 042 - Batavia, NY
May 21, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ana Maria Bazan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The final administrative decision was issued by the Board on April 26, 2010, and the Board subsequently granted a stay of removal. The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, has filed a timely motion to reopen and remand the proceedings for consideration of an application for adjustment of status (based on his marriage to a United States citizen) in conjunction with an application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The motion is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel, and the respondent has met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Given the respondent’s submission and the lack of opposition by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS), the motion to reopen will be granted.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2390766 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 2224504 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAURICIO ERNESTO MORALES-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX1 812 - Los Angeles, CA
May 20, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brenda C. Diaz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of Guatemala, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on September 9, 2002, after failing to appear at a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on December 29, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied it on February 1, 2010, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). We decide the issues on appeal de novo.
 
The respondent acknowledges on appeal that the Notice of Hearing was mailed to 6141 Hazelton Avenue, Van Nuys, California 91401, the address reflected on his June 18, 2002, motion to change venue. According to the respondent, his former counsel provided an incorrect address therein on his behalf. The respondent denies having received notice of his hearing.
 
The alleged failure of a counsel to provide the Immigration Court with the respondent’s correct address would appear to constitute ineffective assistance of counsel, and it perhaps would be an “exceptional circumstance” warranting reopening of these proceedings. However, the respondent must have filed his motion to reopen based on “exceptional circumstances” within 180 days of the order of removal. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). Because a request for reopening based on “exceptional circumstances” was not made within 180 days of the date of the respondent’s order of removal, it is barred from consideration. The respondent has also not satisfied any of the procedural requirements for asserting a claim for reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See De Zavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879, 883 (5th Cir. 2004); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 638 (BIA 1988).
 
We do not find that a failure of notice occurred herein. The respondent is not entitled to receive notice of his hearing on account of the failure to provide the Immigration Court with a correct mailing address. See section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(5)(B).
 
The respondent further argues on appeal that the Notice to Appear is not genuine or authentic. We have reviewed the document, and it appears on its face to contain all the indicia of reliability. It contains the factual allegations giving rise to a charge of removability, is issued and signed by an authorized individual, and it is personally served on the respondent. The respondent has not identified any aspect of the Notice to Appear that would suggest that it is not a genuine document issued by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). The DHS is not required, as argued on appeal, to establish the chain of custody of the Notice to Appear. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2224504 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ORLANDO MEDINA LOPEZ A.K.A. ORLANDO MEDINA
File: AXXX XX4 570 - York, PA
May 20, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeffrey T. Bubier
Senior Attorney
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Removability; remand
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 17, 2010, decision finding him removable as charged and ordering him removed to the Dominican Republic. The Department of Homeland Security seeks a summary affirmance. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872 (BIA 1994). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s 2001 conviction for the Attempted Criminal Sale of a Controlled Substance (cocaine) in violation of section 110-220.39 of the New York State Penal Law qualifies as an aggravated felony. Section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B); Evanson v. U.S. Att’y General, 550 F.3d 284, 288 (3d Cir. 2008).
 
The respondent seeks a remand based upon his claim that his criminal case has been reopened, and thus his drug conviction is not final for immigration purposes (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 4-6). The respondent has submitted new evidence on appeal, including a letter from the Supreme Court of New York County to support his claim. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The new evidence does not indicate that the respondent’s 2001 conviction has been overturned, vacated, or expunged; rather, the evidence merely indicates that the respondent’s post judgment motion has been scheduled before a judge. Therefore, the respondent’s reliance upon Pinho v. Gonzales, 432 F.3d 193 (3d Cir. 2005), is misplaced. The respondent also argues on appeal that his guilty plea was involuntary, and he was not advised of his constitutional rights at the time of his guilty plea (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 6); however, there is no objective evidence in the record to support the respondent’s assertions.
 
*2 The respondent’s arguments are in the nature of collateral attacks on his conviction; however, neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board can entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction unless that judgment is void on its face, and cannot go behind the judicial record to determine the guilt or innocence of the respondent. Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996); Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999).
 
The respondent also alleges ineffective assistance of counsel, yet he has not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Mudric v. U.S. Att’y General, 469 F.3d 94, 100 (3d Cir. 2006) (affirming the Board’s denial of a motion to reopen for failure to comply with the procedural requirements of Lozada). Furthermore, there is no objective evidence in the record to indicate that the respondent was misadvised regarding the potential immigration consequences of his guilty plea. See Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010). Accordingly, based upon the evidence in the record, we conclude that the respondent’s conviction is final for immigration purposes and a remand is not warranted.¿FN1º 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). In view of the foregoing, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant

We deny the respondent’s April 5, 2010, motion and note that the record contains all of the transcripts pertaining to the respondent. Additionally, in his April 12, 2010, appeal brief the respondent does not assert that the transcripts are missing or unavailable or that he was unable to prepare his appeal brief due to missing transcripts.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RICARDO PEREZ-MORALES
File: AXXX XX5 768 - Eloy, AZ
May 19, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Murray Hilts, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alec J. Niziolek
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; waiver under former section 212(c) of the Act
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s February 18, 2010, decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings to apply for relief pursuant to former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), in light of the Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). The Department of Homeland Security seeks a summary affirmance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872 (BIA 1994). Although the respondent concedes that his motion to reopen is untimely, he claims that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled based upon ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 18). The respondent, however, has not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and the record does not show that his ineffective assistance claim is legitimate. See Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582, 588 (9th Cir. 2006).
 
The respondent also contends on appeal that he is eligible for equitable tolling because the Immigration Judge who presided over his 1998 hearing violated due process by failing to advise the respondent of his eligibility for a 212(c) waiver. He also asserts that his appeal waiver was not knowing and voluntary, and a change in the law has resulted in more than a reasonable likelihood that the respondent is eligible for 212(c) relief.
 
We disagree with the respondent’s contentions. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001); Valeriano v. Gonzales, 474 F.3d 669, 673 (9th Cir. 2007) (alien was not entitled to equitable tolling of limitations period for filing motion to reopen removal proceedings). The Immigration Judge in 1998 applied the law then applicable, which did not extend section 212(c) relief to the respondent. At the time of the hearing, section 212(c) had been repealed by the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, and the Supreme Court had not yet issued INS v. St. Cyr, supra. Accordingly, the respondent’s reliance upon United States v. Lopez-Velasquez, 568 F.3d 1139 (9th Cir. 2009), is misplaced and given the respondent’s circumstances and the state of the law in 1998, we do not agree with the respondent’s assertion that the Immigration Judge should have advised the respondent that he might be eligible for section 212(c) relief. Valencia v. Mukasey, 548 F.3d 1261 (9th Cir. 2008) (alien lacking apparent eligibility for relief from removal did not have due process right to notice of such relief).
 
*2 The record also does not support the respondent’s claim that his appeal waiver was not knowing, intelligent, and voluntary. The record contains a bi-lingual “Request for Prompt Removal,” signed by the respondent. The respondent agreed that he would waive appeal of the decision ordering his removal, he would accept the order of removal as final, and that he entered the request “voluntarily, knowingly, and intelligently.”
 
The respondent has not established that he exercised due diligence in discovering essential information bearing on his claim. The record reflects that although the respondent’s wife contacted attorneys in 1998, 2008, and 2009, there is no evidence to indicate that the respondent or his wife took any actions to resolve the respondent’s immigration status between 1999 and 2007 (see Declarations of Ricardo Perez-Morales and Christina Perez in Support of Defendant’s Motion to Dismiss Indictment). Valeriano v. Gonzales, supra, at 673 (for equitable tolling to apply, it is necessary that despite all due diligence, the party invoking equitable tolling is unable to obtain vital information bearing on the existence of the claim, and the party’s ignorance of the necessary information must have been caused by circumstances beyond the party’s control).
 
The respondent did not file his motion to reopen until November 2009, which is 8 years after the publication of INS v. St. Cyr, 4 years after the statutory deadline for filing a special motion pursuant to 8 C.F.R § 1003.44(h), and 2 years after the publication of Reynoso-Cisneros v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1001, 1002 (9th Cir. 2007) (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 19-20). See also Lin v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 979 (9th Cir.2007) (regulation did not preclude jurisdiction over motion to reopen filed by alien who had been lawfully removed after the completion of immigration proceedings).
 
In addition, the record does not contain objective and reliable evidence to establish that the respondent pled guilty in 1994 to possession of cocaine base for sale, nor has the respondent demonstrated that he relied on section 212(c) relief at the time of his 1994 conviction. Thus, there is no basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is not eligible for a 212(c) waiver or any other relief, and the respondent has failed to show a due process violation.
 
The record reflects that the respondent illegally reentered the United States on multiple occasions. Therefore, he is ineligible for reopening pursuant to 8 C.F.R. 1003.44(k)(2). Avila-Sanchez v. Mukasey, 509 F.3d 1037 (9th Cir. 2007) (provisions of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(k)(2) are constitutional). Consistent with the Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, supra, and the section 212(c) regulation found at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(a), the respondent is precluded from establishing his eligibility for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act. Given that the respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for the relief sought, reopening is not warranted. Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 482 (BIA 1996). In view of the foregoing, the following order shall be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2224519 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: ISSA ELIAS HANNA MURCUS
File: AXXX XX7 189 - Hartford, CT
May 12, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rashmi N. Patel, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Israel, moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) to reopen proceedings in which we issued our final order on August 13, 2009. We dismissed the appeal of the February 12, 2008, Immigration Judge order denying a continuance and voluntary departure. The motion requests reopening to apply for adjustment of status. However, as noted during the Immigration Judge hearing and in our decision on appeal, the petition filed by the respondent’s spouse was denied by the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). In the instant motion, the respondent now argues that the attorney who represented him during the hearing, Sharyn D’Urso, provided ineffective assistance in not filing an appeal with the USCIS. However, the motion does not contain the information that might have warranted an appeal with the USCIS. No evidence of the bona fides of the respondent’s marriage has been provided aside from a self serving affidavit from the respondent (Motion at Tab A).
 
Furthermore, insofar as the motion argues that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance, the motion also fails to comply with the procedural requirements to establish such a claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). There is a letter to the prior attorney, but no complaint has been filed with the bar authority (see Motion at Tab B). The only explanation for failing to file the bar complaint is that the respondent feared there would be a negative impact on the immigration case. But as the respondent is now represented by current counsel, who also represented him during the immigration appeal process, this explanation is dubious. The respondent has not established that he suffered prejudice as the result of any ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000). The respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for relief warranting reopening. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
