2010 WL 1284455 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TABUGBO THEODORE ADOGU
File: AXXX XX5 272 - Baltimore, MD
March 16, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Karla S. Baker
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on March 22, 2005, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings following an in absentia order of removal dated April 14, 2004. On July 28, 2009, the respondent flied a motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed an opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
With certain exceptions, an alien is entitled to file only one motion to reopen and the motion must be filed not later than 90 days after the final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s current motion is a second motion to reopen and it was flied with the Board more than 5 years after the final administrative order of removal was entered.
 
The record establishes that neither the respondent, nor his counsel appeared at the respondent’s April 14, 2004, hearing. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia. A motion to reopen and a Notice of Appearance (Form EOIR-28) was filed with the Immigration Judge on June 16, 2004, by Attorney Patrick Tachie-Menson. The motion claimed that the respondent was ill on the day of the hearing, that he had terminated services with his prior attorney and had not yet retained Attorney Tachie-Menson, and that he did not know how to notify the Immigration Court about his illness. Attached to the motion was letter from a doctor dated May 18, 2004. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, and this Board affirmed that decision on March 22, 2005.
 
The respondent’s current motion to reopen was filed more than 4 years later. The respondent now claims that he had retained the services of an attorney, Lloyd Ukwu¿FN1º prior to his hearing, that Mr. Ukwu knew about the respondent’s back problems and said he would appear in Immigration Court on behalf of the respondent, that the attorney did not appear, and that the respondent only discovered this fact after he had been ordered removed in absentia. The respondent states that he received a document from the Immigration Court, took it to Mr. Ukwu for explanation, and was told he had been ordered deported and would have to file a motion to reopen. The respondent states that he was later visited by Mr. Ukwu and Mr. Tachie-Menson, and that he was told to write a letter, based on what was dictated to him, and to sign it. The respondent reports that on June 11, 2004, Mr. Ukwu contacted him again about an urgent need to come to his office, pick up a letter, and have it notarized. The respondent states that he did what he was told, even though he knew it was not in his best interest.
 
*2 The respondent reports that after the June 11, 2004, incident he never again heard from Mr. Ukwu. The respondent states that he called the attorney’s office and also visited there, but was told Mr. Ukwu was out of the country. Later, the respondent’s work permit expired and he sought advice from other attorneys. Based on this advise, the respondent filed a complaint against Mr. Ukwu with the District of Columbia Bar on February 22, 2007. On April 30, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, but it was rejected due to lack of jurisdiction. On July 28, 2009, the respondent filed the motion that is before us now. In his motion he claims that Mr. Ukwu provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent’s motion was filed many years after the final administrative order of removal and is untimely. Further, the respondent does not benefit from equitable tolling because he has not demonstrated, or even alleged, that he exercised due diligence in seeking reopening. See Chao v. Virginia Department of Transportation, 291 F.3d 276, 283 (4th Cir. 2002) (equitable tolling is not available when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf). The respondent was ordered removed on April 14, 2004. He last heard from Mr. Ukwu, the person he claims provided the ineffective assistance, in June 2004. He states that he knew that the documents filed with the Immigration Judge in June 2004, were not in his interest. He did not attempt to seek reopening until 2009. Notably, the respondent filed his complaint with the District of Columbia Bar at the beginning of 2007, more than 2 years before his motion to reopen was filed with the Board. The respondent provides no explanation for the significant delay. Thus, due diligence has not been established in this case, and equitable tolling does not apply.
 
Additionally, we agree with the DHS that the respondent has failed to submit a statement addressing the scope of his agreement with Mr. Ukwu, a requirement for ineffective assistance claims. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. He has also not made any ineffective assistance allegations against the attorney who was actually his attorney of record in the Immigration Court, Mr. Tachie-Menson. The respondent has not supported his motion with a signed or sworn statement, as required by Lozada, and he has also admitted to signing a statement, attached to his original motion, that contained false allegations.
 
For these reasons, the respondent’s untimely filed motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On March 2, 2010, this Board issued a final order suspending Mr. Ukwu for 5 years based on his disbarment in the District of Columbia.

2010 WL 1284442 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROSA MARY ABREU-PAEZ
File: AXXX XX9 019 - New York, NY
March 12, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose Alexis Rojas, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening, reconsideration
 
On April 25, 2008, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen her proceedings. And on May 16, 2008, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider the denial of the motion to reopen. The respondent has appealed from both decisions. The appeal will be dismissed.
  
I. BACKGROUND
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic. On appeal, she argues that the Immigration Judge erred in not reopening her proceeding. She claims that she has shown that she is eligible to adjust her status on the basis of a visa petition filed by her United States citizen husband and that she is prima facie eligible for adjustment under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i).
 
In addition, the respondent maintains that her failure to departure voluntarily or to file a timely motion was not willful. She argues that she did not depart because the extension of section 245(i) of the Act made her eligible for relief. And she claims that she did not file a timely motion to reopen because she was waiting for her priority date to become current or for her husband to become a United States citizen.
 
Finally, the respondent appears to allege that she was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. And she claims that she should not be prejudiced by her former attorneys’ failure to file motions to reopen.
  
II. ANALYSIS
 
As the Immigration Judge correctly noted, the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). And the respondent has not established that she is entitled to one of the exceptions to the time limits on motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4).
 
In addition, the respondent has not established that she is prima facie eligible for the relief she is seeking, despite her claims. See, e.g., Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996) (discussing proof required for reopening to seek newly available relief). The respondent is statutorily barred from obtaining adjustment of status for another year because she failed to depart voluntarily as required on or before February 5, 2001. See section 240B(d)(1)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d)(1)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(a). The respondent therefore is not entitled to reopening to pursue adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act even if she meets the other requirements for adjustment.
 
*2 The respondent claims on appeal that her failure to depart or to file a timely motion was not willful, but she has not established that her failure to depart was involuntary. Cf. Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87, 93-94 (BIA 2007).¿FN1º And there is no “exceptional circumstances” exception to section 240B(d)(1)(B) of the Act. Id. Moreover, the record shows that the respondent received the proper advisals regarding the consequences of failing to depart voluntarily. See Immigration Judge’s Decision dated October 6, 2000.
 
Finally, while the respondent appears to allege that she was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the Immigration Judge correctly noted that the respondent has not met the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We therefore cannot conclude that the respondent is entitled to any remedy due to the possible conduct or misconduct of one of her attorneys. See Matter of Lozada, supra; see also Garcia-Martinez v. Department of Homeland Security, 448 F.3d 511, 513-14 (2d Cir. 2006) (indicating that an alien who fails to comply substantially with the Lozada requirements forfeits his or her ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
Based on the foregoing, we find that the Immigration Judge acted properly in denying both the respondent’s motion to reopen and her motion to reconsider the denial of her motion to reopen. We therefore uphold the Immigration Judge’s decisions and we dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s justification for filing her motion many years after the date upon which she was required to depart voluntarily also is not sufficient to justify sua sponte reopening. See, e.g., Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (indicating that sua sponte reopening is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or to circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship).

2010 WL 1250954 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAVIER BALLONA-AMBROCIO
File: AXXX XX0 819 - Memphis, TN
March 10, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sean Lewis, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William A. Lund
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On August 28, 2009, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen these proceedings based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent asserts that his prior attorneys failed to inform him that he could file a motion to reopen these proceedings once his immediate relative visa petition was approved by the DHS’s United States Citizenship and Immigration Services. The respondent explains that he would have sought reopening to apply for voluntary departure, and would have voluntarily departed and applied for an immigrant visa in Mexico. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
First, the respondent has not met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We do not find the respondent’s explanation for failing to comply with these requirements to be adequate. Further, the respondent has neither demonstrated that prior counsels’ representation was ineffective or that prejudice resulted from his former counsels’ performance. Id. The respondent is ineligible for adjustment of status under section 245(a) because he was not inspected and admitted into the United States, and he does not assert or establish that he is eligible for adjustment under section 245(i). Moreover, the respondent is ineligible for voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b), because he was not physically present in the United States for a 1-year period immediately preceding the date the Notice to Appear was served (I.J. at 3; Exh. 1). Thus, the respondent has failed to establish that his prior attorneys’ conduct prejudiced his case. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 1250954 (BIA)

2010 WL 1250960 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LIDIA LIRIANO LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX1 741 - Boston, MA
March 9, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ivan Crnilovic, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a female native and citizen of the Dominican Republic who has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision mailed on January 8, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia on March 4, 2008. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
Upon de novo review we find that the respondent has not established that proceedings should be reopened. The respondent claims in her motion to reopen that she and her former attorney of record, Paul A. Farina, Esquire, believed the hearing date was scheduled for March 18, 2008. The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent received proper notice since she and Mr. Farina attended a hearing on October 9, 2007, when Mr. Farina was provided with personal service of the hearing notice listing the correct date and time of the hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (providing that whenever a person is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to the attorney or representative of record, or to the person himself if unrepresented). The respondent does not contest the Immigration Judge’s determination regarding a lack of notice, therefore this issue is waived.
 
We turn now to the respondent’s argument raised for the first time on appeal that her former attorney was ineffective for providing the respondent with the wrong hearing date. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and if the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A. G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”).
 
*2 The respondent has submitted a new affidavit on appeal stating that Mr. Farina told her the wrong hearing date. However, the respondent does not state that she informed Mr. Farina of the ineffective assistance claim that she has filed with the Board. Prior counsel has therefore been deprived of an opportunity to respond to the complaint before us. Thus, the Board is unable to consider the position of prior counsel to the allegations filed against him. Moreover, the respondent has failed to detail the agreement that was entered into with Mr. Farina with respect to the actions to be taken in this case and she does not state whether she has sought to file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities and if not, why not. We therefore find that the respondent has failed to establish exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear since she has failed to comply with Lozada. See Beltre-Veloz v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 7, 10 (1st Cir. 2008) (finding that an alien’s affidavit that “makes no mention of the nature, scope, or substance of the ¿ alien’sº arrangement with ¿his attorneyº, ¿and fails toº indicate what communications the ¿alienº had with the attorney” contains a“fatal flaw” under the Lozada requirements).
 
The respondent also contends on appeal that her motion warrants reopening as a matter of discretion because she may qualify for cancellation of removal based upon her three United States citizen children or as a battered spouse. See section 240A(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). Attached to the respondent’s brief on appeal, she submitted documentation apparently related to a battered spouse claim. See 240A(b)(2)(A) of the Act. To the extent that the respondent seeks reopening and remand based upon this evidence, her speculation that she may seek relief does not warrant reopening sua sponte, nor does it support a remand to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of this issue. We therefore find no reason to disturb the decision of the Immigration Judge.
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 1250960 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 1250933 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SEGUNDO FRANCISCO CAGUANA
File: AXXX XX9 479 - Buffalo, NY
March 5, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Shahla Khan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on January 16, 2007. On February 21, 2008, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s denial of a motion he filed seeking reopening of that decision. Therein we found the respondent had failed to overcome the presumption of proper delivery of his hearing notice, and did not demonstrate that reopening was appropriate. On July 29, 2009, the respondent submitted a second motion to reopen, now alleging that he received ineffective assistance from two former representatives, and asking that proceedings be reopened. The motion will be denied.
 
We initially observe that to the extent that the respondent avers that this Board erred in our prior decision with respect to the admission and consideration of a letter from Victor Salazar, these arguments are in the nature of a motion to reconsider (Motion at 5). See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). As it was filed more than 30 days after the Board’s decision, it is untimely, and we shall consider this aspect of his motion no further. Section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b).
 
The respondent now alleges that two persons whom he reportedly retained in the course of his proceedings provided him with ineffective assistance such that reopening is warranted. Specifically, he avers that following his release from DHS detention he met with and hired Victor Salazar of the Jackson Height Rafaelli Law Offices (Motion at 3-4). He states that Mr. Salazar falsely held himself out as an attorney, and advised the respondent to not attend his scheduled hearing as he would otherwise be deported from the United States, and did not inform him of the consequences of such action. He further contends that after he learned in April of 2007 of the removal order resulting from his failure to appear, he retained Jonathan Saint-Preux, Esquire, to file a motion to reopen, claiming that he failed to appear at his heating due to exceptional circumstances.¿FN1º However, he claims that Mr. Saint-Preux did not investigate the facts regarding a letter from Mr. Salazar which related to his claim of lack of knowledge of his hearing date or of Mr. Salazar’s direction that he not appear at his hearing, nor did he raise a claim of ineffective assistance against Mr. Salazar (Motion at 4). After Mr. Saint-Preux was suspended from practicing law, the respondent hired Cindy Paul, Esquire, to represent him on appeal before the Board, and subsequently hired current counsel to file a petition for review of the Board’s 2008 decision before the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit (the petition was ultimately denied).
 
*2 In his motion the respondent also repeats his assertion that he never received personal service of his hearing notice (Motion at 3, 13 (ftnt. 7)). However, the Board previously considered this allegation and found no merit to the same. The respondent has raised no new factual allegations or legal arguments regarding the service of the hearing notice to the address he himself provided, and which he continues to assert was his address during the relevant time period. This issue has been fully litigated, including in a petition before the Second Circuit. Therefore, we decline to revisit these arguments, and shall address them no further.
 
The respondent essentially avers that Mr. Salazar falsely presented himself as an attorney and told him not to appear at his hearing as he would be deported, advising him to await the possibility of future relief. He also contends that Mr. Saint-Preux, whom he retained to reopen absentia proceedings, erred by not investigating the circumstances regarding a letter from the non-attorney sent to the Immigration Court, stating that the respondent could not be contacted, had relocated, and would not be appearing at his hearing. We recognize that the time and number bars on motions to reopen may be equitably tolled to accommodate claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, so long as the movant has exercised “due diligence” in vindicating his rights. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 171 (2d Cir. 2006’).
 
We find that the respondent has not exercised due diligence in this regard. In conjunction with the 2007 motion to reopen filed by Mr. Saint-Preux, the respondent submitted a sworn affidavit which he signed under penalty of perjury. Therein the respondent stated that he was “unaware that ¿heº had a court ¿hearingº date” because his “previous attorney did not notify me that my presence was requested by the courts of Immigration.” These attestations are inconsistent with his new claim that he was, in fact, told of his hearing date by Mr. Salazar. He has not alleged that he informed Mr. Saint-Preux of Mr. Salazar’s purported actions, despite the fact that Mr. Salazar’s letter was submitted with the motion and referenced therein (2007 Motion at 2, attachment B). As such, we are not convinced that Mr. Saint-Preux committed cognizable error as alleged.¿FN2º
 
With the above-noted motion, the respondent also submitted a signed and sworn “Client Verification” stating that the attestations in the motion were true. Thus, by his own statements, the respondent was aware of the contents of his motion, and therefore knew that it failed to include any mention of the actions that he now alleges Mr. Salazar committed. Moreover, in our February 21, 2008, decision the Board noted that the respondent had failed to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Mr. Salazar (Board Dec. at 2). Therefore, at the latest the respondent was aware of the deficiency in his motion as of that date. He has not averred that Ms. Paul, who was then representing him, did not inform him of the Board’s decision, and/or provide him with a copy of the same. Thus, as the respondent waitcd almost a year and a half to file the instant motion since he was, or should have been, aware of the failure of Mr. Saint-Preux and/or Ms. Paul to raise his new allegations of error against Mr. Salazar, he did not exercise due diligence in pursuing his claim, id.
 
*3 In closing, we observe that the errors alleged of Mr. Saint-Preux, of failing to thoroughly investigate the respondent’s relationship with Mr. Salazar and the issue of the noted letter, are equally true of Ms. Paul, yet no claim of ineffective assistance has been prosecuted against either of her. Similarly, although respondent’s present counsel, Ms. Khan, has been representing him since at least March 21, 2008, the instant motion was not filed until more than a year later, and only after the Second Circuit denied the respondent’s petition for review (Motion at 2). This, again, is reflective of a lack of due diligence on the respondent’s part, and serves to further undermine his argument for reopening.
 
In sum, the respondent’s motion is not exempt from the number bar on motions to reopen. Therefore, reopening is not warranted. Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In the April 16, 2007, motion the respondent claimed that he did not receive any notice from either the Immigration Court or Mr. Salazar of his scheduled hearing.

2

Moreover, the respondent has by his own account made no attempt to contact Mr. Saint-Preux and notify him of the allegations made against him. The fact that Mr. Saint-Preux is no longer authorized to practice law does not absolve the respondent of his responsibility to provide his former counsel of an opportunity to respond to his assertions of error. As such, the respondent has not complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel with regard to Mr. Saint-Preux as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637(BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1 st Cir. 1988).

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 On December 14, 2009, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal as untimely. The respondent submitted a timely motion to reconsider on December 28, 2009. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The pending motion does not cause the Board to reconsider its prior decision in this case. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006)(a motion to reconsider must allege a material factual or legal error, assert that the Board erred in affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision without opinion, or argue a change in law).
 
The respondent claims that his former attorney agreed to file an appeal but failed to do so. First, the respondent does not explain why the untimely appeal was submitted almost 3 months past the June 22, 2009, due date, other than to vaguely state that he “had no knowledge of the fraud committed for about two months.” Given the respondent’s lack of diligence, we will not consider this appeal under our sua sponte authority. Matter of Liadov, 23 I&N Dec. 990 (BIA 2006)(Board has authority to consider a late appeal by certification under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (c), under appropriate circumstances).
 
Moreover, contrary to his arguments, the respondent fails to show compliance with Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, including that counsel be notified of the charges against him. Additionally, the complaint submitted to the state bar makes no claim about counsel agreeing to submit an appeal with the Board. Sako v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 857, 863 (6th Cir. 2006).
 
To the extent that the respondent seeks reopening of the Immigration Judge’s decision finding him subject to removal, and his argument that he is eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), we lack jurisdiction over such motion, as this Board never had jurisdiction over an appeal in this case. Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974). The pending motion is, therefore, denied.
 
David B. Holmes

2010 WL 915587 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GURJIT SINGH SINGH
File: AXXX XX1 697 - Chicago, IL
March 2, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Arvin G. Amatorio, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anastasie M. Senat
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), will be denied.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of India. On May 3, 2006, the Immigration Judge granted him the privilege of voluntary departure until September 3, 2006.¿FN1º The Immigration Judge denied a motion to reopen seeking adjustment of status on September 19, 2006. The Board affirmed this decision on May 3, 2007, agreeing with the Immigration Judge that the motion to reopen was untimely filed, and failed to meet the requirements for reopening under Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 20.02).
 
Over two years later, on July 27, 2009, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen with the Board. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The motion brings a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, blaming the attorneys who earlier represented him before the Immigration Judge, and Board, for his motion to reopen being denied.
 
Concerning untimely motions filed in immigration proceedings, the Seventh Circuit Court of Appeals has stated that “attorney negligence is not, without more, a basis to toll a statute of limitations.” Munoz De Real v. Holder,__ F.3d__, 2010 455404 (7th Cir. Feb. 11, 2010). The Seventh Circuit also held in Johnson v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 795, 799 (7th Cir. 2007) that “¿iºt is a rare occasion that we reverse a decision on the basis that a time limit should have been equitably tolled… it must be shown that the claimant could not have been expected to file earlier.” The respondent claiming equitable tolling of a filing deadline must show that he has acted with due diligence. Id.; Patel v. Gonzales, 442 F.3d 1011, 1016 (7th Cir. 2006); Pervaiz v. Gonzales, 405 F.3d 488,489-90 (7th Cir. 2005). The pending motion provides no reason why the motions period should be tolled.
 
Moreover, the respondent fails to show compliance with any of the requirements in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Ghaffar v. Mukasey, 551 F.3d 651,656 (7th Cir. 2008).
 
*2 Finally, reopening under the Board’s discretionary sua sponte authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) is reserved for exceptional situations, which have not been demonstrated here. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

1

The Immigration Judge’s order is incorrectly dated May 3, 2005.

2010 WL 915601 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANDRE GAREL HARRISON
File: AXXX XX9 516 - Napanoch, NY
February 26, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Laura Michalec
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on June 18, 2009, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent removable pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(C) (alien convicted of firearms or destructive device violation), and denying the respondent’s motion to terminate the proceedings. On January 22, 2010, the respondent moved the Board to reopen the proceedings based on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Iavorski v. U.S. I.N.S., 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent asserts that his former attorney failed to challenge the charge of removability and to seek relief on his behalf. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion, which is untimely, will be denied. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
In his motion, the respondent specifically argues that the crime underlying the charge of removability is not an aggravated felony. See generally section 101(a)(43) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43) (defining “aggravated felony”). We agree. See, e.g., U.S. v. Gamez, 577 F.3d 394 (2nd Cir. 2009) (finding that an alien’s prior conviction under New York law for criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree was not a conviction for a crime of violence, and therefore, not an aggravated felony). The respondent, however, was not found to be removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony. The respondent has offered no arguments or evidence that the crime rendering the respondent removable under section 237(a)(2)(C) of the Act, i.e., attempted criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree, in violation of section 110/265.03 of the New York State Penal Law, is not a firearms offense.
 
The respondent further argues that he is eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), and a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). The respondent, who plead guilty and was convicted of the crime underlying the charge of removability on June 4, 2008, is ineligible for section 212(c) relief. See INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001); Cyrus v. Keisler, 505 F.3d 197 (2nd Cir. 2007). Furthermore, the respondent, who became a lawful permanent resident on December 2, 2005, is ineligible for cancellation of removal because as the result of his June 4, 2008, conviction, he cannot establish the 5 years of lawful permanent residence required under the Act. See sections 240A(a)(2), (d)(1) of the Act.
 
*2 Finally, the respondent, who claims that his mother became a naturalized United States citizen when he was 16 years old, argues that he is now a United States citizen pursuant to section 321 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1432. See Motion to Reopen at 2. Section 321 of the Act, however, was repealed and replaced by section 320 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1431. The respondent, who was born on March 13, 1986, became a lawful permanent resident on December 2, 2005, when he was 19 years old. Therefore, he was not eligible to derive United States citizenship at the time of his parent’s naturalization. See section 320(a)(3) of the Act.
 
In sum, the respondent has not shown that he suffered prejudice in these proceedings as a result of his former attorney’s handling of the case. See Iavorski v. U.S. I.N.S., supra. Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen will be denied.¿FN1º
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent also claims that he is now married to a United States citizen. He has also submitted a copy of receipt of a visa petition application filed on his behalf on February 27, 2003, as the unmarried child of a United States citizen. The respondent, however, has neither requested reopening to apply for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255, nor complied with the regulatory requirements for reopening to apply for such relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VALERIO DOMINGO-CINTO
File: AXXX XX6 516 - Atlanta, GA
February 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carolina Colin-Antonini, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mele Moreno
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals from the October 17, 2008, decision of the Immigration Judge which denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings which were held in absentia on July 3, 2008. The appeal will be sustained.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) and (iii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent’s prior counsel admits in his affidavit submitted with the respondent’s motion that his office incorrectly advised the respondent that he was not scheduled for a hearing because the attorney failed to record the new hearing date. The respondent’s own affidavit reflects that he repeatedly contacted his prior counsel’s office regarding the scheduled hearing and was incorrectly informed that it had been rescheduled. As a result of the misinformation supplied by prior counsel, the respondent missed his hearing. The respondent was thereby prejudiced, as he claims 20 years of residence in the United States and eligibility for adjustment of status. We also note that the Department of Homeland Security filed an affirmative non-opposition to the respondent’s motion. Because the attorney admits that his office incorrectly advised the respondent, the respondent has established ineffective assistance of counsel as the basis for his failure to appear.¿FN1º Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The appeal will be sustained, and the proceedings are reopened.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, the motion is granted and the record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Even where prior counsel confesses error, the better practice would be for the respondent to report such conduct to the appropriate bar officials.


2010 WL 915626 (BIA)

2010 WL 1251043 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAGARIE JENNIFER SPEID
File: AXXX XX0 893 - Buffalo, NY
February 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William Z. Reich, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Denise C. Hochul
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility
 
*1 The respondent has appealed an Immigration Judge’s January 17, 2008, decision, finding the respondent removable as charged and ordering her removed to Canada. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has opposed the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge, including any determination of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); see also Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493, 496 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent was previously placed in removal proceedings in September 2003 by the issuance of a Notice to Appear (“NTA”), which alleged that she had been convicted of a controlled substance violation in 1985 and alleged that she failed to disclose any arrest or conviction when she applied for a visa in June 2003. On the basis of these allegations, she was charged under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A), for being inadmissible at the time of entry under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) for her drug violation, and under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) for procuring admission by willful misrepresentation of a material fact. During those removal proceedings, the respondent, through counsel, admitted to the allegations, conceded removability, and requested voluntary departure (Tr. at 21, June 1, 2004 hearing). The Immigration Judge granted the respondent 120 days to voluntarily depart in lieu of removal. The parties accepted the Immigration Judge’s decision as final and the respondent confirmed that she understood the ruling (Tr. at 23-24, June 1, 2004 hearing).
 
On December 24, 2005, upon applying for admission to the United States at the Canadian border, the respondent was once again placed in removal proceedings. The NTA contained the very same factual allegations as the previous NTA and charged her under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act as removable for having been convicted of a controlled substance violation, and under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act for engaging in fraud. Through new counsel, the respondent admitted to the drug conviction and her removability on this ground, but she denied that she made any misrepresentations in procuring her visa (Tr. at 6, Aug. 23, 2006 hearing). In this regard, the respondent’s attorney asserted that the respondent was never asked about prior arrests or convictions in applying for her visa, and argued that any concession to the contrary made by her former attorney constituted ineffective assistance of counsel.¿FN1º
 
*2 However, the Immigration Judge found that the doctrine of res judicata precluded the respondent from contesting and relitigating these issues, given the respondent’s prior admissions and concessions. The doctrine of res judicata “provides that a final judgment on the merits bars a subsequent action between the same parties over the same cause of action.” Channer v. Dep’t of Homeland Sec., 527 F.3d 275, 279 (2d Cir. 2008). Because the removal charges are not identical in both of the respondent’s proceedings, however, it is the doctrine of collateral estoppel which applies in the instant case.
 
The doctrine of collateral estoppel applies in removal proceedings when there has been a prior judgment between the parties that is sufficiently firm to be accorded conclusive effect, the parties had a full and fair opportunity to litigate the issues resolved by and necessary to the outcome of the prior judgment, and the use of collateral estoppel is not unfair. Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 1984). It precludes parties to a judgment on the merits in a prior suit from relitigating in a subsequent action issues that were actually litigated and necessary to the outcome of the prior suit. Id. Such a prior judgment conclusively establishes the ultimate facts of a subsequent removal proceeding, and bars reconsideration of issues of law resolved by the prior judgment, so long as the issues in both proceedings arise from virtually identical facts and there has been no change in the controlling law. Id. at 67.
 
The prerequisites for collateral estoppel are satisfied in the respondent’s case. The Immigration Judge’s prior removal order was a final judgment between the same parties. Both the respondent and the DHS had a “full and fair opportunity” to litigate the factual issue of whether the respondent misrepresented her criminal history when applying for her visa and the legal issue of whether the respondent was removable on that basis. Thus, it is fair to accord the judgment conclusive effect.
 
The respondent’s argument that she did not have the opportunity to litigate the issue of fraud is belied by the record. She was given a full opportunity to do so, over the course of several hearings. In fact, when the respondent’s attorney indicated that the respondent may have received a pardon of her drug conviction in Canada, the Immigration Judge continued the case to allow her to discuss with the DHS the possibility of having the fraud charge withdrawn (Tr. at 13-14, Mar. 24, 2004 hearing). Apparently, no agreement to withdraw the charge was reached, as the respondent’s attorney admitted the allegations, conceded removability and sought only voluntary departure at the next hearing (Tr. at 21, June 1, 2004).
 
The respondent further contends that it is not clear from the record whether prior counsel actually admitted to both the drug charge and the fraud charge, as the Immigration Judge did not ask her to plead to each charge or allegation individually. However, the charges and allegations had been discussed at length during numerous previous hearings, and the respondent’s attorney had waived the reading of the allegations (Tr. at 1-2). Thus, counsel’s statement at the final hearing that the respondent was admitting to “the allegations” and conceding removability is sufficient in this case to cover all charges and allegations contained in the NTA (Tr. at 21, June 1, 2004 hearing). See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal concession made by counsel is binding on the alien).
 
*3 The respondent also asserts that the Immigration Judge violated due process by refusing to play the audio tape of the prior hearing. To the contrary, the Immigration Judge offered to do just that, but the respondent’s attorney changed the subject and never returned to the issue (Tr. at 80). Likewise, the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request to order a transcript of the prior proceedings did not violate due process (Tr. at 84), as all parties had listened to the tapes and the Immigration Judge had offered to replay the tape in court. Moreover, the respondent suffered no prejudice, as we have a full copy of the transcript before us on appeal. See, e.g., Garcia-Villeda v. Mukasey, 531 F.3d 141, 149 (2d Cir. 2008) (“Parties claiming denial of due process in immigration cases must, in order to prevail, allege some cognizable prejudice fairly attributable to the challenged process”).
 
Accordingly, the respondent is barred by collateral estoppel from contesting or withdrawing her prior factual admissions. As such, her removability has been established and her appeal is dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent also argued that her former attorney filed the wrong waiver on her behalf and misadvised her to attempt to re-enter the United States while her waiver was pending. However, although the respondent filed a bar complaint against former counsel, she has not submitted any affidavit, nor has she presented any proof that her former attorney has been notified of the allegations, as required by Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for ineffective assistance of counsel claims.

2010 WL 691284 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LANA SHELLY ANN RILEY
File: AXXX XX9 640 - Moorehaven, FL
February 18, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margarita I. Cimadevilla
Assistant Chief Counssel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Barbados, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 16, 2009. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews findings of fact, including credibility determinations, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). If the Board determines that an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact are not clearly erroneous, it may review de novo whether the facts are sufficient to meet an applicable legal standard. See Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493, 497 (BIA 2008). We have conducted de novo review of the issues presented here.
 
As found by the Immigration Judge, the respondent was admitted to the United States as an immigrant December 2, 1984. On April 1, 2009, the respondent was convicted of possession of marijuana with intent to sell or deliver in violation of section 893.13(1)(a)(2) of the Florida Statutes Annotated.¿FN1º
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is subject to removal as charged.¿FN2º The respondent’s crime, possession of marijuana for sale or delivery, is a controlled substance violation and qualifies as an aggravated felony offense because it is comparable to the federal crime of possession of marijuana with intent to distribute. See Lopez v. Gonzales, 549 U.S. 49, 53-57 (2006) (a state drug offense qualifies as an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. 1101(a)(43)(B), if the offense would be punishable as a felony under the CSA, 21 U.S.C. § 801 et seq.); 21 U.S.C. § 841(a)(1) (making distribution of marijuana unlawful); 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(1)(D) (a violation of 21 U.S.C. § 841(a)(1) is a felony punishable by a term of imprisonment of up to 5 years).
 
*2 The respondent’s aggravated felony conviction renders her ineligible for any discretionary relief, and she expressed no fear of returning to Barbados. Therefore, it does not appear that there is any relief from removal available to the respondent. The respondent has waived all applications for relief in any event (Tr. at 14).
 
We note that the respondent appears to make a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel on appeal. However, she has failed to comply with the procedural requirements for such claims set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent has also failed to demonstrate that she has been prejudiced by any action or inaction of former counsel.¿FN3º See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). To the extent that the respondent maintains that she did not commit the criminal offense which serves as the basis for removal, we note that the Board cannot entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction unless that judgment is void on its face, and will not go behind the judicial record to examine an alien’s claims regarding guilt or innocence. See Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

Although the criminal court withheld adjudication of guilt after the respondent entered a plea of “guilty,” the judgment qualifies as a conviction for immigration purposes. See Resendiz-Alcaraz v. United States Att’y Gen., 383 F.3d 1262, 1268 (11th Cir. 2004). The evidence submitted by the respondent on appeal would not change this determination, and therefore provides no basis for reopening proceedings. See Matter of Coehlo, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).

2

The respondent claims on appeal that she is a United States citizen and, by implication, not subject to removal. APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance by his prior counsel. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion and it is therefore deemed unopposed. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3). The motion complies with the procedural steps for asserting a proper ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). Further, the respondent has satisfactorily established that he was precluded from presenting an application for relief under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), because his prior counsel failed to timely file the application. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (requiring a showing of prejudice in order to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel). We therefore find that he has suffered prejudice as a result of ineffective assistance from his counsel. Accordingly, the motion is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
David B. Holmes

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CATALINA SANCHEZ ALMANZAR
File: AXXX XX3 248 - San Juan, PR
February 5, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose G. Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jose A. Rivera-Ortiz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen and native of the Dominican Republic, appeals the May 2, 2008, decision of the Immigration Judge denying her motion to reopen removal proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed an opposition. We will dismiss the appeal.
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008).
 
A hearing was held before the Immigration Judge on March 10, 2008, to consider the respondent’s Petition to Remove Conditions on Residence (Form I-751). According to the parties and the Immigration Judge, this hearing concluded early when the respondent, through her then counsel, requested voluntary departure. By an order dated March 10, 2008, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s Form I-751 but granted her 60 days voluntary departure.
 
In her motion to reopen, the respondent argues that she had ineffective assistance of counsel at the March 2008 hearing. Specifically, she claims that her former counsel did not allow her to fully present her case, and requested voluntary departure before consulting her or obtaining her consent (Motion to Reopen at 1). However, in making this allegation, the respondent has failed to meet any of the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). That is, she has failed, even on appeal, to submit an affidavit detailing the agreement she had with her former counsel, assert that the former counsel has been informed of the allegations leveled against him and been given an opportunity to respond, or confirm that a bar complaint has been filed against the former counsel, or alternatively, explain why such a complaint has not been filed. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639; Punzalan v. Holder, 575 F.3d 107, 109 n.1(1st Cir. 2009).
 
*2 Further, in the absence of egregious circumstances, an alien is bound by the “reasonable tactical actions” of his or her counsel. Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938 (BIA 1995). Based on the very limited detail contained in the respondent’s allegations, we are unable to conclude that her former counsel’s actions were egregious. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (holding that there is a strong presumption that a distinct and formal admission made before, during, or even after a proceeding by an attorney acting in his professional capacity is a reasonable tactical decision).
 
In view of the foregoing, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

2010 WL 673476 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GAFAR WINSTON YOUNG A.K.A. WINSTON BROWN A.K.A. CAFOI YOUNG
File: AXXX XX7 334 - Fishkill, NY
January 28, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
C. Cullen Sheppard
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 In a decision dated September 29, 2009, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable; determined that he did not apply for, and did not demonstrate eligibility for, any relief from removal; and ordered him removed from the United States to Jamaica. The respondent appealed from that decision. The appeal will be dismissed. The request for oral argument is denied.
 
The respondent was found removable on the four charged grounds, as in the United States in violation of law under section 237(a)(1)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B); as convicted of an aggravated felony under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, in conjunction with both sections 101(a)(43)(F) and 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1101(a)(43)(F) and 1101(a)(43)(G); and as convicted of a controlled substance violation under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act. As substantiated by conviction documents (Ex. 3), he has New York convictions upon guilty pleas on November 24, 2008, for assault in the first degree, robbery in the first degree, and criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree, cocaine. He was sentenced to concurrent prison terms of 5 years, 5 years, and 1 year for these crimes, respectively. The record reflects that he arrived in the United States in 2002.
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that he fears returning to Jamaica because he could be killed by the same unidentified individuals who murdered his sister, brother-in-law, and uncle (Br. at 1 et seq.).
 
We observe that the respondent’s assault and robbery convictions are for both aggravated felonies and particularly serious crimes. See sections 208(b)(2)(A)(ii) and 208(b)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1158(b)(2)(A)(ii) and 1158(b)(2)(B)(i); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16(c)(4) and 1208.16(d)(2) and (3) (2009). As an alien who has been convicted of at least one offense that is a particularly serious crime as well as an aggravated felony, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for asylum and withholding under the Act and is eligible only for deferral of removal under the United Nations Convention Against Torture (“CAT”). In addition, we note that the respondent’s convictions for the assault and robbery offenses are for crimes that are per se particularly serious, since he has been sentenced to imprisonment of at least 5 years for each of those offenses. See sections 208(b)(2)(A)(ii) and 208(b)(2)(B)(i) of the Act; see also section 241(b)(3)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(3)(B).
 
*2 With respect to CAT, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent abandoned such application for failure to timely submit it by the deadline of September 25, 2009, following his final removal hearing on August 12, 2009 (I.J. at 1; Tr. at 11). Concerning the respondent’s present claim of torture, we find that he has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for deferral of removal under the CAT at this time, based on his cursory and unsubstantiated allegations regarding his fear of returning to Jamaica.
 
Next on appeal, the respondent asserts that he will face exceptional and extremely unusual hardship upon his removal (Br. at 4-5). To the extent that he seeks to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1), he is statutorily ineligible for that relief because he cannot establish the necessary 10 years of continuous physical presence in the United States dating from his 2002 arrival and because he has been convicted of offenses under section 237(a)(2) of the Act. See section 240A(b)(1)(A) and (C) of the Act. His period of continuous physical presence was deemed to end in October 2006, when he committed the robbery crime. See section 240A(d)(1) of the Act; Ex. 3. We therefore need not and do not reach consideration of whether he can meet the other section 240A(b) requirements, including whether he can demonstrate that his removal would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to his spouse, parent, or child who is a United States citizen or lawful permanent resident, as required under section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act. We observe that extreme hardship to an alien himself is no longer considered under section 240A(b) of the Act, although it was relevant to whether a respondent could establish eligibility for suspension of deportation under former section 244(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1254(a), which provision the respondent has referenced (Br. at 4).
 
To the extent that the respondent wishes to apply for suspension of deportation under former section 244(a) of the Act, we note that he cannot do so in these removal proceedings, following the enactment of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Division C of Pub. L. No. 104-208, 110 Stat. 3009-627 (“IIRIRA”). If IIRIRA had never been enacted and the respondent were in deportation proceedings, he might have submitted an application for suspension of deportation under former section 244(a)(1) of the Act. Former section 244(a)(1) required only 7 years of continuous physical presence, included the lower standard of extreme hardship, and permitted extreme hardship to the alien himself to be considered. However, we reiterate that suspension of deportation is a form of relief that is not available to the respondent in the current removal proceedings. The respondent appropriately was placed in removal proceedings because the proceedings for him were commenced with the service of the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) on February 18, 2009, which date is nearly 12 years subsequent to April 1, 1997, the general effective date of IIRIRA. To the extent that the respondent is challenging the timing of the institution of his removal proceedings, we lack jurisdiction to second-guess the decision of the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) to initiate removal proceedings by serving the NTA on February 18, 2009. The determination to place an alien in proceedings is a matter of prosecutorial discretion entirely within the authority of the DHS. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980); see also Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 48 (BIA 1982).
 
*3 The respondent also has referenced section 240A(b)(2) of the Act (Br. at 3), which pertains to “special rule” cancellation for a battered spouse or child. However, he has not presented any probative evidence to demonstrate that he is prima facie eligible for relief under such provision.
 
We do not find that a remand is warranted for consideration of any claim for CAT deferral, any claim for cancellation of removal, or for any other reason in this case.
 
In a supplementary appellate submission, the respondent contends that his legal representative failed to assist him with filing a CAT application in Immigration Court. To the extent that the respondent asserts that he received ineffective assistance of counsel in his removal proceedings, he has failed to offer any evidence that substantially complies with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and he further has failed to demonstrate that he suffered the requisite prejudice from counsel’s alleged inaction, such that a remand for consideration of his claim would be warranted. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009).
 
To the extent that the respondent alleges that his removal proceedings were unfair, we find that he has not demonstrated any error by the Immigration Judge in handling his hearings. We also find that he has not demonstrated any resultant prejudice such as would affect the outcome of his case and would amount to a due process violation. See Waldron v. INS, 17 F.3d 511 (2d Cir. 1994), cert. denied, 513 U.S. 1014 (1994). To the extent that the respondent alleges that certain provisions of the immigration laws are unconstitutional, we observe that this Board cannot rule on the constitutionality of laws enacted by Congress. See Matter of Fuentes-Campos, 21 I&N Dec. 905 (BIA 1997).
 
Finally, to the extent that the respondent maybe seeking humanitarian relief to enable him to stay in the United States, we recognize that this Board and the Immigration Judges have limited jurisdiction and can grant only those forms of relief from removal that are expressly authorized by Congress. See Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734 (BIA 1988). We have no power to grant equitable remedies or to confer general humanitarian relief on aliens, even though a particular case may pose sympathetic circumstances. If the respondent wishes to obtain relief on humanitarian grounds, he must pursue such relief with the DHS.
 
*4 Accordingly, we will dismiss the appeal.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 673476 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 334710 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EBERE SIBEUDU EZEUGWU A.K.A. EBERE SIBEUDER EZEUGWU
File: AXXX XX4 920 - Newark, NJ
January 15, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Emmanuel Ahia, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Nigeria, appeals from the November 3, 2008, decision of the Immigration Judge denying her motion to rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on October 14, 2008, and reopen proceedings. The respondent has filed an appeal brief; the Department of Homeland Security has not. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded.
 
Subsequent to the entry of the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent retained new counsel. On appeal, the respondent asserts for the first time claims of ineffective assistance of counsel against two of her prior attorneys of record, Jennifer Klapper, Esquire, and Gary T. Jodha, Esquire.¿FN1º
 
Claims of ineffective assistance of counsel are governed by the standards found in our precedent decision Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). The United States Court of Appeal for the Third Circuit (under whose jurisdiction the instant case arises) has cautioned against a strict interpretation of the Lozada requirements, noting that the case “serve¿sº as a threshold and a screening mechanism to help the agency assess the substantial number of ineffective assistance claims it receives.” Rranci v. Att’y Gen., 540 F.3d 165, 173 (3d Cir. 2008) (citing Fadiga v. Att’y Gen., 488 F.3d 142, 155 (3d Cir. 2007).
 
We find that attorney Jodha’s failure to inform the respondent of the date of her hearing (and to make adequate efforts to ascertain such date) constituted ineffective assistance. We additionally find that the respondent has satisfied the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
Although the respondent herself did not notify Mr. Jodha of the allegations (as required by the second prong of Lozada), we find such requirement to have been satisfied. In a correspondence dated October 21, the Immigration Judge requested Mr. Jodha to inform her as to whether he had in fact substituted as the respondent’s counsel by October 14, 2008, and was “already in possession of the client file” by that date. The Immigration Judge continued that if so, “Mr. Jodha evidently owed Respondent the duty to determine the date, place, and time of the next scheduled hearing because because he had obtained her file and Respondent had already advised former counsel to stop working on Respondent’s case.”
 
*2 Attorney Jodha responded in writing on October 31, 2008. As attorney of record, Mr. Jodha also received the Immigration Judge’s November 3, 2008, decision, in which she determined that there was “a lack of evidence that Mr. Jodha…made reasonable effort to determine the status of this case” (I.J. at 4). Upon review of the entire record, we find no clear error in such determination. As the present allegations of ineffective assistance by Mr. Jodha are identical to the issues already raised in the Immigration Judge’s October 14, 2008, correspondence and her November 3, 2008, decision, we find that prior counsel received adequate notice and the opportunity to respond. See Rranci v. Att’y Gen., supra.
 
As we find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that she and her firm “were both diligent to the Respondent” (I.J. at 4), we find no ineffective assistance by Ms. Klapper.
 
Based on the finding of ineffective assistance by Mr. Jodha, we will rescind the in absentia order of removal. See Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The order of removal is rescinded, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Jim Hilz
FOR THE BOARD

1

Although referred to in the motion and complaint as Jennifer Hernandez, and in the Immigration Judge’s decision as “Ms. Hernandez Klapper,” we adopt the name used by former counsel herself in her later correspondences of record, i.e. Jennifer Klapper.
