2010 WL 334711 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YANIRA MARILY LAINEZ
File: AXXX XX5 467 - Baltimore, MD
January 15, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Manuel Rivera, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leslie Smith Turner
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed the July 17, 2008, decision of an Immigration Judge denying her motion to reconsider. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record indicates that the respondent was granted voluntary departure on March 6, 2000, at the conclusion of removal proceedings, with an alternate order of removal to El Salvador. In March 2008, the respondent filed a motion to reopen claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge concluded that the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was without merit, and the motion was denied as untimely filed. The respondent subsequently filed a motion to reconsider that decision. The denial of that motion to reconsider is the subject of this appeal.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec.493 (BIA 2008).
 
Based on our standard of review, we find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the motion to reconsider. The respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is based, not on new evidence or information that was recently discovered, but on facts that were well established at the conclusion of her proceedings on March 6, 2000. The respondent has not shown due diligence in filing the motion. To hold otherwise would vitiate the statutory and regulatory deadlines, which are designed to bring finality to immigration proceedings. INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992) (motions to reopen are especially disfavored in immigration proceedings because every delay works to the advantage of the deportable alien). Furthermore, the respondent did not meet the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). An affidavit was not submitted with the motion to reopen, and was only submitted with the motion to reconsider. Prior counsel was apparently not notified, and no explanation for the failure to file a bar complaint was included. Finally, we agree with the Immigration Judge for the reasons stated in his decision that respondent has not shown how counsel’s apparent lack of preparation caused the respondent to lie at the hearing. Accordingly, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Jim Hilz
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 334711 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2010 WL 304208 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DAVINDER SINGH GILL
File: AXXX XX0 547 - Detroit, MI
January 13, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gurpatwant Singh Pannun, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The final administrative order in this case was issued by the Board on December 15, 2003, when we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s May 3, 2002, decision finding the respondent, a native and citizen of India, removable under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act and denying him relief from removal on discretionary grounds. The United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit denied the respondent’s petition for review in 2005. Respondent’s motion at 5. Nearly six years after the final administrative order, on November 30, 2009, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen renewing his request to pursue adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i), based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent does not dispute the untimeliness of the motion, but argues that the deadline should either be equitably tolled or alternatively that we reopen sua sponte. We will deny the motion.
 
There is no issue regarding the respondent’s inadmissibility as charged pursuant to section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act. Rather, the respondent argues that the two counsel who represented him before the Immigration Judge and on appeal were ineffective in failing to establish his eligibility for section 245(i) relief, which he argues was denied based on the Immigration Judge’s erroneous finding that he is inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i).
 
However, contrary to the respondent’s arguments, the Immigration Judge simply noted the basis for the former Immigration and Naturalization Service’s denial of his application and did not make an independent finding that any of the section 212(a)(9) statutory bars to admission apply. Rather, Immigration Judge found that the equities presented in this case were outweighed not only by the respondent’s repeated immigration violations, but also the disingenuous nature of his representations regarding his residency and immigration history (including his use of a different name which resulted in the assignment of a different alien number after his illegal reentry) and his lack of candor and forthrightness with the court. The Immigration Judge therefore found that the respondent was not deserving of a favorable exercise of discretion and denied, on discretionary grounds, even the minimal relief of voluntary departure. We are not persuaded that the Immigration Judge’s assessment of the respondent’s discretionary factors would change if these proceedings were reopened for further consideration of the respondent’s adjustment of status application. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (a party who files a motion to remand or a motion to reopen bears a “heavy burden” of proving that “if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all of the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case”). We likewise find that the respondent has not demonstrated that he is worthy of a favorable exercise of discretion. Rather than providing new favorable equities, the late motion compounds the respondent’s prior immigration violations by showing a continued disregard for the final removal order. The Sixth Circuit denied the respondent’s petition for review in 2005. See I.N.S. v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988) (holding that the Board “may leap ahead, as it were, over two threshold concerns (prima facie case and new evidence/reasonable explanation), and simply determine that even if they were met, movant would not be entitled to discretionary grant of relief”).
 
*2 Finally, we add that the failure to argue that a section 212(a)(9)(B)(i) bar did not apply, and that the former Immigration and Naturalization Service had thus incorrectly calculated the period of the respondent’s illegal presence by measuring the length of his illegal presence after his last departure, does not constitute “egregious” ineffectiveness given the undeveloped state of the law regarding the section 212(a)(9)(B) bars at the relevant times. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (“Litigants are generally bound by the conduct of their attorneys, absent egregious circumstances.”). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). We point out that our decision in Matter of Rodarte-Roman, 23 I&N Dec. 905 (BIA 2006), which clarified that the requisite period of illegal presence must occur prior to the departure, was not issued until well after our dismissal of the respondent’s appeal in 2003. We therefore find that untimely reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance is not warranted here. We also decline to exercise our discretion to reopen the proceedings sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (the rules governing motions are intended to bring finality to proceedings and the Board’s sua sponte authority is not meant to be used as a cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might cause hardship).
 
Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 304208 (BIA)
End of Document

2009 WL 5548118 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PABLO CANDIDO MONTES
File: AXXX XX1 140 - San Francisco, CA
December 31, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John Nguyen, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a male native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 22, 2008. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on March 26, 2008. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
On appeal, the respondent reiterates that he did not receive proper notice of his hearing because his former counsel did not inform him of the specific date and time of his hearing. Upon de novo review, we find that proper notice was provided to the respondent. The record reflects that the Immigration Judge reopened proceedings on two prior occasions after the respondent failed to appear. Following the Immigration Judge’s decision reopening the respondent’s case a second time, a hearing notice dated July 31, 2007, was mailed to the respondent’s attorney of record, Deok J. Kim, Esquire. See Exh. 15. We find that the respondent received proper notice inasmuch as written notice of his hearing was provided to his attorney of record. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). The respondent concedes in his brief that his attorney appeared at his hearing without the respondent, indicating that the hearing notice was received by Mr. Kim. The fact that his attorney was allowed to withdraw on the day of the respondent’s last hearing is irrelevant to the issue of proper notice. To the extent that the respondent argues exceptional circumstances because his attorney did not tell him the date and time of his hearing, his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not satisfy any of the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
We are also not persuaded by the respondent’s argument on appeal that he is eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b. The respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear which indicates that he received oral notice in his native Spanish of the consequences of failing to appear for his removal hearing, he is therefore statutorily ineligible for such relief See section 240(b)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(7). See also Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998) (explaining that an in absentia deportation order need not always be rescinded before it can be reopened).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 5548118 (BIA)

APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on June 3, 2009, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On September 1, 2009, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent contends that his former counsel, Ariol Eugene, provided ineffective assistance of counsel before the Immigration Judge and has refused to provide him with a copy of his file. The respondent was represented by different counsel before the Board.
 
The respondent requests that the Board grant his motion to reopen, motion for extension of time to comply with his motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, and motion for a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security objects to the granting of the motion.
 
The respondent has not submitted any supplemental evidence since the filing of the original motion. The original motion does not demonstrate that the respondent complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), with respect to Mr. Eugene. Further, although the respondent generally alleges that he was denied his right to counsel and due process based on ineffective assistance, he has not identified any particular instances of ineffective assistance of counsel or demonstrated prejudice. Accordingly, the motions are denied.
 
David B. Holmes

2009 WL 5443629 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EVANGELINA MUNIZ A.K.A. EVANGELINA SANCHEZ VALLADARES
File: AXXX XX1 319 - Seattle, WA
December 30, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Antonio Salazar, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Gregory Fehlings
Deputy Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent appeals the September 16, 2009, Immigration Judge’s decision denying her motion to reopen following the October 16, 2000, issuance of an in absentia order of removal in her case. Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act; Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 722-23 (BIA 1999). The respondent’s motion to reopen included a declaration asserting that the respondent had “received no … documents to warn ¿herº of any immigration violations.” The Immigration Judge denied the motion based upon his findings that the respondent (1) had been personally served with the Notice to Appear (NTA) (Exh. 1), which included a warning regarding any failure to appear and the respondent’s statutory change-of-address obligation, and (2) had made no claims in her declaration as to her correct address at the time of her missed hearing and any efforts she had made to comply with her statutory address obligations.
 
We recently held in Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674 (BIA 2008), that
¿iºn determining whether a respondent has rebutted the weaker presumption of delivery applicable ¿where a notice of hearing is sent to the respondent by regular mail, as opposed to certified mailº, an Immigration Judge may consider a variety of factors including, but not limited to, the following: (1) the respondent’s affidavit; (2) affidavits from family members or other individuals who are knowledgeable about the facts relevant to whether notice was received; (3) the respondent’s actions upon learning of the in absentia order, and whether due diligence was exercised in seeking to redress the situation; (4) any prior affirmative application for relief, indicating that the respondent had an incentive to appear; (5) any prior application for relief filed with the Immigration Court or any prima facie evidence in the record or the respondent’s motion of statutory eligibility for relief, indicating that the respondent had an incentive to appear; (6) the respondent’s previous attendance at Immigration Court hearings, if applicable; and (7) any other circumstances or evidence indicating possible nonreceipt of notice. We emphasize that these are just examples of the types of evidence that can support a motion to reopen. Immigration Judges are neither required to deny reopening if exactly such evidence is not provided nor obliged to grant a motion, even if every type of evidence is submitted. Each case must be evaluated based on its own particular circumstances and evidence.
 
 
*2 The Immigration Judge’s findings that the respondent was personally served with the NTA,¿FN1º had been sent her notice of hearing (Exh. 2) at the same address listed on the NTA, and made no representations as to her correct address at the time of the hearing notice’s issuance are not clearly erroneous. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). The record contains no evidence that the respondent had attended an earlier immigration hearing, had an available form of relief from removal,¿FN2º or had informed the immigration court of any change of address. Applying a de novo standard of review, we conclude that, given these facts, the respondent’s declaration’s inaccurate and unspecific claim that she received no documents with regard to her removal proceedings is insufficient to rebut the presumption of delivery of the mailed notice of her October 16, 2000, hearing.
 
On appeal the respondent raises a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel by asserting that prior counsel sub-contracted with another attorney to file her motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge; that the filing attorney failed to point out in the motion that an immigrant visa petition filed on behalf of the respondent was pending; that the filing attorney “failed to argue to the Court that the disgruntled ex-husband controlled the dwelling where the notice was sent and could have received the notice by way of the woman he was then living with thus depriving the Respondent of notice;” that the filing attorney failed to submit applications for relief with the motion to reopen; that the filing attorney failed to investigate the fact that the respondent had applied for United States citizenship; that the filing attorney failed to provide the Immigration Judge with “a proper reason” for the respondent’s prior failure to appear, specifically that the respondent had lost her immigration documents when she was forced to leave her husband’s home; and that the filing attorney failed to file an appeal or to alert the respondent of the need to file an appeal.
 
Our review of allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel is governed by our analysis in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In Matter of Lozada, we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the applicant setting forth in detail the agreement between the applicant and her legal representative and what actions were to be taken on her behalf, (2) that the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) an indication of whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. The respondent has not complied with the first two of these requirements.
 
*3 Even if the respondent had met all of these requirements, however, we would nevertheless find that her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel fails. First, in the absence of an affidavit by the respondent as to her agreement with her first attorney, she cannot establish that she had an agreement with him, much less that he failed to comply with the agreement by hiring another attorney to prepare the motion. Second, the respondent has not shown that an immigrant visa petition was pending on her behalf at the time of her missed 2000 hearing or that she was eligible to apply for naturalization, and therefore filing counsel cannot be found incompetent for not raising such claims. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793 (9th Cir. 2005). Third, even if the respondent had shown that she was eligible to apply for some form of relief at the time that the motion to reopen was filed, the failure to submit applications for relief would result in no prejudice to the respondent, see Mohammed v. Gonzales, supra, at 793-94, unless the respondent could first demonstrate that she had not received notice of her missed hearing, as would be required to rescind the in absentia order of removal under section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act. Fourth, the respondent’s claim that she failed to appear at her 2000 hearing because she did not receive notice due to having left her home, where her husband remained with a girlfriend, does not explain why she failed to file a change of address form, as instructed in her NTA. Finally, an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen was in fact filed, and therefore the respondent suffered no prejudice by prior counsel’s alleged failure to file or to alert her of the need to file. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, supra.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JIA QIANG LU
File: AXXX XX4 425 - Memphis, TN
December 29, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stuart Altman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William A. Lund
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the People’s Republic of China, appeals from the January 5, 2009, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to rescind the in absentia order entered on May 21, 2008, and reopen proceedings. Both parties have filed appeal briefs. The record will be remanded.
 
We review factual findings of the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We may review questions of law, discretion, judgment, and all other non-fact issues in appeals from decisions of immigration judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493, 497 (BIA 2008).
 
On November 21, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge that was prepared by his prior counsel, Liu Yu. The motion alleged ineffective assistance of counsel against Mr. Yu’s own office, but argued that it was not necessary for him to comply with all of the requirements for establishing such grounds under our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).¿FN1º The Immigration Judge rejected such argument, and denied the motion for failure to fully comply with the requirements of Lozada (I.J. at 4-5).
 
The respondent retained new counsel on appeal, who filed a second motion to reopen with this Board on March 3, 2009, and an appeal brief on March 25, 2009.¿FN2º The second motion was accompanied by additional evidence intended to fully comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. The Department of Homeland Security responded with a brief arguing that such motion is numerically barred under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3).
 
Pursuant to our de novo review, we affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. However, we will deem the second motion to reopen (which was filed during the pendency of the respondent’s appeal) as a motion to remand. In light of the unusual circumstances presented here, we deem it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge to consider the new evidence in the first instance and determine whether rescission is warranted.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for issuance of a new order not inconsistent with the foregoing decision.
 
*2 Jim Hilz
FOR THE BOARD

In arguing that it was not necessary to comply with the requirement under Matter of Lozada, supra, of filing a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority, prior counsel claimed that the purpose of such requirement “is to prevent aliens or their counsel of ineffective assistance counsel from the collusive of the reopen proceedings. There is no such collusive ¿sicº.” Motion to Reopen at 2.

2

We note that the Compean decision was vacated in a decision of the Attorney General dated June 3, 2009. Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).

2009 WL 5443568 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MUHAMMAD SARFRAZ
File: AXXX XX6 482 - Houston, TX
December 29, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Gerrie Zhang
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Pakistan, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s March 26, 2008, decision finding she did not have jurisdiction to review his motion to reopen. During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent filed a motion to reopen directly with the Board. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and his motion to reopen will be denied.
 
As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent’s case was last before the Board on September 21, 2005. Therefore, the respondent was required to file his motion to reopen with the Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Consequently, the Immigration Judge properly found that she did not have jurisdiction over the merits of the motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is both untimely and numerically barred, as outlined in the regulations. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). To be timely, the respondent’s motion would have been due on or before September 21, 2005. See id (imposing, in general, a 90-day deadline for motions to reopen). However, this motion to reopen was filed on February 19, 2008. Additionally, this is the respondent’s fifth request for reopening, and we have previously denied motions to reopen in the instant case filed by him through orders issued by the Board on October 11, 2005, April 23, 2007, July 24, 2007, and September 19, 2007. As a result, the respondent has exceeded the single motion to reopen limit imposed by regulation. See id.
 
In his motion, the respondent maintains that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from the attorney who initially represented him before the Immigration Court and the Board and an attorney who subsequently represented him before the Board in filing a motion to reopen based on the alleged ineffective assistance of his first attorney. In particular, he maintains that the first attorney erred in seeking a continuance on his behalf rather than asserting his eligibility for adjustment of status and in failing to completely advise him of his rights and responsibilities pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s grant of voluntary departure. The respondent argues that the second attorney failed to timely file a motion to reopen based on his claim of ineffective assistance.
 
*2 Although the respondent has met the procedural requirement for ineffective assistance outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we find that he has not established that he was prejudiced by his prior counsels’ actions. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640; see also Ogbemudia v. INS, 988 F.2d 595, 598 (5th Cir. 1993) (requiring the respondent to demonstrate “substantial prejudice” in order to establish a due process violation). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, supra. This requires a showing that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed had the negligent representation not occurred. Id.
 
As we noted in our July 24, 2007, decision, the respondent failed to demonstrate that he exercised due diligence in pursuing his ineffective assistance claim against his first attorney. Moreover, although he cites this attorney’s decision to seek voluntary departure on his behalf as legal error, the respondent’s dissatisfaction with his former counsel’s strategy in pursuing relief is not a ground for reopening based on ineffective assistance. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 310 (BIA 1998). Finally, the respondent fails to show prejudice as a result of his first attorney’s allegedly ineffective representation, as he was not eligible for relief when the administratively final order was entered.
 
The respondent alleges that his second attorney failed to act with due haste or diligence in pursuing his ineffective assistance claim against his prior counsel, but he has failed to demonstrate any resulting prejudice. He asserts that he is eligible to adjust status as an alien grandfathered under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. Specifically, he asserts that this eligibility arises from a labor certification filed on behalf of Nighat Sultana on April 30, 2001, and that Ms. Sultana is his mother. However, the respondent has not provided documentary evidence of his qualifying relationship with Ms. Sultana. As a result, the respondent has failed to provide prima facie evidence that he is a grandfathered alien, and thus, he has failed to demonstrate prejudice as a result of his second attorney’s alleged ineffective performance.
 
Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 5443568 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2009 WL 5443985 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FAROOK MOHAMMED KHAN A.K.A. ZEHEER ABBAS
File: AXXX XX1 854 - York, PA
December 23, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jon D. Staples
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)º-Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(2)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(B)º- Multiple criminal convictions
 
Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º- Fraud or willful misrepresentation of material fact
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º-Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
Sec. 212(a)(9)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(A)(i)º- Previously ordered removed at arrival
 
  
APPLICATION: Relief
 
The respondent has filed a timely appeal from an Immigration Judge’s September 1, 2009, decision, finding him removable, as charged, based on his admissions (Tr. at 3), and record of convictions, and statutorily ineligible for relief from removal. The appeal will be dismissed. The respondent’s request to proceed on appeal in forma pauperis is granted under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). See Matter of Chicas, 19 I&N Dec. 114 (BIA 1984).
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2009); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (2009); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
Upon our de novo review of the issues presented here, we have concluded that the respondent presents no arguments on appeal that would cause us to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision.¿FN1º Based on the record before us, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is subject to removal from the United States based on the respondent’s admissions in this case (Tr. at 3). See section 240(c)(2)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(2)(A). Moreover, the respondent has failed to establish his eligibility for any relief from removal, including a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h). See also section 240(c)(4)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Furthermore, to the extent the respondent seeks to challenge his previous order of removal dated January 20, 2005, which was executed on March 14, 2005, now alleging ineffective assistance of former counsel, neither the Immigration Judge nor the Board has jurisdiction to consider the issue. The respondent has not shown that he ever raised the issue of ineffective assistance of former counsel in the former proceedings prior to his removal, or complied with the Board’s decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 851 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (setting out the requirements that would support a motion to reopen based on a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel). The respondent has not demonstrated that his removal from the United States in 2005, was not made pursuant to a lawful order of deportation. With limited exceptions not applicable here, the departure of an alien from the United States pursuant to a lawful order of deportation has the effect of depriving either the Immigration Judge or this Board of jurisdiction over the prior proceedings. See Matter of Armendarez-Mendez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008) (holding that the Board lacks authority to reopen removal, deportation, or exclusion proceedings if the alien has departed the United States after those administrative proceedings have been completed); Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281, 288 (BIA 1998); Matter of Okoh, 20 I&N Dec. 864, 865 (BIA 1994); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(d) and 1003.23(b)(1).


2009 WL 5443985 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JASPAL MULTANI
File: AXXX XX2 639 - Omaha, NE
December 22, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Saadia Siddique, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Debra Robinson
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before us on June 5, 2009, when we found that the respondent had filed, and then withdrawn, a motion to reopen his proceedings. Previously, in a decision dated June 11, 2008, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s October 29, 2007, denial of his motion to reopen his in absentia proceedings. The respondent has now filed an untimely motion to reopen on May 20, 2009. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that we should reopen proceedings based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel that he received from the attorneys who represented him before the Immigration Court and this Board. Specifically, the respondent argues, first, that his prior attorney, Mr. Yang, Esquire, failed to inform the respondent that he was required to appear in court, and that this ineffective advice resulted in his in absentia order, his inability to request relief in the form of adjustment of status, and the loss of his opportunity to request voluntary departure. The respondent also argues that his second attorney, Mr. Cianciolo prejudiced Mr. Multani by inadequately representing him in his motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, and by failing, in the motion to reopen, to include an argument regarding Mr. Yang’s ineffective assistance of counsel.¿FN1º The respondent further argues that Mr. Cianciolo compounded his ineffective assistance by filing an appeal from the denial of this motion, which was dismissed by the Board on June 11, 2008.
 
In support of his motion, the respondent has filed an affidavit outlining his allegations, as well as letters to his previous attorneys, letters to the Iowa Supreme Court making known his grievances, and a response from Mr. Yang, Esquire. While the respondent has substantially complied with the guidelines set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we agree with the DHS’ argument that the respondent has not indicated that he filed this motion with due diligence. See Habchy v. Gonzales, 471 F.3d 858 (8th Cir. 2006). The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal in June of 2008, and the respondent attests, in his affidavit, that “he did not trust (Ms. Cianciolo) at that point” so he did not proceed with any further legal contract with her. Therefore, the respondent has lost confidence in Ms. Cianciolo at some point prior to mid-2008, and yet the respondent did not pursue an ineffective assistance of counsel claim until a year later, in May of 2009. We also note that the Immigration Judge recognized, in his denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in 2007, that “it (was) somewhat unusual that the respondent did not apparently contact his attorney (Mr. Yang) following the November 8, 2005 hearing to determine the date of his next hearing rather than waiting nearly two years to file a motion to reopen alleging ‘exceptional circumstances beyond his control’ (for purposes of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii)). The respondent does not explain the reason for either of these delays, except to say that he assumed that because his “labor certification was pending, (the hearing) was still ongoing.”
 
*2 Accordingly, we do not find that the respondent has filed this motion with due diligence, and we, thus, find no merit to the respondent’s motion, and no reason to except the motion from the timeliness requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

Neither of these attorneys filed the previous motion to reopen before the Board, which was later withdrawn prior to adjudication.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ISRAEL MEDINA-GAONA A.K.A. ISRAEL MEDINA
File: AXXX XX9 568 - Los Angeles, California
December 14, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Abisag Ayala, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rubab Z. Razvi
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s April 29, 2009, decision to deny his untimely motion to reopen. On January 7, 2009, the final administrative order in the respondent’s removal proceeding was entered by the Immigration Judge. On April 16, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. On April 29, 2009, the Immigration Judge denied his motion because the respondent failed to timely file and failed to adequately support his application for cancellation of removal with proper documentation.
 
“¿Aº party may file only one motion to reopen deportation or exclusion proceedings (whether before the Board of the Immigration Judge) and that motion must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent did not file his motion to reopen within 90 days of the final administrative decision, nor did he file it on or before September 30, 1996. As such, we agree with the Immigration Judge that his motion to reopen the final administrative order is time-barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that the doctrine of equitable tolling applies to the time limitations for motions to reopen during periods when a respondent is prevented from filing his motion because of deception, fraud, or error, provided that the respondent acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002). The respondent has argued that he received ineffective assistance of counsel and has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
We note that the respondent failed to meet any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, and did not show that he had been prejudiced in his prior proceedings. The steps set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, include, inter alia, that the motion reflect 1) the agreement with counsel; (2) that prior counsel has been notified and afforded an opportunity to respond; and 3) whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. Despite the respondent statement that he has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, supra, the record does not support his position. The respondent has not explained the agreement he had with counsel, given counsel notice of the ineffective assistance and an opportunity to respond, nor has the respondent filed any complaint with the appropriate authorities or explained why he failed to do so. See Respondent’s Attachments to Brief on Appeal. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001).
 
*2 Moreover, an alien must establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought before a motion to reopen will be granted. See, e.g., INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent did not submit the required documentation to support his prior motion to reopen. Furthermore, the respondent has not filed such documentation on appeal. In the absence of such, the Board cannot consider the respondent’s request for reopening to apply for cancellation of removal as he had not demonstrated statutory eligibility for this relief. Matter of K-L-, 20 I&N Dec. 654, 659 (BIA 1993). Therefore, the respondent has also not demonstrated prejudice from the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004) (must show the outcome might have been affected had the negligent representation not occurred).
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 5252764 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN CARLOS BARREDA
File: AXXX XX2 011 - Guaynabo, PR
December 14, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lymari Casta, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jorge A. Ramos
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 26, 2008. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. On appeal, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, the respondent maintains that his counsel informed him that he did not need to appear for his hearing, inasmuch as his hearing had been rescheduled to a different date and time. The respondent’s appeal will be sustained.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
We find that the respondent has demonstrated that exceptional circumstances prevented the respondent from attending his hearing. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). As the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) recognizes on appeal, the respondent has complied with the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, and his motion to reopen was filed within 180 days of the entry of the in absentia order of removal. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C).
 
Furthermore, we find that the respondent’s counsel committed ineffective assistance, which prevented the respondent from attending his scheduled hearing before the Immigration Judge on May 13, 2008, at 10:30 a.m. The record reflects that the respondent’s counsel made numerous requests for continuances before the Immigration Judge based upon conflicts of schedule and due to the respondent’s pending visa petition (Form 1-130). On the day of the respondent’s scheduled May 13, 2008, hearing, the respondent’s counsel faxed a request for a further continuance to the Immigration Court approximately 2 hours prior to the respondent’s 10:30 a.m. hearing. According to the respondent’s affidavit, he was already traveling to the hearing when he received a telephone call from his counsel’s office informing him that he did not have to appear in Immigration Court that morning, because his hearing had been rescheduled. See R’s brief at Exh. C. However, the Immigration Judge did not grant respondent’s counsel’s motion for a further continuance and ordered the respondent removed in absentia when he failed to appear for his hearing.
 
*2 Inasmuch as the record reflects that the respondent was misinformed by his counsel that he did not need to appear for his May 13, 2008, hearing, we will grant the respondent’s motion to reopen and remand the record to the Immigration Judge to provide the respondent with a hearing regarding the respondent’s removability from the United States and eligibility for relief from removal. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 5252763 (BIA)
End of Document

ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
*1 A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was mailed on July 22, 2009. The appeal was accordingly due on or before August 21, 2009. The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on November 19, 2009. We accordingly find that the appeal is untimely. In conjunction with the appeal the respondent has filed a Motion to File Late Appeal. Respondent states that his former counsel neglected to file his appeal on his behalf. However, he provides no evidence in support of this claim and does not comply with the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, the motion to accept the late filed appeal is denied. The Immigration Judge’s decision is therefore now final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15. The record before us indicates that the respondent may have waived appeal. In view of our disposition of this case, we need not determine whether the respondent made a knowing and intelligent waiver in this case.
 
We note that because we are dismissing the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See sections 240(c)(6)(A) & (B) and 240(c)(7)(A) & C of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6)(A) & (B) and (c)(7)(A) & C; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); see Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).

ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.

IN RE: ALFREDO MALDONADO-BERNAL

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Greg Radics
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude (sustained)
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)º - Controlled substance violation (sustained)
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document (not sustained)
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(c) waiver
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 25, 2008, decision. In that decision, the Immigration Judge deemed the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (1996), abandoned. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
At the October 12, 2005, master calendar hearing, the Immigration Judge reset the hearing for October 30, 2006, so that the respondent could submit his application for relief (Tr. at 4-8). On October 18, 2006, the respondent’s counsel filed a Motion to Withdraw Representation as Attorney, which was granted by the Immigration Judge. For reasons not set forth in the record, the hearing was again reset for July 25, 2008. At the July 25, 2008, hearing, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for a continuance and deemed his application for relief abandoned because it had not been filed. The respondent now appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 25, 2008, decision. On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for a continuance. Specifically, the respondent argues that his prior counsel failed to inform him of his obligation to file his application for relief.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is considered to have abandoned his claim because he failed to timely file his application for relief from removal (I.J. at 3-4). An Immigration Judge has broad discretion to conduct removal proceedings and to set time limits and extensions for filing applications. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). In this case, the respondent’s counsel was advised by the Immigration Judge that the application for relief was required to be filed at the next hearing (Tr. at 6). Moreover, as shown above, proceedings in this matter had been delayed to allow the respondent ample time to prepare for his merits hearing. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 350 (BIA 1982) (holding that an Immigration Judge has a duty to determine removability in an expeditious manner and to avoid dilatory tactics designed to delay removal proceedings). To the extent that the respondent alleges that his former counsel engaged in ineffective assistance, we note that the respondent has not attempted to comply with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly, J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See Voluntary Departure: Effect of a Motion To Reopen or Reconsider or a Petition for Review, 73 Fed. Reg. 76,927, 937-38 (Dec. 18, 2008) (to be codified at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c)(3)(iii), (e)(1)).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 73 Fed. Reg. At 76938 (to be codified at 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i)).

2009 WL 5252687 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JESUS AGUIRRE TORRES
File: AXXX XX9 634 - Atlanta, GA
December 8, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria M. Odom, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy Lampley Fortson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(C), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(C)º - Convicted of firearms or destructive device violation
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a 37-year-old native and citizen of Mexico, appealed the Immigration Judge’s September 19, 2007, decision denying his motion to reopen to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). The respondent’s attorney’s motion to withdraw was granted on February 26, 2007. On March 20, 2007, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable, as charged, and that he had abandoned any applications for relief. Through a new attorney, he filed a motion to reopen on June 13, 2007, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. The appeal was remanded by the Board on May 22, 2008, to the Immigration Court so that the record file could be located. The record was located and returned to the Board on August 19, 2009. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent was admitted to the United States on or about December 1, 1990, as a lawful permanent resident. He married a citizen of Mexico on February 24, 2002, in Mexico. They have five United States citizen children ages 19, 16, 11, 10 and 6 years old. He was convicted in Cobb County, Georgia, on or about June 3, 1997, on a plea of guilty of carrying a concealed weapon. He was fined and given a 12 month suspended sentence. The Notice to Appear was issued on April 11, 2005, at the border after he had made a trip to Mexico. His request for a change of venue from San Antonio, Texas, to Atlanta, Georgia, was granted on March 8, 2006. The respondent contended in his motion to reopen that his original counsel was ineffective by not pursuing his application under 240A(a) of the Act, and that the record should be reopened and remanded. The Immigration Judge found no ineffective assistance of counsel had been proven, and that the motion should be denied.
 
The respondent has not complied substantially with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). He submitted a copy of the agreement for representation, which was limited to the initial appearance, and he has informed prior counsel that he intended to make a claim of ineffective assistance to the Board. Former counsel has responded. However, the respondent did not file any complaint with the relevant authorities. He explains in his brief that the situation, in his new counsel’s opinion, does not warrant a bar complaint. If the respondent does not believe a bar complaint is warranted, he also cannot claim that the actions of his former counsel rise to the level of ineffective assistance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Compean, Bangaly and J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
*2 Moreover, ineffective assistance of counsel in an immigration proceeding is a denial of due process only “¿iºf the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from presenting his case.” Ramirez-Durazo v. INS, 794 F.2d 491, 499-500 (9th Cir. 1986) (quoting Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015, 1017 (9th Cir. 1985). See also Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814 (9th Cir. 2003). The respondent must show that the conduct of former counsel was so egregious that it rendered his hearing unfair. Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998). The respondent stated in his affidavit that he was unable to pay former counsel the amount counsel wanted to continue his representation and file the application. He implies that the amount demanded was excessive, but he does not believe it merits the attention of the state bar. He also states that counsel apparently did not inform the Immigration Court of his new address, because the judge’s order was sent to his old address in North Carolina. Counsel’s motion to withdraw informs the court of the respondent’s new address in Georgia. He does not state that the use of his former address by the Immigration Court caused him any prejudice. We note that the respondent admits his former attorney advised him to attend his hearing scheduled for March 23, 2007, and to request additional time to find a new attorney. He did not appear at that time, even though he could not yet have received the Immigration Judge’s order of removal. We find that there has been no showing that the respondent was prejudiced by his prior attorney’s performance. See Dakane v. United States Attorney General, 399 F.3d 1269 (11th Cir. 2005). In these circumstances, we find the respondent’s due process rights have not been violated.
 
The respondent complains that the Immigration Judge did not consider him for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b), but he has not made a prima facia case of eligibility. Primarily, he has not filed an affidavit indicating that he is able and willing to post bond and to leave the United States, if so ordered. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(3); 1240.26(c)(iv).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 5252687 (BIA)

2009 WL 5252688 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LORENZO ENRIQUE GARCIA A.K.A. LORENZO GARCIA
File: AXXX XX0 570 - Eloy, AZ
December 8, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dominique J. Honea
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)º - Convicted of a controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATIONS: Removability; reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 11, 2009. Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is inadmissible under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i), as an alien present in the United States without being admitted or paroled. Based upon a Verification of Removal (Exh. 7), the Immigration Judge made a factual finding that the respondent departed the United States on May 22, 2004, after being ordered removed (I.J. at 4), and this factual finding is not clearly erroneous. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). Applying a de novo standard of review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has failed to meet his burden of proving his lawful re-entry to the United States. See Lopez-Rodriguez v. Mukasey, 536 F.3d 1012, 1015 (9th Cir. 2008) (advising that once DHS establishes identity and alienage, the burden shifts to the respondent to prove time, place, and manner of entry).
 
Based upon evidence of the respondent’s 2003 conviction for possession or use of drug paraphernalia (Exh. 4-B), we further affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is inadmissible under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation. See Matter of Martinez-Espinoza, 25 I&N Dec. 118 (BIA 2009).
 
The respondent focuses his arguments on appeal on his request for reopening based upon a claim that prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance by filing a deficient motion to reopen his prior removal proceeding following the March 9, 2002, issuance of an in absentia order of removal. The Immigration Judge denied that motion on April 2, 2002, and the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of that decision on July 2, 2003.
 
*2 The respondent’s request for reopening fails. First, the respondent’s 2004 removal from the United States pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s 2002 in absentia order of removal acted to strip us and the Immigration Judge of authority to reopen prior removal proceedings, whether on the respondent’s motion or sua sponte. See Matter of Armendarez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008) (declining to follow Lin v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 979 (9th Cir. 2007)). Second, the respondent has not attempted to comply with the procedure set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for establishing claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 5252688 (BIA)
End of Document
